Allah yihfazak;” and may all your ways be peace!Author of “The Odyssey of Chander Roy” ONROY should have been con- tent to derive his information from books and explain his mission in life to well-chosen friends in his club’s smoking-room. Then he would have acquired a reputation, and his monographs on the advancement of civil- ization among native races would have been accepted without reservation. As matters stand now, however, Conroy snarls when the word “native” is mentioned in his presence afld in his more impassioned moments he recommends the use of poison or other foul means to rid the earth of his erstwhile “dark skinned brethren.” It all happened because some ten years ago when he was inspecting cattle stations on the fringe of the great Australian desert he was invited to witness the Aruntas’ totem dance. Thereafter he ceased to be merely one of “new South Wales’ farseeing finan- ciers,” as the Sydney papers called him, and acquired a mission in life. On the strength of his one close contact with aborigines he decided that they were unfortunate people, downtrodden by the brutal white races, and he brought a good deal of influence to bear to have their conditions improved. Such was his enthusiasm that he read Spencer and Gillen’s “Australian Abor- igines” and looked at the wood cuts in Codrington’s “Melanesians.” People took him seriously because he had much money and controlled far more live-stock than any “The Naked Truth" copyright, 1922, by J. D. Newsom. 64 man should really be allowed to handle. He even wrote a couple of articles for a daily paper urging the abolishment of marriage by capture among the Cape York tribes and the establishment of compulsory education. Because he nearly succeeded his mission in life became an obsession. He talked folk-lore at directors’ meetings until somebody suggested that he visit some of the Pacific Islands and make a com- parative study of existing conditions. As he had just cleaned up half a million pounds by selling out some Western sheep-ranches just before the big drought, he decided to act on the suggestion. “I’ll make things hot for the colonists out there if I find any slavery or oppression,” he told one of his acquaintances just before sailing. “I’ll raise the roof. People know me. I’ll expose ’em!” “Going to New Caledonia?” asked the acquaintance. “Yes, worse luck. Got to go to Noumea and transfer there to a schooner for Tanna in the New Hebrides.” “Why not stop off for a few weeks and look over the island?” queried the acquaint- ance. “We have a cattle-station out there at a place called Nakitalo. McLaird, our manager, tells us the interior is full of tribes who have never seen a white man. He could put you up. I’ll give you a letter of introduction.” ‘Splendid!” boomed Conroy.The Naked Truth 65 He was a large man with a heavy red face barred by a fierce, upcurling moustache, and he affected a broad smile which never quite reached his round, prominent eyes. “Splendid!” he repeated. “And if you think the place looks promis- ing, and I assure you it is,” added his friend, “we could talk things over when you come back.” “I’ll think about it. Thanks for the introduction,” answered Conroy who never allowed his mission to run away with his business sense. So he went to New Caledonia and jour- neyed up to Nakitalo in a disgustingly dirty little boat which lurched and staggered in the slightest swell. For three days he was too miserable to admire the scenery, and the smell of food appalled him. The ever- changing panorama of jagged yellow hills, sharply outlined against the blue sky and the narrow green valleys where the graceful coconut trees stood guard at the water’s edge, failed to excite his interest. Once ashore at Nakitalo, however, with a fellow-Australian to talk to he became as self-assertive as ever. McLaird impressed him not at all, for he was a little, bald man, with very blue eyes set in a clean-shaven, weather-beaten face; and his voice was low and gentle. In fact, Conroy tended to assume command of the homestead and hinted at a change of ownership. “Well, that’ll be nice for you,” murmured McLaird when he heard the news for the first time. “Nice—it’s a drop in the bucket, my good fellow,” retorted Conroy, “a mere drop. You’ve probably worked on some of my ranches around Kalgoorie.” He named an imposing list of stations extending over an area of some thousands of miles. “Your memory is still good,” sighed his host without so much as a ghost of a smile in his blue eyes. Those eyes at tirftes really annoyed Con- roy for they were unwavering and not in the least abashed. ' Otherwise everything went admirably well. McLaird provided him with a half- caste boy from Mare to act as interpreter and turned him loose among the coastal tribes who knew the value of money and were quick to sense what the fat, red man wanted to know. They told him beautiful tales of oppression and cringed when they mentioned the gendarme who administered the district or McLaird, who gave them em- ployment. They were sophisticated people with a craving for contraband rum and they retained few of their original customs, living in miserable hovels patterned after the gendarme’s fort-like house at the end of the valley. But Conroy thought that it was all magnificently primitive. He filled several large note-books with comments on native life and customs and devoted a lot of space to the tribes’ economic life. As he paid the villagers five francs an hour to talk to him of their grievances they never failed to cluster about him when he walked down the village street and took his seat in the shade of a great banyan tree. News spreads fast in the islands, and be- fore long, in twos and threes, men began to drift down from the more remote villages in the foot-hills to acquire some of the fabulous wealth which was being poured out by this red-faced man. The newcomers attracted Conroy’s attention for they were not dressed in the conventional blue loin- cloth and open mesh singlet of the coastal natives, but wore strips of bark cloth wound about their waists and carried queer weapons. The local people seemed to resent these intrusions, but always gave way in fright- ened dismay when the hill-men appeared. At last they went to the hated gendarme and begged for protection. ‘Master,” they wailed, “the men of Vohlu are out again, and we walk in terror66 Adventure lest evil befall us. Our war-clubs are goneS and you are our father and mother.” And the gendarme, who knew the treacher- ous ways of the Vohlus, rudely broke up one of Conroy’s friendly meetings and marched the visitors out of the village with orders to leave their weapons at home next when they visited Nakitalo. Then the gendarme sought cut McLaird, who was busy breaking in a new saddle, horse for the next season’s round-up, and explained in explosive French that Conroy must not, under any circumstances, stir up trouble between the villages. It appeared that he had given the people of Nakitalo less money than he had offered to the hill- folk and things were not as quiet as they might well be. There was more drunken- ness than was absolutely necessary, and the Vohlu Avere restless. The gendarme wanted peace and quiet in his district, so did McLaird for the sake of his bullocks, but neither of them had taken Conroy’s mission into consideration. He was beside himself with indignation and bel- lowed at the gendarme until the latter bellowed back and the air rang with the oaths of Marseilles and the curses of unre- generate Kalgoorie. McLaird sat on the paddock fence and smoked cigarets until the noise gradually subsided. ‘Say,” he called to Conroy in the same dispassionate voice he used for all occasions. “Say, Mr. Conroy, you don’t own this place yet. I got to keep on friendly terms with the gendarme, so just cool off a little. He’s quite right, too. I can’t get any labor at all since you started throwing money at the natives. Talk to ’em all you like, but don’t be so darned generous. I need labor.” “All right,” he added in halting French for the benefit of the enraged gendarme, “I will guard him tranquil. Comprenez? No more de le noise. Tres bien, mon ami, oui?” The officer grinned good-naturedly as he turned to go and waved his hands to express hopeless disgust. But Conroy felt that his great mission was being trampled upon. He was being forbidden free access to the natives by a glorified policeman and a herder of cattle who called Sydney the “Big Smoke” and couldn’t lay claim to a bank-account. These ignorant men were afraid of him, his power, his reputation. “No,” he shouted at McLaird, “I will not be quiet. I am going to finish my investi- gation and I refuse, ab-so-lu-te-Iy refuse to be deterred or coerced by you.” McLaird lighted another cigaret and smiled. His sunburned face creased and wrinkled into a thousand little lines, his small teeth gleamed and his blue eyes danced with merriment. No man, ever before, had dared to grin at Conroy in a temper. “Laugh at me!” he spluttered. “Laugh at me—how dare you? I’ll tell you what I am going to do!” He was still explaining as the shadows grew long and the sun slipped down the side of a purple hill into the sea. He was appalled at McLaird’s callous- ness, he declared. He had seen enough to convince him that everything had been done or left undone to wreck the natives’ standards and reduce them to slavery. On the morrow, he would go up to the Vohlu territory and see for himself exactly how these hill-people fared. No doubt but con- ditions were quite as bad up there. He went to great lengths, repeating him- self several times, to assure McLaird that he might as well begin looking around for a new job. “As a drover perhaps you’ll be a success,” he snarled, “but you’re incapable, absolute- ly incapable of holding such a responsible position as this where the future of a race lies in your hands.” McLaird finally broke his long silence. “Come in to dinner. Food must be getting cold,” he suggested as he jumped off the fence and walked away with an indiffer- ence which quite upset Conroy’s calcula- tions. He followed dumbly, choked by the thousand things he still had to say. After coffee had been served he launched another -attack. He leaned back in his chair and eyed his host malevolently through half-closed lids. “Your silence leads me to believe—” he began. “That’s enough,” snapped McLaird. A change had come over him, his blue eyes were cold and merciless and his mouth was set in a thin, hard line. “ I’ve put up with you for a week. As I said once before you don’t own this place yet. Remember that. “I’ve nursed you because you’re out of your depth as soon as you step off an as- phalt pavement. I won’t bother to tell youThe Naked Truth 67 how wrong you are because it’s waste of time. But get this straight—you are not going up-country tomorrow. You’d have to have a permit from the gendarme. He won’t give one to a maniac like you. You’re dangerous. You’d either get killed or start a rebellion. That’s not needed. And I won’t let you have that interpreter or lend you horses. The sooner you pack up and get out the better. Hospitality is wasted on you. Go back to Sydney and get control of this place -before you come back. There’s a launch going down tomorrow to Mbopari. You’ll be able to shift for yourself there.” Conroy was amazed. This little man trying to bully him! He smiled tolerantly and blew a ring of cigar smoke toward the ceiling. No interpreter, no horses—that could easily be fixed. The gendarme’s permit? What did he care for that? Had he not talked to some official at Noumea who had assured him that he could go any- where he liked about the island? Without a word he left the table and went out to the stockmen’s quarters where he found the Mare boy squatting at ease by the door. They whispered together for some min- utes and a wad of notes changed hands, silencing the boy’s objections. Then he went back to his room and slept soundly. He dreamed a little of his coming journey through the hills and thought of the space he would devote to it in his book. At dawn, carrying a heavy kit-bag, he tip-toed out of the house, down through the mist to the ford where he found the boy awaiting him with two horses. They lost several precious minutes strapping the bag in place, and Conroy cuffed his guide in his hurry to be away. He didn’t want to be stopped now at the very outset of his real work. At last when all was ready he swung heavily into the saddle and cried out good- naturedly— “Lead on, Apatwa, my boy, now we shall f find our way to the truth!” He raised his riding-crop and pointed ahead with a commanding gesture. “ Yassah,” dubiously answered the boy. “When we get back-” Conroy got no further for McLaird, at a run, loomed out of the mist. In his hand he carried a murderous looking Colt which he leveled at the speaker. “Come on, Conroy,” he ordered. “Get off that horse!” Conroy hesitated and he came closer. “Come on,” he repeated. “Get down.” But for once in his life he was caught off his guard. Conroy, bending forward as if to dismount, suddenly brought down the butt end of his heavy crop like a flail. It glanced off McLaird’s head and crashed on to his shoulder. He fell to the ground and rolled over. “That,” muttered Conroy, “will keep you quiet for a while.” And he urged his horse forward across the crumpled body. KASSUI, chief of all the Tiukas, owner of twelve wives, squatted on the sacred stone seat before the men’s sleeping-house and listened with wick- ed eyes to the reports of his spies. “O Chief,” said Ngoroto, a lean warrior with hair bleached yellow after the fashion of the Lifou people—a proof that he had traveled greatly, “I come from Vohlu. They have grown fat and lazy once again. Their men are weak and their women many. Their pigs would gorge our ovens. The way lies open before us to the very heart of their huts. We ^ieed heads for the shrine-” “Go ari, I know,” snapped Kassui tugging at his gray beard. “My son’s new house will need its attendant spirit, and my father’s soul calls for another slave.” For among the Tiuka a dead man should go in state to his heaven with an escort of prisoners’ souls to wait on him, and no house is complete unless the skull of an enemy be stuck upon the ridge-pole to ward off evil. “Is that all you saw?” asked Kassui after a pause. “ The Vohlu are close to the valley, and the white men lend them their magic, I am told.” Ngroto laughed and spat scornfully. “Wah! I watched for a week in the shadow of their skull shrine on the hill. They no longer honor their dead, and their magic is harmless. Do I not know? See-” From his loin-cloth he produced a handful of human teeth which he threw on the ground before the chief. The assembly swayed and shuddered. Who knew what might not befall? All were not as strong as Ngoroto to withstand evil. But an old man laughed shrilly and slapped his shriveled thigh in derision. “O Chief,” he cackled, “Ngoroto is strong. Already he has slain as many men as I have toes on my feet. He has much68 Adventure honor among us—but he has no cunning!” The crowd shrank back as Ngoroto arose grasping his war-club, but the old man still squatted on the ground and nodded at Kassui. He was Ngoroto’s great-great- uncle by marriage and no stripling may strike such an one without losing his soul. “What is your tale, old man?” grunted Kassui. “This—” he answered. “While Ngoroto lurked by the deserted shrine, I went down into the village as one who comes in peace. Have I not a woman of the Vohlu in my hut?” “ Wahl” yelled the assembly, and the men stamped their feet in approval. Here, in- deed, was temerity worthy of a man of Tiuka. “And when I found my kin,” went on the old man, “ I saw a very fat white man sitting by their cooking-house. I made inquiries. He is neither a bearer of magic nor a spy but a fool who pays in the money of the French to be told lies. They sit about him all day and receive notes for their talk. It is said these notes will buy many things such as tobacco and cloth and rum—but who knows the truth when a Vohlu speaks? “How did he come among them?” asked Kassui. “He is said to love the Vohlu. He sought them out alone, for his guide fled when they reached the first little hills. _ He says he is the Vohlu’s brother, but they will surely kill him when they need a sacrifice.” “His head,” suddenly exclaimed Kassui, “shall go on my son’s house, and we shall feast as we did of old.” He stepped from the sacred seat and hur- ried out of the village up into the bush on the steep hillside. “He has gone to talk to our fathers,” whispered Ngoroto. “War!” shrilly cackled the old man, and the assembly clapped and chanted while the young men, three deep around the throbbing drums, danced the Eragi war-dance, which is dedicated to the ghosts of the ancestors who have died in battle. The call was heard from afar. It was an- swered from remote hillsides and from lost villages. There came warriors with their clubs and spears, elders with the hideous masks of the secret lodges, keepers of sacred places, naked and daubed with red ocher. All through the night and- all the following day they drifted in, gathering family by family, group by group until the tale was complete and not a man missing. There followed a great feast while the elders took ghostly council at the shrine beneath the banyan-tree and the drums boomed incessantly. Then the elders re- turned to the round-house and the tribes- men, in a hushed silence, waited expectant- ly. Just before dawn, when the tree-tops began to take shape, Kassui appeared in the doorway. In his hand he held aloft a bundle—a wild taro to which had been bound a tuft of the red wooly hair of the flying fox and the broken shaft of a spear— the sign of war. Shout upon shout filled the air, and the drums spoke with a new voice as the young men stamped and swayed to the measure which called all men to the killing. But war, even among the hill-tribes of New Caledonia, is a matter of preparation. Spies must make sure that the path lies clear, ancestors must be propitiated and omens be observed. The spears, the clubs and the throwing stones must all be placed for a second upon a certain carved rock that the spirit of the original founder of the tribe may enter into the weapons and guide them swiftly on their way. All went well with the Tiuka. The yam harvest on the terraced hillsides was abun- dant, the warriors grew sleek and smooth- skinned and the comer posts of Kassui’s son’s house were put in the ground the day after his marriage; for no woman needs a home until her first child is born. “The young men grow restless,'" an elder at last told Kassui. “All things are ready. We wait for a sign-■” “Aie,” answered the chief, “it has come!” And he sprinkled water over the fire in the men’s house. The steam dissolved and vanished before it reached the thatch, proof that the ancestors had accepted the liba- tion and the old men began to chant until the drums drowned their clamor. That night the men of Tiuka swarmed out of their valley and dropped swiftly down the rocky slope beyond. Dark bodies gleamed here and there in the moonlight, occasionally a dislodged stone rattled from ledge to ledge or a spear rasped against a tree-trunk. Before the cool dawn wind rustled through the palm leaves they reached the outskirts of the Vohlu village. There fol- lowed a rush and a yell and a scream.The Naked Truth 69 Flames suddenly licked up the walls of a hut, a shower of sparks flew wide and roof after roof caught fire. By the red glare the warriors swept through the village, and the Vohlu died, squealing for mercy. Long before the morning mists left the valley the village was reduced to ashes, where here and there a headless body sprawled in the open, and Mr. Albert Con- roy, the farseeing financier, was being prod- ded none too gently up and up and up the mountain side, through thorny bushes and over sharp rocks. In a hysterical voice he cursed his captors, for he was clad in red morocco slippers and pink pa jamas, which were rapidly shredding and disappearing as he clambered up the slope, and he was suffering acutely. To make matters worse he was herded with a score of Vohlu pris- oners and as many of their lamenting women folk. He fainted when the blazing sun beat down on his unprotected neck and two other captives were given the task of bearing him throughout the day. When he recovered he lay face down in the dust before the sacred stone where sat Kassui. Some one had thrown water over him and his hair hung damply in his eyes. “Wah!” grumbled the chief. “Never have I seen such fat on any man.” Conroy sat up slowly gathering together odd strips of pink cloth which had clung to him to his journey’s end. In a wide circle about him stood or squatted a crowd of men blood-stained, wild-eyed, with great head-dresses of cowrie shells and feathers. On the fringe of the crowd women hovered, watching him. Beyond the cluster of huts he saw the towering forest-covered hills penning him in. A Vohlu man with his arms bound tightly behind him was thrust forward into the circle and cringed before Kassui. “ What is this fat man’s magic?” demanded the chief. “Great One, I speak the truth, seeking mercy,” came the stammered answer. “His magic is great. He rules men of his own kind by virtue of tokens which he gives to those who serve him. White men pay him homage and one who lives near the coast at Nakitalo, a powerful man indeed, is said to be his slave.” “Where are these tokens?” queried Kassui anxiously. In his haste to seek favor the Vohlu man fell into the trap. “The tokens were burnt when his hut was set on fire,” he answered. “Can he create more?” “Who knows? He had many with him when he reached us.” “Then tell him that unless he produce two fists full of such tokens he shall die, and his soul go to keep my son’s house.” “And shall I live?” “Tell him!” thundered Kassui, and the circle of warriors narrowed in. So the-prisoner made shift to explain the order to Conroy, who understood not at all. ‘ Notes—or we die. Money, French money—or we die,” the captive repeated again and again. Light dawned at last in Conroys’ mind and a great weight lifted from his heart. These people were not as dangerous as he had been led to believe. They dared not kill him. They were holding him for ran- som. “Money? Why, my good fellow, where am I to get it? If they will send a messen- ger to Nakitalo they will be given a thou- sand francs. Also explain that unless I am released forthwith I shall see that these people are punished, severely punished. I cannot imagine how-” “What does he say?” sharply queried Kassui. “Is it a spell?” “No spell, Great One, but he cannot pro- duce the tokens,” answered the Vohlu man. “If you send to Nakitalo, he says you will be given tokens in handfuls for his release, for he is needed by his people. More I cannot understand.” “Make clear that unless the tokens appear now he shall die when my son’s house is ready,” retorted Kassui. Gradually Conroy began to understand. He must produce money or die. Fear, icy- fingered, clutched at his throat and he tried to bluster and bully while Kassui gnawed at his beard and watched him with blood- shot eyes. “He has no magic,” miserably repeated the Vohlu man. “May I be spared, O Great One?” For answer the chief raised his'left hand, and a half-dozen spears struck down the shrieking captive as the warriors closed in to the kill. Now Conroy, for all his vast experience, had never seen death, violent death at such70 Adventure close quarters. Street accidents he knew and understood, for he had seen an old man knocked down and killed by an automobile in the busiest district of Sydney; but he could not face this ghastly horror. With dis- tended eyes and drooping jaw he watched a warrior chop off the fallen man’s head with one blow of a bamboo knife, saw them pick up the body and bear it away, heard the clamor of the other captives and then fainted dead away for the second time that day. Thus he mercifully missed the great feast which took place that night when the Tiuka ceremonially ate their victims to acquire their soul substance and afterward danced to the sound of the drums, stopping fre- quently that each man might recount his deeds of bravery. ^TIIE ensuing days, however, be- came one long nightmare. Another prisoner had been spared for a fu- ture sacrifice and through him Conroy dis- covered how and when he was to die. The surviving captive had a gift for graphic description, and Conroy was tortured by visions of his own bleaching skull adorning the new hut. He vividly pictured the last rite when he would be led out to his death surrounded by frenzied tribesmen. To his surprize he was not harshly treated. True, he was kept under guard but the Tiukas, even Kassui, provided him with food and conversed with him through the Vohlu man. When he spoke of his great power in his distant home they grinned and chuckled. The man of Vohlu, at once resigned and contented, worked in the fields with his cap- tors and took up a definite place in their social system. He was given a wife and a hut of his own and lived apparently, on the best of terms with his neighbors. His days were long before him, he told Conroy, for his soul would only be needed when the taro season came around. Mean- while he was happy. He had food and shel- ter and a woman. Death came to all men, to each his appointed hour. Conroy merely shuddered and glanced fearfully at the hut which his soul was to guard. The whole tribe worked spasmodic- ally at its construction. For days it stood untouched, then a score of men would work at it, singing lustily while they lashed and pounded and chopped. Conroy grew de- pressed and frightened. The remnants of his pajamas and his slippers had long since vanished and he went about clad in a strip of bark cloth, which a native woman, laughingly, had thrown to him. Gone was his mission and his love for primitive people, gone his desire to help and improve them. In his mind he evolved drastic disciplinary systems to curb and check their barbarous ways. The sword and the bullet were mild when compared to the methods he would advocate. Above all things his soul revolted at their lack of cleanliness and decency. Once he wit- nessed an initiation ceremony which left him gasping in horror. When the crossbeams were put in place on the fateful house he tried to escape, only to be caught as he stumbled heavily in the darkness between the huts. He incurred no punishment for this offence except that Kassui spat at him and the women laughed him to scorn. At last, however, when the singing Workers began to thatch the hut and the women were collecting fruit and food for the coming feast, Conroy roused himself from his dejected torpor. He would not be killed like a scared rabbit, he told himself, he would make one last dash for freedom. “I can’t die like this,” he repeated again and again as he lay in the dark and listened to the heavy breathing of his jailer. Late at night he became hysterical and sobbed in great gulps. The native stirred restlessly in his sleep, and Conroy trembling, edged toward the dimly outlined doorway. His groping hand found the Tiuka’s club and his fingers closed around the smooth handle. Crouching on his knees he raised the weapon above his head. But he had no control over his muscles now. His arms refused to act. He was shaking with terror. “I can’t do it,” he whimpered. “I can’t do it. Oh, God, the last of my manhood has gone. The very last of my manhood.” Tears streamed down his dirty cheeks as he looked at the dark body barring his way. Still murmuring, “The last of my man- hood,” he suddenly stiffened and brought the club down on the sleeper’s head. Somehow he crept out of the village and up through the bush. As the dawn broke he reached the crest and paused for breath. He was torn and bleeding and still sobbed as he lashed the low bushes about him with the blood-stained club.The Naked Truth 7i Away below him he heard faint cries which reached him softened and mellowed through the still morning air. The man- hunt had begun. The clamor increased in volume. He caught sight of a dark body leaping from rock to rock. They were after him already. And ahead lay the sea, placid and blue and still. He stumbled forward and began to toil down the hillside, falling and rolling among the rocks, crying aloud for help. McLAIRD sat with his feet cocked up on the window-sill reading a month-old copy of the Sydney Bulletin. The cigaret between his lips glowed at regular intervals and unhurried puffs of blue smoke emerged from his nostrils, curling slowly up into the hot, still air. Behind him at the table sat two heated gentlemen in faultless white clothes who glared at the back of his bald head and fidgeted uncomfortably. “This is ridiculous!” one of them declared. “Mr. Shelburn and I demand your atten- tion. Be kind enough to listen to me.” “Mr. Thorpe is quite right,” assented his companion. “You cannot refuse to answer our questions.” ' Well,” drawled McLaird, “it’s this way. You two have been here for a week telling me about Conroy and how much money you lose if he dies. I’ve been think- ing about him, too, and what an almighty beating up he’ll get if I ever meet him.” “Now, now,” admonished Mr. Shelburn, “No threats, please. The whole affair looks bad enough as it is.” “So you’ve said a good many times,” pointed out McLaird. “ Conroy disappears. I’m found with a dislocated collar-bone and a bruised head, so I must have murdered him. That’s the idea, isn’t it?” “I’m no lawyer,” answered Mr. Shelburn, “but on the face of it-” “Rot,” snapped McLaird. “You’re as crazy as that nut Conroy. Just hang about here for a few hours until the gendarme turns up and he will give you all the latest news. This business pleases him an awful lot, I should imagine; having to run around after a wild Australian when he could be writing nice reports or nursing his goats. D’you know, he lets a whole bunch of ’em stray into my fattening paddocks! Graft, that’s what it is. But he’s not a bad lad.” Mr. Thorpe helped himself to warm brandy and set the bottle down on the table with a crash. “I am not concerned with your absurd troubles,” he exclaimed angrily. “Mr. Con- roy is urgently needed to complete the plans for the Australasian Livestock Cor- poration. He must be found if he’s alive. We are losing hundreds of thousands through this delay.” ‘ I’ve heard that before,” McLaird gently reminded him. “My people in Melbourne told me to give you every assistance. I’ve tried to. I’ve been doing nothing else ever since I got out of bed. And I want friend Conroy just as much as you do. Now leave me alone until the gendarme gets here. “But—” began Mr. Shelburn. McLaird turned and leveled his discon- certing blue eyes at the speaker. “Keep quiet,” he ordered, and Mr. Shel- burn sat back limply with a dismayed look on his face. The gendarme arrived in the middle of the afternoon, tired and disgusted, but he had some information. He had been up to the most remote Vohlu village, which he had found burnt to the ground. He had not been told about it earlier because the tribesmen wanted to hit back at their enemies. The attackers were little-known people living beyond the pre- cipitous slopes of Mount Ndoki. They had captured Conroy and taken him away alive, so a survivor had told him. “This man should be put in prison if he still lives,” grumbled the officer. “No per- mit did he have from me! No permission to visit these people. He should do twelve months in Noumea.” “Oui, oki,” assented McLaird. Then he translated the conversation for the benefit of his guests. “An expedition—” suggested Mr. Shel- burn. “And I shall have to ask for a squad of soldiers,” went on the gendarme. “It will ruin my district’s reputation. If I catch this imbecile it will go hard with him.” Here McLaird took command of the situation. “ I’m going up-country tomorrow hunting for strayed cows. I’ll have a look around for Conroy while I’m about it,” he told the official, and that worthy nodded his head vigorously. If his friend, Monsieur McLaird, was fit72 Adventure enough to undertake such a journey, well then, he would accompany him. Perhaps they could locate this crapule without out- side help. “He’s probably dead,” sighed McLaird. “but we’ll, find out something definite. I’d have gone before, but I couldn’t move, my shoulder was too bad.” The gendarme was delighted; here was a chance of maintaining his district’s good standing. On the morrow then, at five o’clock, he would call for McLaird. To- gether they would scour the country. He saluted and withdrew before either of Con- roy’s friends understood quite what was happening. When matters were explained to them, however, they grew frantic, de- manding punitive expeditions, reprisals and detectives. “I protest against such methods!” cried Shelburn. “I want to register a most em- phatic protest. The proper authorities, the competent authorities, should deal at once with this matter.” “It will take another three weeks before any outside help can reach Nakitalo,” an- swered McLaird. “Conroy was probably killed the very day they caught him. It’s a certainty he won’t be alive in another month. This is about our last chance, and it’s a mighty poor one.'” “Then I insist that we accompany you on this expedition,” said Thorpe. “I want to get to the bottom of this affair.” “You want to come along?” grinned Mc- Laird. “That’s fine. It will do you good. I’ll order a couple of horses for you at once.’' NEITHER Thorpe nor Shelburn would have undertaken the next day’s journey had they realized the dangers they were called upon to face. At first they were rather pleased with the nov- elty of the ride. Ahead in single file along the shaded track all bordered with palms and pines and silver-barked niaouli trees, went four native stockmen bareback on lean Australian cattle ponies, then came Shel- burn on a high-stepping dun-colored mare and Thorpe fumbling at the bit of a rangy seventeen-hand horse with a wicked under- . lip. Behind rode McLaird and the gen- darme wrangling as usual about the latter’s pasture rights. “This,” quoth Shelburn as they wound along following the river bed, “is entrancing- ly tropical. The color of those hills—splen- did!” He waved a well-kept hand at the tum- bled mountains turned to gold by the rising sun. “Quite,” answered Thorpe looking at his horse’s ears which had a nasty habit of lying flat back against his head as no good horse’s should. After lunch they reached the foot-hills and left the narrowing valley for a path which carried them up, straight up, until Shelburn, looking downward, called back— “I say, Thorpe, this is trick riding!” Thorpe’s answer was brief and incisive, but he kept his eyes straight ahead of him and held his reins bunched in his left hand, for his horse exhibited a growing fondness for grisly pas-seids neatly performed on crumbling ledges overhanging dangerous heights. The next day when they attacked the real mountains the track grew worse. “Like riding over waves in a high sea,” thought Thorpe, sawing at his horse’s now bleeding mouth. At last they reached the charred and de- serted ruins of the Vohlu village and camped among the wind-blown ashes. “Tomorrow,” said McLaird when they had dined off corned beef and stale bread, “we’ll push along across that crest.” He pointed to the frowning height just ahead of them. “The gendarme thinks the raiders came down that way. There’s said to be a big tribe in the next valley, but nobody knows much about them.” “Are we in sufficient numbers?” asked Thorpe. “We might meet with opposi- tion.” “If we do, just keep out of the way,” an- swered McLaird. “We don’t want any more deaths in the family.” When they went forward the following morning there was no track to follow. On foot, leading their horses, they clambered up through dense undergrowth and around boulders. Shelburn’s white riding-breeches ripped open at the knee, and he left a per- fectly good leather legging hanging in some particularly thorny bushes. Thorpe who had come to fear his horse jerked savagely at the bit when he found himself being left behind. His steed, flattening back his ears,The Naked Truth 73 began to practise an intricate jig on what seemed to be the world’s overhanging cor- nice. The others at last reached a rocky pla- teau, half-way up the mountain-side and rode slowly forward beneath the pitiless sun, and Thorpe cursed with a fluency and directness which would have astonished his more fortunate colleagues of the Australian Livestock Corporation. Sweating, panting and infuriated he reached level ground and swung into the saddle after his horse had danced and circled around for several min- utes. In his exasperation he did a very rash thing—he used both his long, nickel-plated, heavily roweled spurs, and his horse bolted headlong at right angles to the party, taking boulders and bushes in his sudden mad rush. By some miraculous intervention which had nothing to do with good horsemanship Thorpe kept his seat and his wild cries reached McLaird just before he was carried out of sight behind a high clump of bushes. The party wheeled and followed as best they could while Shelburn, closing the cavalcade at a dignified trot, kept crying: “Stop him! Oh, stop him!” THE Tiukas recaptured Conroy without trouble. They found him sprawling and exhausted at the foot of a giant pine. In fact, at the moment, he was past caring what his fate might be. He wanted to lie flat on the ground and sleep and rest. So they bound and trussed him hand and foot, offered a prayer to the spirits of the giant pine for having arrested the captive’s flight, and Squatted down in the shade for a while before bearing such a weight back across the hills. “Why not finish him here?” suggested a young warrior who had only killed his first man in the raid on the Vohlus and had but scant respect for traditions. “Kah! Be still,” grunted one of his com- panions. “He must be alive and whole for the sacrifice; strong and healthy, otherwise his soul would be weak and useless.” “And I say it is better to kill while the blood is hot,” retorted the young man. “That is indeed a sacrifice, but if Kassui wants this fat man-” He arose and stood looking out toward the distant sea, thinking of the wives he would have now that he had reached man’s estate. Suddenly there came a thunder of hoofs and cries. “Look,” he yelled, “they come to his rescue!” And all at once they saw, heading straight at them, a group of charging horsemen, led by a demon who yelled with a wide open mouth. The young warrior paused to hurl a spear, a bullet caught him neatly in the chest as he lunged forward, and he went in state to the land of his fathers, dying with honor in the heat of battle. But his companions who knew the ways of white men fled, fled and were engulfed beneath the thick under- growth on the hillside. The hurtling spear struck Thorpe’s horse full in the neck. It halted, reared and crashed to the ground throwing its rider into a tangle of low shrubs. McLaird, with a smoking revolver in his hand, helped him to his feet.	/ “What a horse,” spluttered Thorpe. “A devil!” “Well, you’ve got a long walk ahead of you,” answered McLaird. “But what about this black boy?” “Gendarme,” he added as the latter rode up, “ what do you make of those people?” “Tiukas,” replied the officer very much out of breath. “Many of them. It is strange. Let us go and see.” They found Conroy shortly afterwards, quite naked, very pink of body and very red of face, lying on his back on a bed of pine-needles. Somebody gasped with astonishment, somebody chuckled, somebody else mur- mured— “My lord, what a beau-tiful baby!” and they broke down and roared and laughed until tears streamed from their eyes. When a pause came Shelburn cackled: “He’s so pink! The dear boy!” and they laughed again until they grew weak and sore. Then they cut his thongs and listened to his opinions of mankind while they gulped and wiped their eyes. There is little more to tell. Along George Street, Sydney, where the financiers hold court, Conroy is called “the pink baby,” but not to his face, and he is apt to resent any references to his tour of inspection of the Pacific islands, which began and ended in New Caledonia. He takes no interest74 Adventure in people of ruder culture and has given Nakitalo Station, where McLaird still lives away his two books on native customs. and raises fat bullocks what time he argues Moreover, he has not bought up the with his friend the gendarme about goats. WAS a day of peace and quiet, i#y| cKjj of beautiful sunshine and in- $Q ||g| tense heat, wherefore the crew 0f Number One Piece shot craps in the shade of the gun-$hield for matches, cigarets and their next month’$ pay. They were not allowed to leave their gun, “per order,” and they had been fighting the war in the same manner for a week. That is to say, they rolled bones all day and slept until one o’clock each morning, when they would all awake, answer, “Here,” and go back to sleep again. Number Four Piece, which was off in the woods somewhere, did all the shooting for the battery, about ten rounds a night. The gunner, having made three passes, lighted one of his winnings and allowed that it was a great war. “I will bet you,” said he, “my second mess-kit of slum, that there are no Jerries on the other side of the Marne, nor any- where else in this sector. Here have we sat and worn out our uniforms at the seat for some seven or eight days, and there is a pile of tin cans back of the kitchen that you can see in Berlin, and not a-shot have we heard, and not so much as seen a balloon. No wonder this war has gone on for four years.” Now there was one within hearing who was shining the eye-pieces of his gas-mask and cleaning a pistol and burning the end of a piece of cork, and he looked up at this. “Look you, now, Sheehan, for the Con- nemara bog-trotter that you are. I will bet you a bottle of vin rooge, which I gave a Frog my slicker for this very morning, against your syrup ration for the week, that there are Huns across that river, and that I will bring one back for you to send home to your girl, more by token that the old man has detailed me to go patrol this very night with a wild crazy looey from the doughboys, a curse on him and all his, and I must go and prowl about in the wheat with no hat on. Moreover and beyond, I must blacken my face with this cork, so that when I am dead no man will know me, and I shall fill an unknown grave to the glory of some mail-order officer.” With that he applied himself to the cork once more. “You observers,” said Sheehan, “lead a hard life, what with your meals brought to you and every two nights in bed all night and no rocket-guard to stand, and I will bet you my allowance of Karo for the week, and if we have none then I will get a bottleThe Patrol 75 of white mule for my extra pair of shoes and bet that. But I am not very like to lose.” NOW when it began to grow dark the observer gathered his pistol and a short and husky knife to him and walked out through the wheat field and along a path to the road. There was a sign here that said Gas Alert, and the observer knew that it was just four hundred yards from that sign to the machine-guns that guarded the batteries’ flank, which was the reason for the sign’s being. So then he went on up the road and passed a cross-roads where the signs said Brownsville and Dragon Fly and Home Run, they being the names of regimental or battalion headquarters that were in that direction. The observer looked off at the German lines that were across the river and won- dered whether any one was watching him and thought that the sunset was gorgeous and that the valley of the Marne was quite the most beautiful place that he had ever seen. He went on down a road bordered by elms, ancient and immemorial, down into a little town where every house had blanket- ed windows, and after following a line of arrows went down into a cellar and reported to the officer that he found there. This one turned him over to a sergeant, who led him by a nice trench back of some houses and through some gardens to a place just under the railroad track, where he found some seven men, hatless, and each with his face blackened and a look of dis- quiet upon it, like a child seeing the castor- oil appear, or a man on horseback who is not quite sure of his seat. “I suppose,” said the observer, seeing no sign of any shoulder-bars, “that the officer is still eating his supper. Perhaps the steak was done a trifle too much, or the potatoes cold. So shall we sit here and wait, and the American Army suspend operations until Sears Roebuck lias smoked his cigar.” “We are waiting until it is dark,” said a voice that seemed to come across miles of snowy fields and glittering ice, and the observer looked and saw that he who spoke had on a heavy gold ring of peculiar design, and then he knew that there was the officer, and that he was a real one, and that it was like to be a wild night. “Now that the artillery is here, give ear to our plan,” said the icy voice. “We are directed to cross this river and to bring back a prisoner if we have to go to Germany to get him. Now we will cross in a boat which has been provided and go straightway up the bank in single file. I will be in the lead, and each man shall hold the ankle of the man in front of him. As we crawl along, should I kick twice, the signal will be passed along the line, and we will all execute right into line, each man with his bombs in his hand, and when I say, ‘Now!’ then let each rush forward. For the rest, let every man do as the man in front of him does. Any questions?” “I have no bombs,” said the observer. “It will be your duty,” said the officer, “to locate machine-gun nests, strong points and trenches, by the system of Cartesian coordinates, and not to be throwing high explosives around with an unpractised hand. This is once when the artillery will not shoot up its own troops.” When it was dark the patrol crept over the railroad track and down through the wheat to the river’s edge. It was damp and cold, and the observer was stricken with chills, and his teeth chattered, and his body shook like a leaf. When they were getting into the boat, he nearly capsized it, and had he done so, they were all dead, for the other bank was German and was but ten feet away. They lay under the shelter of the bank for some time, and then up and across a narrow path into the wheat, the artillery man clinging for his life to a thick ankle. Now this ankle belonged to a large, rough man, by name “Gopher,” a Tar Heel, and he was a good man to cling to. So they went on, and ever and anon stopped to listen, and loud was the pounding of their hearts. The wind whistled through the wheat, and the observer wondered how they would ever get back across the river, and the tears ran down the bridge of his nose and tickled it so that he was hard put to keep from sneezing. Now some one with few brains and much curiosity found a rocket on the American side and touched it off, to see what would happen. At once every man with a Very pistol discharged it so that the bank on both sides of the river was as bright as day. The patrol lay without breathing but cursing inwardly, for they knew that they must have worked either east or west and were in front of troops that had not been warned that there was a patrol out, and by7 6 Adventure the fact that the lights were almost over them they knew they were not far from the bank for all their crawling. Moreover, the Americans were having a merry time with their machine-guns, and although the bullets were all going high, still it was not pleasant to hear the pounding and see the wheat falling and dropping around one’s ears. It came like a thunderbolt. Gopher kicked the observer suddenly in the face, and he rushed forward without further parley, falling headlong into a shell-hole. There was a startled grunt, and he saw some one running off trailing a blanket. Out pistol and a bullet away to the moon, the lieutenant kicking him vigorously and cursing him to a fare-you-well, and a free fight going on under his nose. The light went out, and darkness came that you could taste. Much straining and cursing, then a thud, and silence. Two husky whispers proclaimed— ‘"I’ve got one of the illegitimates.” Startled mutterings, and— “Why, I thought you was one of them,” from two throats at once. “Hot dog!” said a voice. “You got teeth on you like a shark.” “Oh, blessed day,” said the lieutenant, “that ever I joined the Army! What an aggregation of wooden heads and tipsy soldiers! You naughty, naughty boys, not to know enough to be able to tell one another apart from the enemy, for the double- cross, compound, triple-expansion, nameless, brainless descendants of horrible ancestry that you are. Blight me and strike me all colors of the rimrocked rainbow if I ever saw anything like it before. And ‘Wild West Artillery’ here, shooting off his pistol right in front of the whole German Army. By this and by that, you ought to write the Kaiser a letter that you are going to call. Now look at that—” as a rocket curved up behind them, green and blue— “Fritz will reap and sow and harrow this field with artillery and machine-gun until the prince of all evil himself wouldn’t have it. And after all this commotion, which was as much as would be made by a division with trains, Y. M. C. A. and all, we have got no prisoner and are like to be made some ourselves.” “Not so,” said Gopher, “for we have one, on whose head I now sit, and I know that he is a German, for I can smell him.” Now this prisoner was for the moment quite still, as Gopher had felt him wiggle and had wound the barrel of his pistol about the German’s head just over the ears. In his limp state, then, he was confided to the care of Gopher and another man, called the “Squarehead,” because no one knew his real name, the patrol coming from all over the regiment. “If I find, upon returning home, that you have lost my prisoner, then shall you come softly back and get another one, and no mistake. Now, let us begone from this shell-hole.” SO THEN they started the return, crawling single file as before, with Gopher and the Squarehead in the middle with the prisoner and the rest with their hearts in their throats. There was no artillery fire and few lights, which meant that the enemy had out patrols looking for them, and their hearts sank in consequence. It was growing colder as the night ad- vanced, and the way back to the river seemed overlong. They had'been crawling since the world began, thought the artillery- man, and then he tried to think how long it had taken them to come from the river, and it seemed to him that they were, some- what overlong on the homeward way. The wheat stopped. They were on the edge of a road. Two cautious kicks, and the patrol rushed to the front. At once they were all among flower pots and crashing glass, lights going up everywhere, machine- guns pounding, men rising up under foot, a dark shape jabbing at the artilleryman with a bayonet, a house with windows that leered in the ghastly light from the flares, the lightning thought that the patrol was lost and that it had crawled into Chartreves or Jaulgonne. The observer fled headlong into the wheat. For the infantry the task of fighting hand to hand with angry Huns. Two jumps, and he swan-dived in gorgeous style into a shell-hole, among a number of soft things. “You-!” said a soft whisper, and the artilleryman’s wind was at once shut off with a hand of steel, the while something hard was slammed against his ribs. “Let him up,” quoth another voice, “it is one of our guys.” The three men dismounted from the fourth one’s head, leaving him to gasp and choke as best he might. “Now,” said one, resuming a conversationThe Patrol 77 that had been interrupted, “get gone, and see that you lose not my prisoner. Give me a gun and a couple of eggs, and I will see if I can not hold Jerry in play a while.” Now all this time there was great thrash- ing-about in the wheat and much muttering in an unknown tongue, so that it appeared that the patrol would have scant chance to get back to the river. Howbeit, they eased out by inches, the observer, the Squarehead and Gopher going first with the precious prisoner, who was still in the land of Nod, and the lieutenant bringing up the rear. A long hiss, and a faint plop. The patrol hugged the ground until the light would die out. Six inches from the observer’s face was a German, looking at him with slack jaw and goggling eyes. Both recoiled with speed, a cry, a tearing grunt. “Run like -!” and the artilleryman sped toward the river, toward the American lines, and toward Spain and the African coast. Had his wind lasted, he would have been on the edge of the Sahara by daybreak. The flare still burned. The officer was standing up and was taking them as they came, until both guns were empty. So died a very gallant gentleman. So long as one has his face toward the enemy, terror keeps at a distance. Let him once turn, let him feel that safety lies before and that a bloodthirsty foe presses on his heels, that all is lost save his own neck, and a man’s own fright will drive him on when all danger of the enemy has long since ceased. The artilleryman ran at the top of his speed. He fell into shell-holes ancl scram- bled out of them. His breath came in sobs. He threw away his pistol, belt and gas- mask. He wept. He cared not the slightest whether he was seen or not. He took no heed to the fact that all this banging and yelling would arouse the whole sector, and that the Americans would gaily shoot up that wheat field until a mouse was not safe in it. So the Providence that watches over fools brought him safely to the river again. There was a little path along the river, where aforetime youths and maidens were wont to walk of an evening. The observer lay on his stomach on the far side of the path, trying to regain some of his wind and to quiet the roaring in his ears. The pound- ing of his heart shook him like a leaf. If he tried to cross the path and swim the river, most likely the Americans would lay down a rain of steel and throw bombs and have a most delightful time killing him. If he called out that he was a friend, Jerry would make a colander out of him from the near side of the bank. While he debated with himself he heard a stealthy creeping along the path, and when he was like to die from fear and horror he heard words and at once knew that the creepers were Americans, for they were both cursing a third to their heart’s content. There is no more heart- breaking thing than to have to swear at some one in whispers. Gopher it was, and the Squarehead, and the prisoner, who still slept. “He began to take on and squeak a while back,” said Gopher, “but I hung one on his chin, and he has held his peace the while we dragged him around in the weeds by the slack of his neck.” It seemed that Gopher knew where there was a machine-gun whose crew knew him, and it was his mind to crawl along until they were opposite and then hurl his gas- mask, which had his name on it, across the river, leaving the little book on which one was supposed to write each time he used the mask, hanging outside. On the book he would write— “Throw us a rope, and shoot like -• when you want us to come over.” This then was done. The note was written as best it could be in the dark and the three started down-river, dragging the Jerry with them. They stayed in the wheat, but not so far in as to get themselves lost, and well it was that they did, for they passed several dark, humpy things that they knew were machine-guns, with their crews lying along- side in a hole. Anon they paused, and Gopher crept down to the edge of the bank and gave a mighty heave to his gas-mask. They heard it land. Shortly thereafter a length of rope whistled through the air. A flare went up at this, and much banging ensued. It died down, only to have the whole American line leap into a long, con- tinuous pounding. “Let’s go!” and they were in the water and scrambling through the reeds on the other side. Eager hands dragged them under a little foot bridge. Others pulled right lustily78 Adventure on the rope which Gopher had tied around the prisoner and brought him over, a little damp but not spoiled. A group of officers arrived, bringing a doctor. Excitement, hurried whispering, cigarets, hand-shaking, clapping on the back, feeling of great pride by the patrol, visions of the D. S. C. “When,” said the doctor, who was kneel- ing over the prisoner, “was this man shot?” “He was not shot,” said Gopher. “I smote him as to the jaw.” “Not so,” said the medico, and he pointed to the blouse which he had taken off, all soaked with blood, and then to a small hole just over the prisoner’s collar-bone. “It is in my mind that this Hun was shot earlier in the evening and laid aside to cool, for his wound has been dressed by unskilful hands. Now whether that long ridge-run- ning Tar Heel really heard him squeak or thought he did is not material, for it is very certain that he has been plugged through the lungs, and has been dead some time, for he is quite cold.” Whereat the survivors of the patrol, being but children of a larger growth after all, wept bitterly. THE MEN WHO WOULDN'T BE LED by Wm. L. Mallabar CHARLIE got his in a barroom scrap Somewhere north of the Line; Tom was doped on the Barbary Coast; Jack was crushed in a mine. There isn’t one of the old gang left But me—and I’m nearly dead; The last of the crew—the crowd I knew, The men who wouldn’t be led. We’re not so bad when you figure us out; We played the game pretty square. We always shared the good—and the bad, And treated the worst of them fair. It was just a kink in our brains, I guess, That made us hate home and a bed; We had to get out and travel about, The men who wouldn’t be led. We have left our bones on the desert plains And up on the Yukon Trail. We have faced big odds, laughed at men and gods, But you never saw one of us quail. To us a flag is only a rag, But we fought for the hand that fed; For the Game—won or lost; no count of the cost, The men who wouldn’t be led. Up on a trestle or down in a mine, On the veld—in the hills—through the snow, We have followed our choosings, a will-o’-wisp fire; All we knew was—it forced us to go. And now I am going—that last shot was straight; No name—“Unidentified Dead;” Oh, the tales that we’ll tell when we gather in hell— The men who wouldn’t be led. “The Men Who Wouldn't Be Led,” copyright, 1922, by Wm. Mallabar.Author of “The Pal with the Number Twelves,” “The Commissary in the Hills,” etc. v HAR d’ y'u come frum?” An un com pi im en tary empha- sis distinguished the use of the personal pronoun. Ed Elsware, the speaker, was a huge deep-chested man of the pioneer type whose muscles bulged conspicuously beneath his dirty khaki clothes. The queries he addressed to the newcomers were short and pointed. He was looking over their physical qualifications prior to assigning them to their jobs. “New York,” replied Bob Kingdon whose apparel, though now soiled and travel- stained, bespoke the man of culture. “N’ Yawk, hey?” commented Elsware with an unenthusiastic grunt. “I hed some N’ Yawk fellers on th’ job oncet or twicet, but they didn’t stay long. I never yit seed one of ’em thet was willin’ t’ shake han’s wi’ hard work.” “I’ll do the best I can,” was the reassuring reply of Kingdon, who nevertheless had a guilty consciousness that his acquaintance- ship with hard work was likewise likely to prove both short and uncongenial. “Put this feller down fur th’ rock cut,” directed Elsware sourly to the camp clerk. “We’ll try ’im out thar.” Kingdon and hard work soon became acquainted. High above him the jaws of a deep rock-cut opened wide toward the scorching sky. Below and about him stretched piles of blasted rock newly torn by an explosion from the face of a granite hill S'N'Yawk and EXsware,” copyright, 1922, by Max Bonier. 79 through which the men were cutting. Around him two-score sweating bodies strained at their toil. They broke up the huge pieces of blasted rock with heavy sledges and loaded the fragments upon dump cars preparatory to being drawn away and dumped into the “fill.” At intervals upon hearing the sharp warning, “Fire! Fire!” the workers would leap for shelter behind jutting ledges of rock while the dynamiter “bulldozed” a big boulder. This, having been split into sec- tions by the charge, would be pounced upon by the returning workmen and smashed by the sledges into pieces small enough to handle. The hot air was filled with eter- nally-circling black flies, the steady clink of hand-drills biting their way slowly through hard granite and the ring of steel on steel as the husky drillmen drove home their heavy blows. Sweat was running into Kingdon’s eyes; black flies were persistently lodging in his eyes and nose and ears; his soft hands were cut and blistered from handling jagged pieces of rock; his back was aching. Alto- gether he felt in no mood for manual toil. “I wonder how Dante came to overlook this?” he muttered. “This must be the latest style in hells.” The New Yorker’s musings were swept" suddenly and rudely out of his mind, how- ever, by the burly foreman of the rock cut who thrust a ten-pound hammer into his reluctant hands.8o Adventure “Hi, young feller!” he bawled threaten- ingly. “Take this sledge an’ git busy!” Kingdon shamelessly spat upon his blistered palms and began trying to wield the heavy tool. Each time the hammer struck its hard target a fresh shock was registered on his tortured spine. Whenever he would pause in his efforts and seek to straighten the kinks out of his aching back, he seemed to see the foreman’s keen glance fastened ominously on him. When the gang knocked off work Kingdon walked dejectedly to the bunk-house trying to de- cide whether he was a hero, martyr or plain fool. • Night and repose brought a stiffening of the muscles. Morning found him scarcely more competent than a cripple. Nevertheless he went unfalteringly to the cut, picked up the heavy hammer and began a second day’s torture between the grim rock walls. The minutes crawled like snails across the long day. The inexorable sun from a cloudless stronghold threw vertical mid-Summer shafts upon the toiling forms beneath, superheating the still air that was filled with black flies and rock-dust and the fumes of frequent blasts. Kingdon strove manfully to perform the unaccus- tomed labor; but he could not help realizing that toward him continually the suspicious gaze of the foreman was being cast. At nightfall after he had eaten his"supper he was suddenly confronted by the huge bulk of the walking boss. “N’ Yawk,” demanded the latter dis- gustedly. “Y’u hain’t never worked on a railroad—hev y’u?” “No, to tell you the truth, I haven’t,” was the New Yorker’s ready and candid response. “Wal,” continued Elsware sarcastically, “Th’ foreman tells me y’u ain’t got no guts fer th’ rock—termorrer y’u’re a-goin’ in th’ sand-cut.” Morning found Kingdon shoveling sand with the sand-gang. The job, which con- sisted of digging through a hill and loading the sand upon dump-cars, proved to be somewhat easier than the slavery of the rock-cut. Unfortunately, however, no blasting went on in the sand cut. Conse- quently there were no pauses in the labor. The shoveling was continuous. Ten hours a day the routine was stoop and shovel, swat black flies, stoop and shovel. The monotony was intolerable. “Us blokes ’as got a - good boss, Yank,” confided a fellow shoveler to Kingdon. “ ’E don’t s’y nothink hat all.” That this was very true the New Yorker soon discovered; but, while the foreman of the sand cut didn’t “say anything,” never- theless his eyes proved to be even busier than those of the rock foreman had been. All day long he maintained an attitude of silent surveillance, literally looking down the men’s collars and never even consider- ately walking away for a minute to give them a chance to straighten their backs. All day Kingdon stooped and shoveled, swatted black flies, stooped and shoveled. He feared that he was beginning to contract curvature of the spine. All day long the foreman’s eyes seemed to be drilling holes in his shirt collar; so he kept on stooping and swatting and shoveling. Gradually also there grew upon him an unaccountable tendency to scratch. He had heard some of the men complaining of sand-fleas in their blankets and therefore concluded that a few of these pests had wormed their way beneath his clothes. Nevertheless—to sat- isfy himself upon this point, which op- pressed him with an increasing uneasiness— after working hours he went to a secluded spot in the forest and examined his under- clothing. “Fleas!” he muttered in amazement. “If these be fleas, where are their wings?” Suddenly the full horror of his plight burst upon him. With a shudder he ran to the office and had his account charged with new underclothes. These he took, together with soap and towel, to a tiny lake that lay within gunshot of the camp. “Good ——!” he groaned. “Yale alum- nus, member of the exclusive Yorkville Club and—getting lousy on a railroad job!” | [ LIGHTNING flashed from the eyes rl|M of the big walking boss when he sought Kingdon after supper on the following day. “Blast y’u, N’ Yawk!” he bellowed. “Th’ sand foreman tells me y’u’re a-makin’ trouble in his gang!” “Why, Mr. Elsware-” “Thar hain’t no misters on this job, young feller! Y’u hain’t in N’ Yawk now!” The New Yorker dropped formality, still attempting to explain matters. “ ’E’s torkin’ roight, boss,” chimed in one of the shovelmen standing near. “Th’ /N’ Yawk and Elsware 81 Swede is mykin’ gyme of Yank, ’ere, an’ Yank ’its hout an’ ’ands th’-Swede a foiver.” Elsware glared the voluble cockney into silence. “Y’ur pusnal affairs hain’t no consarn o’ mine, N’ Yawk,” he went on harshly. “It jist happens this foreman is a Swede hisself—thet lets y’u in bad. Ef y’u hain’t got nuff sense t’ drive a hoss-” The walking boss glared at Kingdon with unutterable contempt and then swung abruptly on his heel and strode away. “What does he mean by ‘take out a horse’?” inquired the New Yorker curiously of the cockney. “Stroike me pink if you ’aven’t ’it it sorft, Yank!” exclaimed the shovelman enviously. “Nothink to do orl d’y but droive a—— ’orse!” “Drive a dump car, you mean?” “Roight, O, mytey.” “Well, to tell you the truth, cockney, I’ve never driven a horse in my life. I’m game to try it, though. Can’t be much about it to learn. I’ve often seen cabbies and coach- men do it.” “Bli’ me, Yank, if you haren’t a funny un!
