I wired the dad for money to get back, but he turned me down. Served me right. Now I’ve got to earn money to get back.” On the following morning Kingdon went early to the stable and stepped gingerly between the rows of breakfasting and stamping beasts until he located the stableman. “Elsware told me to take out a horse,” he announced briefly. “Wal, y’u’ll hev t’ take ol’ “Dinnymite” thar—he’s th’ only hoss thet ain’t a-workin’. “Dynamite!” echoed the New Yorker curiously. “What a name!” “Thet name jist suits ’im, young feller. He’s lybel t’ go off any time. Y’u got t’ handle ’im wi’ care.” “I’ll be kind to him,” promised Kingdon hastily. The stableman, choking a queer sound back into his throat, well-nigh swallowed his cud ' of tobacco. After Dynamite had broken his fast the harness was thrown upon his back and he was led forth. The animal proved to be coal-black excepting his eyes, which glowed with a strange red fire. “Giddap!” commanded Kingdon firmly after the manner of the city hack drivers, at the same time gently slapping the horse’s flanks with the reins. Dynamite giddaped, at the same time pulling his driver after him at headlong speed. He giddapped all the way to the rock-cut. Kingdon, with the reins wound firmly about his hands, served merely as a sort of rudder to steer the speedy beast whose broad flanks, for- tunately for the man behind them, broke down or swept aside the impeding forest undergrowths. “Well, he’s obedient, anyway,” gasped Kingdon, trying to regain his breath and picking the thorns and brambles out of his clothes. The fiery steed had paused at the mouth of the cut—head held proudly high and ruby eyes flashing—awaiting further orders. “Hook y’ur hoss on t’ this here kyar, young feller,” directed the foreman sharply. “Then go git y’ur load.” Kingdon began maneuvering with Dyna- mite’s steering-gear in an effort to haul his stern tackle to within hooking distance of the dump car. Dynamite’s hoofs stac- catoed nervously on the tow-path. “Be keerful o’ thet hoss, now,” warned the foreman harshly. “He’s been a-standin’ in th’ stable too long.” “Giddap, Dynamite,” said Kingdon cau- tiously. A hundred yards down the track was a crossover. This evidently did not enter into the calculations of Dynamite, whose private ideas on railroading seemed to counsel him to proceed via the most direct routes and ahead of schedule time. “Hang on t’ thet hoss!” yelled the fore- man frantically. That was precisely' what Kingdon was doing; but his one hundred and seventy pounds’ weight made little difference to Dynamite, who utterly ignored the New Yorker’s frantic signals to starboard his82 Adventure helm and continued his fleet course un- swervingly along the tow-path. The car left the rails at the crossover and landed across the ties, bringing the plunging horse suddenly back upon his haunches. “Y’u blasted ijit! Didn’t I tell y’u t’ hang on t’ th’ hoss?” shouted the foreman wrathfully. “Now my men’s got t’ lose time a-gittin’ y’ur kyar back on th’ rails ag’in, —•— y’u!” Kingdon’s eyes flashed. A slow rage was fermenting in his bosom, but he held his peace. Half a dozen workmen straggled leisurely over to the crossover and hoisted the dump-car back upon the rails. “Now give this feller a load,” directed the foreman angrily. “Give ’im a-big load, too—a load thet’ll take some o’ th’ hell’ry outen thet black devil, Dinnymite!” Dynamite’s sable hide, however, har- bored much more “hell’ry” than the fore- man was aware of. The stout collar strained against his broad chest as he bowed his neck and tugged obediently at the burden. The heavy load of rock began to move along the rails with gradually in- creasing speed. The rails led directly out to the crest of the advancing wall of crushed rock that was being thrown across the neighboring valley. This crest was called the “dump.” The terminating rails pro- jected a few inches beyond the edge of the dump and across their ends was bolted a piece of heavy timber called the “bumper.” The modus opercmdi was as follows. A dump-car was driven at a moderate rate of speed to within a few yards of this bumper. The horse would then be pulled sharply about, automatically unhooking the whiffletree chain from the car. The car would then proceed at its own momentum until it struck the bumper, the force of such impact operating the mechanism that dumped the load. The horse would then be rehooked to the dumped car and the process repeated. Dynamite was quite familiar with the details of this operation—much more so, in fact, than was his driver. Whether the powerful black had it in mind to show off his capabilities before his new attendant or whether his act was inspired by a pure love of deviltry, was a matter of individual opinion. At any rate, notwithstanding Kingdon’s desperate attempts at control, the horse assumed complete charge of the proceedings. He pulled the heavily loaded car toward the dump at full speed. The dismayed New Yorker could hear the fore- man’s voice filling the air with shouts and curses behind him, but all the power of his arms could not swerve Dynamite from his self-willed course. Ten yards from the dump the horse turned sharply about and unhooked himself with practised ease. The speeding car struck hard against the bumper and instantly dumped its contents. Then, with its momentum still urging it on, it turned completely over the obstacle and crashed down the side of the hill of rock in a series of somersaults until it landed bottom up in the valley a hundred feet below. Kingdon stood holding the reins with pal- sied hands until Dynamite, scenting his driver’s confusion and helplessness, took advantage of the situation by galloping away to freedom. The whiffletree, still attached to the traces, caught against a stump and snapped in twain as the powerful beast crashed his way through the forest. Kingdon’s ruin was complete. The gang from the rock cut came running excitedly out to the dump. Curses and epithets popped from the foreman’s mouth like sparks from an anvil. The New Yorker’s face paled and his hands clenched as he faced the foreman’s opprobrious attack. “Even a worm will do it,” he muttered. “-y’u! W’at y’u drivin’ at?” howled the foreman, failing to understand the danger signal. “You!” The New Yorker struck. A moment later his persecutor was lying on his back on the tow-path. Blood was flowing from his nose. The grimy crowd of workmen stood silent, gaping at the strange man from the big city who had actually had the audacity to strike a railroad gang foreman! Kingdon quickly left the amazed knot of laborers clustering about their fallen slave-driver. He made his way angrily back to the bunk-house. It was there, an hour later, that Elsware found him. “Git outen th’ camp, y’u-!” bellowed the walking boss. “Thar hain’t no money a-comin’t’ y’u! Git back t’ N’ Yawk, whar th’ likes o’ y’u belongs! Ef we counted 011 fellers like y’u t’ build this here railroad, we’d never git it done in a million years!” Elsware’s surly visage contracted into a glare of mingled ferocity and contempt. He towered above the New Yorker, moun- tainous and threatening. Kingdon knewN’ Yawk and Elsware 83 that even one clumsy hug from this big hulk of a man would have been almost as deadly as- that of a grizzly bear, but some- how the New Yorker was not afraid. “You great big ignoramus!” he roared desperately. “You don’t know one thing in this world but rough stuff! You might be king out in these jungles, and know it all—but if I only had you in New York—” Half an hour afterward Kingdon was striding swiftly along the trail toward the south. Between him and the end of the steel lay sixty miles of forest and granite hills and muskeg. From the end of the steel it was still seven hundred miles to New York. He hadn’t so much as a cent in his pocket. “Sent adrift into the wilderness,” he muttered grimly, “because I don’t know how to build a blasted railroad!” 1 j THE drowsy aftermath of carouse was written unmistakably over this man’s gigantic person. He was standing in the center of a throng—standing stock still, as if anchored to the pavement. The massive head had sagged down into the shirt collar. The great shoulders were canted sidewise like a listed ship. It seemed almost as if the giant were trying to person- ate the leaning tower of Pisa. The awe- struck spectators had ringed themselves in a wide circle about the unusual spectacle •—wide, because they evidently feared that at any moment the tower-like legs might give way. “Teched.” The monosyllable rumbled suddenly and sleepily out of the big man’s mouth like a distant growl from a sleeping volcano. A set of massive, calloused fingers began list- lessly and apparently half-consciously stray- ing in and out of his various pockets. “He’s been robbed,” came in hoarsely whispered accents of understanding from several of the bystanders. A vigilant policeman hove in sight, drawing the legal bludgeon from it^ leathern scabbard. An imperative flourish of this weapon opened a lane through the fast- collecting crowd. Expectantly the on- lookers looked from the center of attraction to the officer. He, however, scratched his head in pardonable perplexity. The job of salvaging such a huge derelict bade fair to be an unenviable one. Moreover, he was not exactly disturbing the peace. In fact he seemed, on the other hand, to be the injured party in some shady transaction. This officer, who liked an occasional tot himself, was loath to add to the big man’s misfortunes by making him a target of legal discipline. However, he was un- deniably obstructing traffic. Some sort of action therefore became immediately neces- sary. “What’s your name? Where do you live?” came out of the policeman in a friendly growl. “Teched,” grunted the stricken giant dismally. The policeman repeated the query, which elicited merely a dolorous repetition of the barbarous word. A strong liquory aura invested the victim’s person. “Huh! Charged up to the neck. Don’t even know your own name, eh?” sum- marized the Law disgustedly. “Don’t any- body know who this human elephant is?” He looked appealingly about the circle of grinning countenances—sailors, lumber- jacks, trappers, railroad laborers—because Craig Street at that time was a.sort of little Bowery where the rough-and-ready tribes were accustomed to disport themselves while in Montreal. It was shortly after eleven o’clock at night. Montreal’s curfew for the bibulous rings at that hour. Pre- cisely then the saloons’ doors are slammed and locked, the faucets are turned off and the disciples of Bacchus, with such cargo aboard as they have been able to acquire, find themselves in the cold and unin- spiring street. This was undoubtedly the reason for the tipsy big man’s appearance on Craig Street. Some saloon lounger had got his money—he had subsequently been thrust into the street. Too bad. The Law sighed. He would have the job of his life to haul such a monster to the nearest call box. Just at that moment, when the officer was about to take a grip on his hesitation and act in accordance with the letter of the law—a young man pushed his way smil- ingly forward. “Sh!” he whispered. “It’s Elsware, the big railroader. I know him. He’s con- siderably under the weather, eh?” Human nature is often a sheepy thing. The officer had never heard of Elsware, but the well-bred-looking young man’s cautious implication generated the idea that here was84 Adventure a “big” man of affairs whose foibles must occasionally be winked at. The policeman smiled knowingly. “I’ll take him home,” volunteered the stranger. “Wait till I call a cab.” “Cab?” grinned the officer skeptically. “Why not a truck?” Eventually, however, they stowed Els- ware’s ponderous frame away in a hack— the task being accomplished through the combined efforts of the Law, the affable young stranger and the hack driver. The curious crowd began disappointedly dwind- ling into the night. The officer, heaving a sigh of relief, sheathed his bludgeon and continued his interrupted saunter through Montreal’s Tenderloin. The young stranger climbed to the box and sat beside the driver; after which the hack proceeded east through Craig Street. Elsware sat behind in solitary and maudlin majesty, his chin drooping indolently upon his breast. “Teched,” he rumbled faintly. IN AND about the labyrinth of the great Grand Central Terminal; from lower level to upper level; from upper level back to lower level; into the subway’s mouth that growled constant subten-anean thunders; back through the interminable network of corridors and into the vast waiting-room—slowly, dazedly, dejectedly, a conspicuous figure of a man was making his way. He was a colossal specimen of manhood, standing easily six feet four inches in his heavy shoes. His eyes, how- ever, held such a look of dismay as accorded ill with his formidable mold. At intervals his hands fumbled nervously in his pockets as if loath to abandon a search for some- thing no longer there. His demeanor was so completely indicative of bewilderment that at last he was accosted by a lynx- eyed little man who had been observing him for some time. “Pardon me, have you lost something, sir?” The giant turned sad and anxious eyes upon the questioner. “Reckon I hev, young feller—reckon I’m all lost—me an’ ev’rythin’ I got.” “How’s that? Don’t you even know where you are?” “I dunno nuthin ’bout m’ self. Whar am I at?” “You’re in the Grand Central Station,” replied the plain-clothes man, beginning to suspect that he had located a case of am- nesia. “I knowed I wuz in a station, all right, cuz some fellers put me offen a train this mornin’,” was the naive reply. “But w’at d’ y’u call this here place?” “The name of the city, you mean? Why, New York, of course.” “N’ Yawk!” The words shot out of the big fellow’s mouth like the bang of a siege gun. A new terror leaped into his haggard eyes. “How fur’s it t’ Muntreeyawl?” he gasped. “Between four and five hundred miles.” Something like the last sigh of the dying Cyclops came out of the lost man’s mouth. “Who are you, and where do you come from?” persisted the detective. “I’m frum th’ North—name’s Ed Els- war’—I come t’ Muntreeyawl fer a couple o’ days’ good time. Yistiddy—leastaways I think it wuz yistiddy—I hed two hundred dollars wi’ me. As fur’s I kin mind w’at happened, some fellers teched me while I wuz a-tryin’ t’ git hum. I don’t mind nuthin’ arter thet till I got put offen th’ train this mornin’. An’ now here I be—-in N’ Yawk!” The plain clothes man smiled under- standingly. He had already lost interest in the case, which had turned out to be nothing more exciting than the common or Barleycorn variety of amnesia. “I guess you’d better get the air,” he said meaningly. “Jist w’at I’m a-tryin’ t’ do, mister,” groaned Elsware, “but durn me ef I kin git outen this tarnation place nohow!” The sleuth laughingly piloted the big man to the main entrance and at last saw him swallowed up in the swirl of traffic on Forty-second Street. “Some hick!” he muttered. Elsware breasted the human billows as far as Fifth Avenue, pausing in consterna- tion at the moving wall of vehicles that barred his further progress. The traffic policeman suddenly lifted his hand and, presto! A lane was miraculously provided through which the ponderous railroader nervously thrust his way. At Times Square he stopped in astonishment, gaping at great buildings that thrust themselves fearlessly skyward; looking in awe at the tens of thousands of people who streamed Heedlessly along in six directions; tremblingN’ Yawk and Elsware 85 at the frequent underground roarings that poured upward through the subway grat- ings beneath his feet. As he wandered aimlessly about the city he saw railroads in every direction. There were railroads through the thoroughfares, railroads in the air over his head and railroads under his feet. Elsware walked along the gayest prome- nade in the world without receiving a sign of recognition or a glance of friendliness from a single one of all the thousands that passed him by. Curious looks there were aplenty, drawn naturally in his direction by his unusual size. But alas! He en- countered never a welcoming grin or a cheery “Bon jour!” or a “Howdy!” When night at last overtook him—the weird, metropolitan night with its hundreds of thousands of lights stabbing the darkness —Elsware was sitting in Herald Square listening dejectedly to the thunder of the subways, watching with awed eyes the racing “L” express trains and marveling at the never-ceasing clang and hum of the trolleys. “Dang me,” he sighed, “ef they hain’t more railroads in this here N’ Yawk than they is in th’ hull dang Province of Quee- bec!” The rugged railroad pioneer felt like an ant with a sudden conception of the uni- verse. Out in his own wilderness he was king, but here, in this bedlam of sound and scene and motion, where they built up to the sky and down, for all he knew, to the infernal places; where all his northern im- portance and cocksureness seemed somehow to have been cast by the board—why, he was nothing more than a drifting hulk of a man headed irresistably for the rocks. Where would he sleep? His huge organ- ism was already clamoring for food. How could he eat? His brain was still hazy from debauch and practical ideas for his relief offered themselves very slowly. How could he get back to the job? He hadn’t even the price of a telegram to his em- ployers so that he migh ask for a loan of money. How did men go about these things when they had neither money nor friends and were adrift in the great wilder- ness of New York? Elsware groaned. At that moment a well-dressed young man, nonchalantly puf- fing a cigaret after the manner of his kind, seated himself upon the bench beside the despondent derelict. “Elsware,” he said evenly, “about four months ago you chased me out of your camp—without a nickel—because I didn’t know anything about building a railroad. How I ever got back to the steel is a night- mare—1" don’t remember half what I suf- fered on the way. At last, when I was nearly all in, I landed a job in the chief constructing engineer’s office at the end of the steel. Saved six hundred dollars in four months. So you see, Elsware, I know some- thing about building a railroad after all. “I was on my way to New York when I saw you last night on Craig Street. I’ll say you needed a friend. I took you away from a cop, finally got you to the station, hired a porter and the cabby to help me hoist you into the smoker, shoved a ticket into your hat-band and let you snooze. I’ve been tagging you around all day, en- joying myself.” “N’ Yawk!” exclaimed the unfortunate railroader, with a glance of piteous appeal from his haggard eyes. “Y’u be n’t a-goin( t’ git even wi’ me now, fer w’at I done t’ y’u—be y’u, N’ Yawk? Hain’t I got ’nough trouble now. N’ Yawk?” Kingdon laughed, throwing away his cigaret and slapping Elsware jovially upon the shoulder. “Why, you great big grampus. I’ve got nothing against you! The experience did me good. I learned lots that I never could have learned in college. But here’s the idea—I want you to get the other fellow’s point of view. I learned some things in your country, where men know how to buikl railroads and things with their hands; but now I’m going to show you around a town— and we’ll put some paint on it, too—where they teach people to build railroads and things with their heads”Author of “The Other Allingham,” “Gilford the Giftedetc. The first part of the story briefly retold in story form (")N THE banks of the Benin River in the Niger Delta were sprawled the buildings of Marsden & Co’s, trading-factory. Across the way stood the decrepit establishment of the only rival in the vicinity, which belonged to Bart Jardine and his daughter. A distance down-stream rotted what was left of a concern once known as Grandison’s, and it was to this latter beach that Charteris, a man with silvery hair and the face of a youth, came, appearing no one knew why or whence. At the same time Hugh Morrison, owner of Mars- den & Co., arrived in his yacht with his daughters and servants to look over his agency and investigate the circumstances surrounding a disturbing quarrel between two important native traders, Agwala and Abado. His visit had not lasted more than a few days when, one pitchy night, as he paced the deck of his boat, Mr. Morrison was done to death by a mysterious assailant, and immediately the neigh- borhood of whites and blacks was thrown into an uproar. Every effort was made to determine the murderer, and, during the proceedings of inquiry, suspicion fell upon every one from the puzzling stranger, Charteris, to Noni, Margaret Jardine’s negro maid, and next Abado—who had incontinently vanished during the confusion. 'THE examination continued, but little light was thrown on the situation. Gradually, however, there grew up a feeling that the gray man knew more than he cared to tell, and it increased when mysterious goings-on at Grandison’s beach were reported. Word was brought that Charteris was sheltering Abado there—a fact which naturally pointed to a double implication in the recent murder. Mr. Parker, the Government commissioner, and all those associated with Marsden & Co. concen- trated their efforts toward verifying the rumor and then apprehending Abado. They solicited the help of Agwala, who was only too pleased to comply. Charteris, however, having other plans in mind, managed to keep them all guessing. Thereupon Mr. Parker decided to use force as a last resort and set about preparations for an armed visit to Grandison’s. Meanwhile the trade at Marsden & Co. com- menced falling off. On the other hand, there was a sudden flurry of trade at Bart Jardine’s across the way. Why don’t you do something instead of just looking terribly wise?” Parker laughed. “Then we’ll have to move you down to Forcados. It’s cooler down there.” “You won’t do anything of the sort! You can move the yacht if you want to, but I’ll go and live at Sandy MacGregors’.” “Can’t do that. It’s against the law.” Catherine paused, searched Parker’s face for a hint of humor and found none. “You are not really serious about this, are you?” “Very.” “Immediately?” “In a day or so.” “Why?” “I think you would all be much better off at Forcados.” “You don’t mean that at all,” Catherine accused. “If we can stay at Forcados, we can stay here. Unless— What is it you are really afraid of?” “Simply that Benin River is a bit upset and may become a trifle more so.” “Fighting?” There was an eagerness in Catherine’s voice that, for a Sabbath evening, was sacri- legious. “No. I think not,” Parker prevaricated guiltily. Again Catherine hesitated, biting her lips reflectively. It was one thing to be defiant and quite another to make good her de- fiance. Parker waited a minute or so, asked if he might smoke, lighted a cigaret, and then allowed a Warri policeman to scuff past one of the library doors before he spoke again. And in the interval, Catherine’s mood changed. “I wish you would let me advise you in this,” Parker began in a friendly voice, and suddenly became uncomfortably aware of Catherine’s eyes. She smiled quietly and tolerantly, and he felt somewhat foolish. “Please don’t mince matters,” she said and her voice pleaded a little. “I’m not afraid of any kind of truth, provided it’s true. You’re just tired of us.” “No—I assure you—I have enjoyed-” “You have not,” .softly. “We cramp your freedom of action—get in your way— ask stupid questions—make you say dash when you want to say, ‘Damn’ and all that tommyrot. You’re not comfortable. And you’ve either made a terrible mistake in us or you’re simply and bluntly fed up.” Parker rose, crunched his cigaret in an ash receiver, and Catherine had no difficulty in observing that the operation was a vi- cious one. She calmly retained her seat, however, and while he fumbled about for something to say, she added with a gentle note of disappointment: “I shouldn’t blame you a bit if you would say so and be done with it. But I do most heartily object to a man pretending to be considering my welfare when he’s really thinking of his own.” Parker’s jaws snapped and bulged, and, for a moment or two, Catherine detected the man beneath. She liked that man, part- ly because she was afraid of him, but greatly because he did not attempt to apologize or argue or meet the situation above a con- versational level. While she clothed her anxiety in an assumption of gentle resignation, and did not for a moment allow Parker to escape the discomfiting attention of her eyes, he said simply— “In that case, there does not seem to be anything more to say.” “No, I suppose not. Except to decide our sailing date?” “You can discuss that with Captain Walton.” “Yes. But when?” “At your own discretion,” he said, and Catherine admitted that his self-control was worth watching. “However, I should advise you to get under way by the first of the month.” Catherine hastily consulted the small desk calendar. The first of the month was eleven days off. Much could happen in eleven days, including the possibility that she would be able to induce the Great Dane to change his mind again. “All right. Thanks. I’m sorry we’ve bothered you and—and I’ll try not to ask any more questions.” “That will help,” Parker said with all88 Adventure the agreeable effect of a straight right be- tween the eyes. “Please ask Malkett to come in—no, don’t bother, I’ll-” “It’s all right,” Catherine interposed sweetly. “I’m only too glad to help, even if it’s simply as an office girl.” “Oh,-!” Catherine halted with her fingers on the knob of the screen door. “Thank you,” she said gratefully. “That assures me that you think I’m almost grown up.” Then softly, “Good night, Commis- sioner.” Catherine went out leaving Parker to dis- color the atmosphere in the library as savagely as he pleased, and with a doubtful smile of triumph hugging the corners of her mouth, she searched for Malkett. Captain Walton and he had joined Hogmanay and Margaret, but Fran was standing beside the rail looking up-river. Catherine did not see her at first, but, hav- ing delivered the Great Dane’s message to Malkett, she looked about, then moved across the deck. Fran’s arm now lay along the rail and her forehead rested upon it. Apparently she did not hear Catherine’s step until she came very .near to her, then she lifted her head sharply and spun about in startled inquiry. There was also a haunting guilt in her eyes that Catherine did not see clearly enough to enable her to detect it. “What’s up?” Fran demanded impa- tiently. “Nothing,” quietly. “Got a headache?” “One of the buzzing kind,” Fran returned and suddenly decided to be pleasant about it. “Bees and bees and bees. It’ll be gone in the morning. What did Parker have to say. to you? Ponderous words of eternal devotion, I suppose, in the guise of official business?” “No,” sourly. “We’re to get out by the first.” “And he’s been making sneaky goo-goo eyes at you all day!” “Has he? I hadn’t noticed. Don’t you think you’d better toddle to bed?” And Catherine took a gentle but firm hold of Fran’s wrist. “Rot!” Fran snatched her wrist out of Catherine’s grasp with a sharp jerk. “You’re not going to make an interesting pathological study out of me. Go over there and take MacGregor’s temperature. If he had a clinical thermometer under his tongue this minute, the picture would be complete.” Catherine looked and smiled warmly. Fran laughed, and the sound struck a new note in her life, a note even Catherine did not suspect or understand. Also, Fran did not have a headache, nor, just then, was she giving a great deal of thought to her eyes. “Are you really going to leave on the first?” she asked and seemed a little anxious. “Not if I can help it.” “Then there’s nothing to worry about,” Fran declared confidently, and drew a long, silent breath of relief. NEXT morning, Catherine received another cable from her mother, and cable-like, it did not squander words. Therefore Catherine’s anxiety for the sort of action that was wholly visible and understandable increased feverishly with every hour. She met the Great Dane’s morning-after tendency to be pleasant with a saccharine humility that was somewhat thin and ragged in spots; and when Parker or Mal- kett were not looking, she became suddenly attentive to Arthur Garriman Bark. Obviously Bark did not like the questions she asked. He appeared to be afraid of them, and in the evening, when Catherine told him blandly that she expected Char- teris to visit the yacht “in an hour or so,” Arthur’s confusion became most manifest. Shortly after nine o’clock he disappeared. In the interval, Catherine held a long and persuasive conversation with Andy Gra- ham, the chief engineer. Andy said “No” and shook his head most of the time, but finally he nodded in a slow cautious way that did not commit him too much.The Gray Charteris 89 And shortly before ten o’clock Catherine announced that she was going over to Hogmanay’s to talk over and have him send the reply to her mother’s cable. Oddly enough, she did not ask Fran to accompany her, and Andy Graham was in charge of the Bonny’s launch when she reached the foot of the companion ladder. The launch started off in the direction of Marsden& Co.’s wharf; then, about half- way, swerved sharply in the pitch black dark and shot up-river. CHAPTER XVII ABDUCTION IN THE flickering light of a small kerosene lamp, Charteris squatted upon his little pile of blankets in his corner of Grandison’s erstwhile office, and smoked and talked sonorously to Abado, who paced up and down in impatient restlessness, hurling an occasional staccato curse at Agwala’s an- cestry. Abado’s state of mind was chaotic. Not because, for the time being, he was not perfectly safe; simply that Charteris put an awkward and galling restraint upon his impulse to do rather than to wait. In the bush round about were many eyes that watched every moment of the day and night. They belonged to Abado’s sons or his hirelings and at the first hint of danger, the Sobo slithered to cover by the simple process of vanishing into the bush. When the danger departed, he returned to the shelter of the gray man’s protection, which was as complete as it was beneficent. Neither the government nor Agwala seemed at all eager to interfere with him. Nevertheless, Abado was not happy, despite the promises Charteris was at that moment making; and when Ogulo suddenly projected himself into the room with the rather tiresome announcement — “Gov’- ment come”—-Abado whined like a child whose patience with authority is on the verge of exhaustion. “Go to back beach bush!” Charteris ordered sharply. “We go Dowadda when gov’ment go ’way.” Abado muttered under his breath, but obediently vanished like a wraith, and Ogulo followed him down the galley stairs. Charteris did not leave his corner. Quickly dropping his mosquito curtain, and composing himself as comfortably as possi- ble behind it, he gave every appearance of having gone off to sleep. His position furnished him the advantage of seeing without being seen and when foot- steps sounded on the stairs, he knew that Malkett or Parker, or whoever it was, had not come alone. And then Charteris smiled a wisp-like smile that almost became a laugh. “Anybuddy at home?” Andy Graham queried doubtfully from the doorway. Catherine was directly behind him look- ing inquiringly over his shoulder, and her expression was that of one who is trying to be just politely interested in waiting for the performance to begin. She was thankful, however, that she wore skirts. Otherwise, her tell-tale knees might have told a more nervous story. Charteris yawned audibly. “What’s that? Who’s there?” And then before any one could answer, he thrust back the mosquito curtains and looked out. “Oh, that you Graham? And Miss Morrison!” He scrambled to his feet, rubbing his eyes. “I must have dozed off. This is splendid of you. A most unexpected pleasure. If I had known it was coming off, I should cer- tainly have become possessed of a chair. May I, however, invite you to squat? Those blankets—but perhaps you would rather stand?” Catherine laughed at the queer look of horror on Andy Graham’s face. “No, I’ll squat,” she decided, and promptly became enthroned in Charteris’ corner. “But what are you going to do if I use up all of the blankets?” “Squat lower and look worshipful. What’s wrong, Graham? Where are you going?” “I’ll go and sit on the stairs,” Andy ad- vised, moving toward the door. “Ye’ll find me there if ye want me.” Charteris looked pointedly at Catherine as Andy went out, then seated himself on the floor. “He didn’t think of that all by himself, did he?” he asked mildly. “No—not altogether.” “Then this isn’t a social call?” “Ladies do not pay social calls after ten o’clock at night. Not even on the Benin River.”90 Adventure “Quite true. They don’t. Er—if I may smoke, there’s a tin of cigarets almost at your elbow. Will you be so good as to cb uck them over to me, please? Also the matches. But be careful you don’t cut yourself on that knife.” The Hausa dagger Etaji had unwillingly left behind him, was some distance from the cigarets. Catherine probably would not have noticed it if Charteris had not made known its existence. Whether he knew this or not, or whether he was simply taking a roundabout method to put a weapon into bter hand if she thought, even vaguely, that she needed the comforting assurance of a ready defense, Catherine did not know. Her color height- ened just a little, however, as she “chucked” the cigarets and matches at her host. Then she picked up the knife. “It’s an ugly thing,” she commented. “Horribly business like.” “It .wasn’t made for sharpening pencils,” Charteris admitted, contentedly exhaling cigaret smoke. “You may have it as a me- mento of this frankly improper call. I’m sure its former owner would be glad to think it was in your possession.” “Thanks. I think I’d like to have it. Who did you take it from? Some one who tried to use it properly?” Charteris glanced toward the door and beyond it into the fire-fly-dotted blackness toward the river. He did not seem to think that Catherine’s question required an answer. “What did you Come to see me about?” he asked suddenly, but Catherine was in no wise flustered by it. “Because I’ve become conviced that you know a great deal more about my father’s death than you pretend to,” she said with unfaltering directness. “I?” “Bark is afraid of you,” Catherine went on steadily, “and when Agwala came to the yacht the other day he told Mr. Parker, in my presence, that you were interfering, or likely to interfere with justice.” “Great Scott!” Catherine studied his astonishment, try- ing to find a flaw in it. Then, quietly, deliberately, measuring each word, she added— “He said, in short, that you were—shel- tering Abado.” Charteris threw his head back and laughed. Even his eyes joined in. Appar- ently the accusation was too funny for words. Then, when he found himself able to be serious again, he asked. “Sure you are not mixing me up with some one else?” Catherine had a sinking feeling. Even though she heard Andy Graham cough every now and then, she felt terribly alone— futile—circling about an impenetrable wall with a hair-pin for a battering-ram. “You are the ‘gray man’ aren’t you?” she 'asked, as if there might be some doubt about it. “I’m gray enough if that constitutes identification.” “Then Agwala meant you. Also, when Bark came Up here the other evening and reported you absent, I’m sure he knew you were not here or else he would not have come up.” Charteris smiled unbelievingly. “What on earth makes you think that?” “It isn’t my thought exactly. Fran— Miss Goddard, you know—put it into my head. Said she had had an ocular demon- stration of Bark’s fear of you. So to-day I watched him, quizzed him, frankly both- ered him about you and became convinced that Fran was right. The man actually jumped when I told him that I expected you to call this evening, and he at once left the Bonny for an unknown destination.” Again Charteris laughed. “That wasn’t quite fair. What, might I ask, did Parker say when Agwala accused me of throwing a monkey wrench into the law’s machinery.” “Oh, he just told him he was talking rubbish, or something to that effect. But I don’t altogether believe it. So I’m here.” “I see,” quietly and flipping his cigaret through the open door. “Frankly, I did not expect to see you occupying that corner. I appreciate the honor keenly. Graham a trusted confidant of yours?” Catherine’s heightened color was per- ceptible this time. She glanced quickly down at the knife in her lap, gingerly fin- gered the edge of it, then put it aside with an involuntary shudder. “I’ve known Andy for years. And I’ve never known him to betray a confidence. Dad trusted him implicitly, and Dad knew men.” Charteris nodded.The Gray Charteris 91 “No one other than Graham knows you are here tonight?” Catherine looked just a little startled. “Why do you ask that?” she questioned cautiously, and felt, for the first time, that she was farther away from the yacht than she had any right to be. Charteris picked up the cigaret tin and the matches and rose. His movements were leisurely but deliberate. “Then you’d better get back before any one finds it out,” he said simply. “Be- cause, of course, there is really nothing I can do for you.” Catherine stared. Her cheeks flamed. She felt like a very little girl who was being sent home because her conduct had not been good. Her eyes lighted up angrily. “I’m not a child!” she began, not at all aware of the impulsiveness of her speech. “And I didn’t go to all the bother of induc- ing Andy Graham to bring me up here sim- ply to go away again without coming to some sort of understanding about your po- sition in this terrible affair. Malkett, of the police, agrees with Mr. Parker that Agwala was talking nonsense. But I don’t.” “No?” “No, I don’t. The government officials— and Sandy MacGregor, too, for that matter—know too much about this country to believe that a white man would shield a black one in a case of this sort. I don’t know anything about it, so I am not allow- ing prejudice to interfere with my ideas about things.” “There is something in that,” Charteris admitted pleasantly. “And I’m afraid I shall have to talk to Agwala in plain language for trying to ruin my spotless reputation. But, before you further im- peril yours, I shall have to send you home. You’re altogether too desirable to be wan- defing around in the dark like this.” Catherine’s expression declared that she did not know whether to be angry or sur- prized or suspicious or all three at once. So she compromised by laughing softly and rising lightly to her feet. “That’s just make believe,” she decided. “You want to get rid of me and are trying to scare me off. I didn’t come here alone.” “No. But there is a back door as well as a front door to this establishment.” Catherine paused. The stillness that gripped the place had a smothering effect. There was a queer hint of stifled laughter in the air. She loitered a few steps nearer to the door. “I wish you would be frank with me,” she said simply, as though his veiled threats were not worthy of any consideration. “I have been brutally frank with you because I think you can help me to—to discover the man who did it. And I won’t leave the country till I do. I had another cable from mother this morning, too.” “What does Miss Goddard think about it?” “Fran? Oh, she—” Catherine stopped. “Why is that important? You know per- fectly well you irritate her simply because she can’t hurt you.” Charteris glanced along the veranda to where the shadowy outline of Andy Graham was wrapped in a furious cloud of pipe smoke. Then he moved out of the doorway into the room again, giving Catherine an opportunity to run if she wanted to. “You forgot the knife. Shall I get it for you, or would you prefer to get it your- self?” Nothing could have been plainer to Cath- erine than that. The way to the door was clear, and he gave her permission to possess herself of the ugly Hausa dagger to make sure that the way remained clear. Her color drifted and her mouth became a severe line. There was something of Hugh Morri- son in her face then. She walked suddenly toward Charteris and there was not the slightest sign of fear in her eyes. When she halted he could have touched her with his outstretched hand. “I’ve come up here because I believed— and still believe—that you really know more about the inner workings of native intrigue than Malkett and MacGregor and Parker and all the rest of them put together.” “Thank you.” “And I thought you would be man enough to help a white woman when she needed help very badly. You see, I don’t believe in playing with words or facts, nor have I any intention of getting down on my knees.” “No. Don’t do that.” “And I’m not going to be frightened or driven out of the river one minute sooner than I want to go.” “I hope not. I like to see the Bonny lying out in midstream when the sun comes up. Reminds me of lots of things—includ- ing cream puffs and white icing cakes.”92 Adventure His hands reached out quickly and caught Catherine’s wrists. She gasped, drew back a step, stopped because she had to—but did not scream. Charteris knew she was trem- bling, but her eyes did not show it. The sound of Andy Graham coughing had no effect upon the situation except to relieve the outer darkness a trifle. A moment later it was blacker and more silent than ever. Catherine asked with just the suggestion of a sneer— “This is my cue to yell, I suppose?” “Not unless you want to. Frankly, I don’t believe you do want to.” His hands slipped up her arms to her elbows and Catherine pulled her head back sharply. “Don’t! This is just cheap dramatics. Or more trifling. Please let me go.” Charteris’ shoulders ceased to lean against the wall. He drew her a little nearer. “You know, there’s a whole lot to be said in favor of taking advantage of one’s oppor- tunities. And if you refuse to use the voice the Lord gave you, what’s a mere man to think? You aren’t even restless!” Catherine tried to draw away from him. “Rubbish!” she exclaimed disparagingly, but her voice sounded weak and uncon- vincing. “This is all bosh! Please let me go. Don’t!” Charteris’ hands had reached-her shoul- ders in a twinkling and she found, in another moment, that she was being held very firmly in his arms, his face a scant few inches above her own. Yet she did not scream. She was, in -point of fact, afraid and ashamed to admit to Andy Graham that her secret adventure had been a mis- take. “You’re hurting me,” she said quietly, trying with all her might not to look frightened while she shrank from the queer, cold laughing blue of his eyes. “I’m sorry,” softly. “But I understand that even the nicest ladies like to be knocked about a bit. In fact, I think they prefer to be carted off bodily.” His left hand curled about her neck and slipped over her mouth without a second’s warning, stifling her involuntary cry of fear so that it was nothing more than a choking gurgle in her throat. Before she fully realized that it was possi- ble that such a thing could happen to her, Charteris was half way down the rickety galley stairs and she was being “carted off bodily” as if she were no more trouble than an infant. The arms that held her captive were sure and strong and without mercy. The hand that was clapped across her mouth was by no means gentle. She could not use even her teeth to bite her voice’s way to free- dom. So that the experience hurt as well as outraged, and, going down the galley stairs, Catherine had a moment 01 two in which to realize her predicament, because it was dangerous to struggle then. At the foot, however, she leaped in Char- teris’ arms like a wild thing; leaped and kicked and tried to tear her head out of the clamping grip of his left hand. The result was simply a tightening of that grip—a crushing sense of helplessness—a groping in the dark of unbelief as she gathered her- self together for another strenuous bid for freedom. She could not see very well where they were going, but presently she heard Char- teris say softly: “This is really exciting. Wonder what poor old Graham will say when he finds it out. Hope he doesn’t take it too much to he&rt and shoot himself or something like that.” Again and more furiously this time, Cath- erine twisted and lunged and tried to tear his hand from her mouth. Then, she struck at him, but her blows fell short and broke their strength upon his shoulder, hurting her knuckles more than they did his shoul- der-blade. Presently, out of the storm, came Char- teris’ voice smoothly: “A perfect conclusion to a wonderful evening. What a pity it isn’t real!” And then he suddenly stood her upon her feet, saw that she preserved her equilibrium, and stood off a pace blandly watching her amazement. She was standing on the gig-wharf. CHAPTER XVIII THE BULGE IN THE SCREEN “T TOLD you there was a back door,” Charteris said pleasantly. Then after a short and heavy pause. “Shall I call Graham, or would you prefer to make your own explanations?” Catherine leaned against the wharf railThe Gray Charteris 93 in absolute silence. Her mouth hurt, her breath came hardly and she was trembling in the grip of a reaction that was almost as terrifying as the imprisonment of Charteris’s arms. Speech was beyond her, but she felt so inconsequent and so utterly foolish that nothing at all mattered very much. Had Charteris laughed at her, perhaps she would have liked it better. But he was so irritat- ingly pleasant and sympathetic that even the incessant hum of mosquitoes about her ears did not disturb her. She looked down into the Bonny’s launch that was tied up to the wharf, smothered a somewhat hysterical sob of anger that was mixed with an odd kind of disappoint- ment, then, with a tremendous effort, her aching body straightened and she said in a voice that, though it hesitated a little for want of breath, was almost normal— “I suppose that was your way of proving to me that it isn’t wise to be too sure of any- thing or any one?” “Perhaps. But I’ll admit you were a violent burden. Elsie—that’s your sister’s name, isn’t it?—would be lighter and more tractable than you, I imagine?” Catherine swallowed once or twice and then decided to ignore his reference to Elsie. “It was also your way of saying definitely that you won’t take sides with me? That you would rather I’d go away and not bother you?” “Good Lord, no! I’m glad you came and I hope you’ll come back some evening and stay longer.” And though Catherine gave vent to an exclamation of impatient unbelief, he actu- ally meant that. Then, in quiet warning, he added: “Better not lean too heavily on that rail. It may collapse.” Catherine left the rail without a sound, looked down into the launch again and stepped on board, denying herself the prof- fer of any assistance. “Please ask Andy to come down,” she said in a low, toneless voice. “Certainly. What shall I say to him?” “Nothing. I’ll tell my own lies.” Charteris excused himself politely, then walked up the path leaving her to her own somber reflections. Her mouth and her throat were dry and, in a moment or two, whether she would or not, angry tears sprang into her eyes. She jabbed at them with a handkerchief, clenched her hands and bit upon her already aching lips with astonishing intensity for a woman of her accustomed poise and calm. It was not often that Catherine Morrison had occasion to feel cheap and, in these few minutes, she did not bless the impulse that had encouraged her to trifle so carelessly with edged tools. Her anger, however, was not directed against Charteris. Repeatedly and without stint, shrinking from the shad- ows of canoes that whispered and crooned as they passed her by, she called herself a fool. The night that had wrapped her in a cloak on the way up, was now pitted with eyes that peered and laughed. Not because she had come there and had been ridiculed for her pains, but because she was going away again so unimportantly and so pain- fully little desired. Therefore Catherine writhed and con- tinued to denounce herself as an impression- able idiot, who was so mentally unbalanced as to become maudlinly sentimental over a man who was little better than a tramp. Presently Charteris appeared again, chat- ting most unconcernedly with Andy Gra- ham who was obviously suspicious of the Hausa dagger Charteris carried so openly in his hand. “You forgot this,” Charteris said to Catherine, bending over to put the weapon carefully into her grasp. “Oh—thanks,” she acknowledged weakly when she realized what it was. “I—I’m sure Elsie will be glad to add it to her col- lection.” Later, when the silent Andy Graham had steered the Bonny’s launch away from the little wharf and was heading thankfully down-stream, Catherine could almost have enjoyed cutting her tongue out for that final remark. It sounded so small and weak afterward. Charteris, however, did not stop to think about it. He hurried to the bush at the back of the beach. Passing the galley stairs, he whispered to Ogulo the dwarf to go up and blow out the lamp, then went on to join Abado who was fuming impatiently at the entrance to the narrow bush path that led to the village of Dowadda. “Wha’s matter?” the Sobo complained in a disgruntled voice. “I wait long time. What gov’ment man do?”94 Adventure “Shut up,” Charteris ordered in an impo- lite whisper, and then added in belittling pidgin: “Abado talk all same mosquito. Mm — Mm — Mm — Mm — Mm — all time. Soon Agwala shut him hands—so—” Charteris brought the palms of his hands sharply together to illustrate—“and Abado shut up plenty too quick. Sof’ly, sof’ly. Wan!” Without any further talk he dived into the bush path and thereafter brushed his way through the eery, bat- and moth- haunted darkness with no more concern than he would have shown in the Strand. He had evidently traveled the path often enough to be able to dispense with a lan- tern. Abado followed in silence; the sulky quiet of a disciplined child. He did not understand the queer gray man’s queerer methods and did not pretend to. And now that his rage against Agwala had roused him completely from his suicidal lethargy, he was beginning to think for himself and to hunger for action of his own devising. As in the case of the murder of Morrison, that action would be subtle only in the soundlessness of its execution; simply a question of striking swiftly in the dark be- fore his enemy had any opportunity to do likewise to him. And, in truth, Abado was frankly out for blood. Every nerve and sinew of him cried out for the kind of vengeance that his hands could feel and his eyes could gloat over. There was nothing fine about his vision. His mercy would be the mercy of a wounded bush cat, and if it were possible that he could see Agwala’s fat body die slowly, inch by inch, or pound by pound, he would con- sider that all of life had been well worth the trouble just for the reward of that single evening. The village of Dowadda did not promise anything to him. It’s chief was a small oil- trader named Roku, who, from Abado’s viewpoint, would sell himself to the highest bidder. Why Charteris put faith in men whose canoes and wives were few, Abado could not understand. It would not have startled the Sobo if, in the interval since the sun had gone down, the village of Dowadda had become a death trap, bought and paid for by Agwala. Suddenly Abado heard the light scuff of Cgulo’s feet behind him, but, save for a half-turn of the head, he ignored the dwarf altogether. Keeping his eyes fixed upon the middle of the gray man’s back, he plodded on in an unenthusiastic jog-trot that expressed his mental attitude more eloquently than speech. The night voices of the bush rustled and whimpered and coughed and shrilled. And though it was not at all impossible that among the unseen, friendly shadows of near-naked men who saw them go past, there might be others who would risk their lives to serve Agwala, Charteris did not change his gait or hesitate even for a mo- ment. Roku, the chief of Dowadda, met them just as they emerged from the path and headed for the village. Evidently Char- teris’ visit had been prearranged. Roku was a stout, but well-conditioned Jakri 'who had migrated from the Warri district some years before; which was one reason why Abado did not trust him. Jakri men from Warri were, in Abado’s opinion, as smooth and treacherous as their women were expensive. “Gov’ment come,” Roku informed Char- teris in a mumbling voice. “Mas’ Malkett. He go ’way. I t’ink so be all ri’. Agwala man come talk oil palaver. Five puncheon., I laugh inside my head.” Then, taking belated notice of Abado, he said “Doh” as a matter of course, but other- wise exhibited neither surprize nor fear, nor any emotion save a desire to serve the gray man far beyond Agwala’s proffered bribe of five puncheons of oil. As he turned toward the village, Char- teris laid the tips of his fingers retainingly on his arm. “Wait. Agwala live Benin River all time. Little time pass, gray man go ’way. Savez?” He kept his fingers on Roku’s arm, but v the Jakri did not tremble. He laughed, and his laughter was so spontaneous that even Ogulo, who hung in the background, thought the sound mocked Agwala too loudly. Then Roku said in Jakri: “Agwala is great. He is the voice of many. But I am Roku, Chief of Dowadda. I am my own tongue and the gray man is my friend.” Abado sneered silently, and Charteris paused a moment or two. “All right. We go,” he said at last, and allowed Roku to lead the way into the smallThe Gray Charteris 95 mud-built village, which, being the home of a Jakri of Warri, was cleaner, less evil smelling and less of a hodge-podge enter- prise than the majority of the villages in the Benin country. Just then it was in complete darkness, and save for the spasmodic wailing of a wakeful infant or two, it was also sound asleep.	\ Roku was wholly unattended, and the stark and sprawling forms of those who chose to sleep upon mats outside their huts stirred but slightly as the little company made its cautious way to the door of Roku’s mud-and-thatch establishment. It was, of course, a larger hut than any of the others, situated at the head of a semi- circular lane that formed one half of a dou- ble circle, the compound being in the center. They went no farther than the first room of Roku’s house, however, and Ogulo, be- cause of the curse that was upon him, did not go even as far as that. He composed himself in the dust outside the door while Roku seated his guests upon trading-beach camp chairs around a roughly made table constructed of unplaned deal boards. Roku liked to give the impression that he lived like a gentleman, white man fashion. In the dark and silent rooms that lay beyond a screen of fiber mats, the mysteries of Roku’s household, in spite of trading- beach furnishings, were as rampant as ever. A white man might go through the rooms from the “hall of the gods” to the burial chamber and look those mysteries full in the face and be entirely unaware of their mean- ing and even of their presence. Some of them would remain unsolved even at Roku’s death, because if his gods were good and no ill of the flesh or spirit befell him, he would die and be buried in that house. ' For such was the honor due to a chief such as he had every intention of being—with the gray man’s beneficent assistance. And to prove that he was worthy, he lighted a small kerosene lamp, dropped the mat screens over door and window, and from a hole in the mud wall that was con- cealed behind another colorful mat, he pro- duced a bottle of perfectly good Scotch and another of English gin. Glasses followed, and for the honor of Roku’s house, Charteris drank two fingers of Scotch. Abado with a surly grunt took gin. Roku, himself, took a little of both and immediately, according to his own ver- sion of it, became a blood brother to the Sobo and the thumb-nail on the gray man’s right hand. Charteris smiled, glanced from one to the other of his companions, and said simply— “Maybe so, I want eight-paddle canoe?” Roku nodded. That was easy enough. “I think I go Agwalla-town—now—one time.” The whites of Roku’s eyes showed sharply for a second, then vanished. “Cha/” The exclamation was Abado’s and he leaped to his feet, glaring wildly about him. Roku, having composed his features again, merely nodded gravely and did not appar- ently observe the big Sobo’s excitement. Charteris gave no heed to either of them, He was casually studying a nervous- appearing bulge in the fiber mat screen which separated the room from the others. This screen was divided into two equal parts, the one overlapping the other, and the bulge might have been caused by a slight breeze. It began, however, at a spot that was about the height of a man’s shoulder. “Squat!” he ordered Abado quietly, and the Sobo sat down again as if his camp chair were charged with electricity. Then, before he had an opportunity to feel half-way comfortable, Charteris declared— “You go back to Materri, sof’ly, sof’ly.” Again Abado stood upright expressing more amazement than before. Materri was his own village and he knew it would be the height of folly to go there. Roku smiled and shrugged his glistening shoulders and the bulge in the fiber mat screen shifted. Charteris leaned across the table just a little and began trifling with the bottle of Scotch. “You fit to give him canoe?” he asked Roku, as if Abado’s protests were of no con- sequence. Roku admitted that he could provide Abado also with a canoe. “All right. Two canoe, one time. We talk Doridi palaver, tomorrow time.” Roku nodded. He knew Doridi was the confidant of Agwala who had generously told Noni and Abado so much about the Morrison-Agwala interview at Forcados. No one had seen or heard much about him since that time. When the chief of Dowadda departed to do the gray man’s bidding, Abado’s protests96 Adventure broke out anew in a jabbering crescendo. He came and stood beside Charteris’ chair in an effort to impress him with the sin- cerity and wisdom of his contentions. If he went back to Materri, he argued, it would be equal to walking into the govern- ment barracks at Warri or Sapeli. The village of Materri was surely being watched night and day, both by the government and by Agwala, and Abado thought it would be more simple to cut his own throat then and there than to do anything so foolhardy as to paddle his way into Agwala’s hands like that. Charteris did not attempt to suppress him. Instead he allowed him to go on talk- ing, and, rising from his chair, argued convincingly with him, too, all the while twining his fingers about the neck of the bottle of Scotch. The bulge in the mat screen leaped for- ward suddenly. Abado’s back was toward it. One mo- ment he felt the grip of Charteris’ powerful left hand upon his arm; the next he was spinning across the room to the startling accompaniment of the crash of breaking glass, a sudden darkness that swirled and smothered and the sound of a revolver shot that made his ear-drums ring. Then, out of the dark there came the unmistakable threshing of struggling bodies, a sharp squeal of pain that died in an un- canny grunt, and finally a low, feeble moan that chilled. Abado, who was not unused to fights in the dark, crouched against the farthest wall and shuddered. CHAPTER XIX A PARTIAL EXPLANATION OGUL0 was many yards from the door by that time. Roku’s household was awake, rubbing its eyes. The lifeless figures who slept outside their huts were no longer lifeless, and Roku himself, down at the edge of Dowadda Creek, making ready the canoes at the gray man’s bidding, turned his head sharply at the sound of the shot and prompt- ly became the color of dirty ashes. Muttering whispers that grew louder and louder passed up and down the village from end to end and back again. The jabbering of the women and the staccato grunting of the men, the frightened cries of many chil- dren and the bleating and stampeding of equally frightened goats and sheep, the scurrying of excited naked feet and the querulous inquiries of the very old—these demonstrations rose and fell in waves and died down again like the sucking rumble of receding waters. Roku did not stir. Neither did he inquire. He was afraid he knew; so afraid that he cursed the white man’s government and, still more fluently cursed Agwala. His dream of greatness burst like a bubble in a moment; and, if that were so, there was nothing to be gained by flight. Abado still cowered against the wall in Roku’s hut. Once, he heard a sound like the rattle of a belt buckle, but it meant nothing to him. When he finally did move, it was because he felt the approach of Char- teris rather than saw or heard him. Why he knew it was the gray man, he could not have told, because, while there was a tu- mult outside, within that room there was a silence that made breathing as difficult as it seemed thunderous. Then Charteris was at his elbow whisper- ing in his ear. Abado was frightened. There was no doubt about that. But that which Char- teris whispered made a slow, evil grin ex- pand from ear to ear. He obeyed the touch of the gray man’s hand without so much as an instant’s hesitation, drew his overcloth up over his head to conceal his face, and followed Charteris out into the welcome open, still grinning. A gasp of astonishment and fear went up when they made their appearance. It died into a whispering awe as they proceeded to the waterfront where Roku waited. There was something unreal in the manner in which every mouth was stopped and ev- ery arm hung in listless inaction as they passed by. Charteris ignored Roku and gave all of his attention to Abado. He saw to it that the Sobo entered the small canoe provided for him, and when Abado again whimper- ingly protested his return to Materri, Char- teris cursed him roundly in his own jargon and told him impatiently to be off. Like a child venturing into the dark alone, Abado paddled slowly and reluc- tantly up stream. So reluctantly at first, that Charteris picked up a handy piece of mangrove stick and hurled it after him with such good aim that Abado uttered a fright- ened cry and paddled furiously out of range.The Gray Charteris 97 His grin, however, was broader and more evil than ever. Then Charteris turned to Roku and said simply: “Come. We go.” And he led the way back to Roku’s hut. The Jakri followed quaking. He had not failed to observe that there was a revolver sticking in the gray man’s belt—a revolver that had not been there before. And he knew to whom the weapon be- longed. Fight, under the circumstances was even more useless than flight. Roku never even^ thought of it. His skin was still whole and he passionately wished it to remain so as long as possible. “Bring lamp,” Charteris ordered when they reached the door of the hut again. “Tell all man go sleep. No make talk. Sof’ly, sof’ly.” Roku gulped. Quaveringly he issued an order to his excited villagers to cease their chattering and resume their disturbed slum- bers, then scurried nervously in search of another lamp. His dignity, for the moment at least, was gone. He might recover it again if the gray man did not put an everlasting blight upon him. But until he was sure about this, he would feel like a paddle boy whose back was raw after a flogging. Charteris entered the hut and went direct- ly in the direction of the right hand corner of the first room. Some one was wriggling like an eel in that corner and giving vent to an occasional moan of pain or fear. When Charteris told the wriggler to save his energies, quiet fell instantly. Presently Roku returned with a lamp, placed it on the table and then looked fur- tively at the trussed figure of Arthur Garriman Bark. Roku seemed afraid to assimilate too much of the truth all at once. And, unquestionably, Arthur was a pa- thetic spectacle. His arms were strapped behind him with his service belt, and his ankles were bound with the belt that ordi- narily supported his trousers. He was dirty and disheveled and his head ached from the effect of the quieting crack Charteris had given him with the butt of the revolver. His morale, therefore, was at a very low ebb. Charteris had the revolver, Abado had escaped and Malkett did not know he 7 was there. Many things could happen to him long before Malkett found it out. “Sorry I missed you when you called at Grandison’s the other evening,” Charteris said blandly. “Would have saved all this trouble if I hadn’t. Was Malkett foolish enough to post you in there?” Arthur did not answer. He was afraid to lie and more afraid to tell the truth, and he was not at all sure that Roku was well enough paid to go on lying indefinitely. So he kept his peace. “Why were you so confoundedly anxious to put a bullet into Abado that you couldn’t wait until I was out of the way?” Arthur did not answer that either, but his expression at once betrayed the fact that Charteris’s surmise was unfailingly correct. “Remember what I told you on the Bonny the other day?” Arthur remembered, and looked as if he would like to forget. “And yet you took a cold-blooded pot shot like that at him.” A thoughtful pause. “I suppose that was why you visited Grandison’s in my absence. Thought you might spot him and do the trick without having to take so desperate a chance as this? You used to be such a nice voung man, too. What is it? Agwala or wonfen or both?” Bark writhed. He hoped Roku did not understand much of what Charteris was saying. “As I see it,” Charteris informed him gratuitously, “you were going to take your man, as dead as you could make him, and turn him over to Malkett as a good servant of the Crown should. In other words— Come here!” The command was addressed to Roku, who was edging toward the bottle of gin. All he wanted, however, was a drink to steady his trembling legs, but Charteris ordered him to stand against jthe opposite wall and await his pleasure. Then he turned to Bark again. “In other words,” he went on as if no interruption had occurred, “you were will- ing to take that chance with me in the room, and if a careless shot had gone wild-” “No!” The negative was as hoarse as it was desperate. “My chief gave me orders to secure Abado, dead or alive-” “And Agwala prefers him dead.” “I know nothing of Chief Agwala’s desires.” “You were so nervous about it, too, you98 Adventure could not keep your shoulder out of the picture.” Arthur looked down his nose because there was nowhere else to look with any comfort and he was visibly relieved when Charteris switched*Tiis attention to Roku, whose knees sagged the moment the gray man turned in his direction. Outside, quiet had fallen again. Prob- ably there were whispering speculations being indulged in within the darkened walls of every hut in Dowadda. But they were only whisperings and did not reach Roku’s ears. Even Ogulo the dwarf had returned and resumed his place jn the dust outside the door. Charteris spoke in a low voice in Roku’s own tongue: “Speak truth, small snake. Whose oil paid for—this?” He touched Bark contemptuously with the toe of his boot. Roku looked all about him in a shivering terror, and his fear of the gray man was not merely physical. Several times he began to speak and each time the words died in his throat. Finally, being between the devil and the deep sea—almost as fearful of Agwala’s far-reaching vengeance as he was of the powers of darkness attributed to the gray man—he flung himself in the stereotyped native fashion at Charteris’ feet, mumbling the equally hackneyed admission that he was a poor Jakri man whose village was small, whose wives and goats and puncheons were few and—— Charteris put his right foot down hard on the groveling neck and his left dug sharply into Roku’s ribs. “Get up!”	_ / A squeak of pain answered him and the Jakri threw himself bodily out of reach of further punishment. He huddled on the floor about a yard away from Bark and quakingly awaited the gray man’s next move. “All right,” Charteris said as if he were perfectly satisfied. “We go Agwala-town. Wahl” Instantly Roku began to whine in real earnest, while Bark, staring up at Charteris in petrified unbelief, made queer sounds in his throat as if he were choking for breath. Neither of them seemed to be at all anxious to visit Agwala-town under these conditions. “I won’t go!” Arthur sputtered at last. “It’s preposterous! I Stfn a servant of His Majesty^ __ I wear his uniform. Apparently Charteris understood Ogulo’s hesitation. “The house of Roku is the house of a very small lizard,” he said simply. “Smaller even than you. Come.” Ogulo obeyed this time,, but gingerly. And when Roku saw Ogmo-of-the-long- arms put his foot across the threshold, he immediately set up a wail that threatened to rouse the house and the village again. Whereupon Charteris gave Roku most explicit instructions as to his conduct on the way down to the creek side, and indi- cated, with a prod of Bark’s revolver muz- zle, what was likely to transpire, first and foremost, if there was any trouble of his making. But I didn’t anticipate quite so much company.” CHAPTER XX THE POPULARITY OE AGWALA Hogmanay sandy MacGregor had not stuck a revolver in his belt since Chief Motu of Segwanga had made his justly famous “last stand.” This had been several years before.
