Even Jardine, over there, has done nearly as much business as I have in the past two ’r three days!” Catherine regarded Hogmanay’s unusual indignation with a slight smile. “You don’t mind that very much, do you?” “Mind! And why not?” Then observ- ing Catherine’s scepticism more closely. “Oh, ye mean Margaret?” He shook his head slowly. “That’s neither here nor there. But since ye’ve brought up the sub- ject, can I ask you a favor?” “Surely.” “Will ye try to kidnap her or something and take her wi’ ye when you go home?” Catherine laughed. “Fran suggested that this morning,” she said simply. “And I hope we can persuade her to come.” “That’s fine.” Hogmanay paused a moment or two, then got back to business with an abruptness that jarred. Apparently he was in no mood to let sentiment interfere with his responsi- bility to the firm. “Agwala is not livin’ up to the promises he made your father at Forcados,” he rum- bled. “When I sent for him to come up here tonight, he put me off wi’ an excuse. So, I’m goin’ to him.” “And where do I come in?” Catherine asked expectantly. “Well, ye see, you’re a Morrison,” Mac- Gregor ansveered with solemn conviction. “And Agwala knows the difference between a member o’ the family and a servant. I’m just a servant. You will be something he never expected to happen. And I’ll warrant, if we land in on him at this hour, he’ll be a whole lot more afraid o’ you than you will be of him. Are ye sure ye want to go, now?” “Of course! I wouldn’t miss it for worlds. And I’m just crazy to do something to help.” “Aye. I thought ye would be. It’ll be a sort o’ business meetin’, with you as chair- man o’ the board. I’ll do the talkin’ and you’ll nod like a judge and make him as un- happy as ye can by sayin’ as little as possi- ble. The Charteris man didn’t happen to mention trade, did he?” Catherine’s smile faded with a sharpness that was startling. “Nothing. I told him what Agwala said about the shielding of Abado, and he laughed at me.” “Um—well, ye shouldn’t have told him that,” MacGregor said cannily, as the back beach watch boy solemnly struck six bells on the old ship’s bell that hung inside the oil yard fence. “But it’s no matter. He probably knew it already. That watch boy’s a bit slow. It’s nearer twelve than eleven. Just a minute till I call Garland.” “Is he coming, too?” “Aye. I’m thinkin’ he’d better. He’s a handy man to have about ye in a native village.” “Gracious! That sounds exciting!” “No-—just a bit o’ caution in the matter of where to put yodr feet,” Hogmanay said, halting a moment in the doorway. “Native villages are not overparticular under foot, and ye wouldn’t be fit to live with if you were laid up with a sprained ankle now.” Catherine laughed. She was tingling all over in the hope that the visit to Agwala- town was not going to be as mild as Hog- manav promised. MacGregor went along102 Adventure to Garland’s room praying that he was not making a mistake in taking her there. Garland was waiting for him. “All right,” the shop clerk announced. “I’m full of artillery. Can you see any of it?” Hogmanay looked him over critically. “Just a suspicious looking lump under your left arm. Pull it for’ard a bit and it’ll be all right.” Garland’s hand disappeared under his shirt and pulled the “lump” forward a little. “Fine,” Hogmanay approved. “But I’m hopin’ we don’t have to murder any- body. Come along and take Miss Morrison down to the launch while I get ready.” In another minute Hogmanay was ex- plaining to Catherine that he had one or two little things to attend to and that if she would go to the launch and advise Andy Graham where they were going, he would join her in a jiffy. Catherine accepted Garland’s escort with a smile that put him immediately at his ease. The moment they were gone, Hog- manay promptly retrieved the revolvers from his desk. He loaded them carefully, and, like Gar- land, slung one under each armpit inside his shirt. It was a trick he had learned from a poetic gentleman, known from Sierra Leone to Duke Town, Calabar, by the vio- lent pseudonym of “Knife-Edge” Harring- ton; the gentleman, by the way, who was responsible for the aforementioned last stand of Chief Motu of Segwanga. Hogmanay recalled the affair and the man with a reminiscent grin, then, making sure that there was nothing but the measure of his own speed to hinder the “draw”, he solemnly gave instructions to a sleepy steward not to blow out the lamp, and went slowly and meditatively down stairs. There was not the smallest danger to Catherine in the little midnight adventure. Agwala-town, under MacGregor’s guidance, would be safer than West Kensington. Not because Hogmanay’s mighty right arm was a source of terror; simply that he was a trader. A uniform has frequently been a man’s death warrant in the Delta; while a gray flannel shirt and a pair of oil-smeared white drills have gone blandly through many a seething caldron of rebellion. And MacGregor believed that this was still the case in spite of the murder of Hugh Morrison. Morrison had died because, blinded by the strong sunlight on the heights, he had thought himself immune from the penalty of taking sides in a black man’s quarrel. So that Hogmanay, with two revolvers under his shirt, was not worrying to any extent about what would happen to Cath- erine. He was troubled, however, because of the demonstration he would probably have to make to induce Agwala to keep his promises. And he knew that the ponderous chief of Agwala-town was desperately afraid of guns. ELSIE and Fran Goddard lay in mosquito-curtained camp beds on J the Bonny's after deck, partially surrounded by canvas screens and entirely free from the annoyance of mosquitoes and sandflies. It was much more pleasant to lie there than to sit on deck under the light and be eaten alive. And simply because one went to bed, it was not necessary to sleep. “Where in the world could she have gone?” Elsie asked for the fiftieth time as the Bonny's bosun struck seven bells. “It’s hours and hours since Garland was here.” “Oh, she’s probably listening to Mac playing the fiddle,” Tommy Dillingworth said assuringly from the other side of the canvas screen, where he sat in a deck chair and smoked incessantly. “I see his light is still burning.” “Fiddle, fiddlesticks!” Fran interjected. “Go home now, Tommy, and let us get to sleep.”	- “Sleep!” Elsie was horrified. “Do you think I could sleep before I was sure Cath was all right?” “Tum-te-tum-te-tum,” Fran ridiculed. “Run along, Tommy. You have no busi- ness to be hanging around a lady’s dormi- tory any way. And this one hasn’t any door or a roof. Go away.” Elsie snickered, but Tommy lingered a minute or two, hoping she would ask him to stay because she needed the assurance of his presence. But Elsie was silent. She had begun to count the beat of her pulse, wondering when she was going to have malaria. So Tommy, murmuring a reluctant good night, strolled forward, altogether disgusted with life in general and Fran Goddard in particular.The Gray Charteris 103 ? He hung over the rail near the companion- ladder till Captain Walton joined him. Then, because there was nothing else to d<v he accepted the captain’s invitation to pro- ceed to the smoking-room where they played cribbage and drank long drinks. Presently the bosun intruded with a pen- cil-scribbled note which he said had been delivered by a Kroo-boy from Marsden’s beach. It was directed to Captain Walton and read: Captain Walton:—■ I am going down-river with Mr. MacGregor and Andy. Don’t let the girls wait up for me. Catherine. Tommy read it, too, and at once departed for the after deck and the “ladies dormitory.” “Walton’s just had a note from Cather- ine,” he announced. “It says'-” “Sh!” Fran Goddard cautioned sharply. “Elsie’s asleep.” Therefore, Tommy whispered the rest of it and walked heavily away. Fran listened to his lagging step for a little while as if it annoyed her, then re- sumed the business of biting nervously upon her underlip. Her eyes were curiously wide—dilated— great black pools that were sullenly dis- turbed. All at once they stormed. She turned suddenly upon her face and beat the pillow savagely with her clenched hands. “—*— you! I want to sleep!” The whispering voice of the bush echoed it mockingly. CHAPTER XXI GUNS CATHERINE had but a misty realiza- tion of the journey between Marsden & Co.’s beach and Agwala-town. When the launch had turned from the main stream into Agwala-town Creek, there came a blacker dark that made the walls of mangrove still more forbidding. Canoes slipped past like ghosts, giving forth no sound save the swish of the pad- dles which was not unlike the rustle of a shroud. Others that dawdled close in to the bush whispered a little more excitedly than usual as the launch passed by. But it was all vague and indefinite to Catherine, who had a desire to turn a searchlight upon it to discover what it was all about. “Won’t Agwala be annoyed at us for" waking him up?” she asked Garland while Hogmanay was occupied showing Andy where to go. “He’ll be annoyed whether we have to wake him up or not,” Garland declared with a grin. “But I’d be willing to wager he hasn’t gone to bed yet. He’s a grand old night owl, is Agwala. His own people say he never sleeps.” “From what I saw of him, I should have said he was asleep most of the time. Are we nearly there?” “In a few minutes. Just around the next bend.” “He doesn’t expect us, does he?” “No. But we won’t surprize him very much. He has too many watch boys.” Catherine was visibly disappointed. The little adventure had a dark, midnight flavor, it was true, but it lacked the spice of uncertainty. Keeping the mosquitoes away seemed to form the most active part of it. The next bend was accomplished without difficulty and in another minute the launch had scraped alongside Agwala’s little wharf and Hogmanay was saying to the shadow- like figure of the watch boy thereon: “Tell Agwala I bring Missy Cap’n Morrison. Make quick.” The watch boy, startled by the announce- ment, shot up the path to the town with hardly a second’s delay. And he had barely disappeared when another, also carrying a bobbing hurricane lantern, came forward suddenly from a by-path to the right and took his place. Evidently the second watch boy, when he saw Hogmanay, Garland and Catherine climb on to the wharf, did not consider him- self and his solitary lantern sufficient for so large a visitation of white people. So he made a thick, unintelligible noise in his throat and two more watch boys ap- peared out of the bush with a suddenness that made Catherine jump. She realized that Garland had spoken the truth when he had stated that Agwala had too many watch boys. Garland took a firm grip upon her arm with his left hand. His right, under caver of a broad, flowing four-in-hand tie, rested idly on his chest. MacGregor’s beard made a better screen than Garland’s tie, and as he grunted an order to the watch boys to go ahead, his expression indicated that Agwala was going104 Adventure to keep the promises he had made at For- cados or else have reason to regret he had ever made them. Andy remained in the launch and smoked the mosquitoes from about his ears. With- out expressing any opinion on the matter, he condemned the whole business utterly. Passing along a broad, well-worn bush- path and around a single turn, then past a straggling group of mud-and-wattle huts that became less scattered and developed into a large and fairly compact circle, Catherine was aware of only a blurred con- fusion of shadows and unclean smells, and of indeterminate things that lay around like animals asleep. Then something huge rose up out of the dark; something with a light in it; and she stood before the house Agwala had built. It was a white man’s house, and Chief Agwala was secretly annoyed by the cir- cumstance that he was not the first native chief who had built one. But it was bigger than that of any competitor, and when Catherine saw it, she wondered if she were not actually standing before one of Marsden & Co.’s house-and-shop constructions. As a matter of fact, she was. Agwala had built his house after the pattern of Mars- den’s at Warri. Even the lower of the two floors, in imitation of the customary “shop,” was used as a store-room and, like the general warehouse of the trading-factory, contained all things necessary to man. Agwala lived “on top.” In one corner of the house there were lamps burning, and as Catherine climbed the stairs, she realized that she had been climbing just such stairs on factory after factory all the way up from Old Calabar. From the top of the stairs, however, she could see that Agwala-town itself was much as it had ever been. It spread out, all around Agwala’s house, fawning in slavish squalor at the feet of opulence. Sordid, dank and sinister, and but vaguely in- definite of population and purpose, it was still but a meager shelter of a groveling people, huddling together in the heart of a sun-scorched mangrove swamp. So that Agwala’s house was all the more incongruous. It had no place in Catherine’s picture. Particularly at that time of night. His “reception room”—the equivalent to a trading-agent’s office—was furnished in wicker from Madeira, and lavishly strewn with native mats more colorful than Jo- seph’s coat. Here and there were enlight- ening evidences of Upper Niger handcraft and needlework. But the extravagant number of lusty colored cushions that occupied the wicker divan and the chairs and overflowed, as it were, on to the floor, suggested at once to the initiated that Agwala had become pos- sessed by a fad and had indulged in it to his heart’s content. He met MacGregor in the doorway lead- ing on to the veranda, and even in his suave and oily moments—and this was one of them—he was simply a little less neutral than usual. “Doh,” he greeted MacGregor, Jakri fashion. “I glad Missy Cap’n Morrison come look me.” He made a heavily stilted courtesy to Catherine. “You sit down? I hab boy bring drink one time.” Hogmanay waved the offer of drinks aside in a manner that sent Agwala back to his cushioned divan with an inquiring look in his eyes. “No drink,” Hogmanay said shortly, and evidently intended to waste no time with the finer points of diplomacy. “Missy Cap’n Morrison came talk trade palaver. Savez?” Agwala nodded. Apparently he thought the prospect both new and interesting. He made no audible comment, however; simply settled himself among his cushions and waited for the discussion to begin. “You ’member you talk with Missy Cap’n Morrison’s father at Forcados?” MacGregor asked, as Garland casually took up a position behind Catherine’s chair. “'I ’member. I sorry he done die. Sorry too much.” “You ’member you talk trade palaver?” MacGregor continued. “I ’member.” “Then what’s matter you no do them thing you promise to Missy Cap’n Morrison father?” Agwala thought that over. He looked at Hogmanay with his head a little on one side and then slowly shifted his glance to Cath- erine. “I no savez,” he said simply—which is the Delta native’s invariable answer to anything he does not care to understand. Thick guttural whisperings from the vil- lage lifted themselves into the short silence that followed; a restless sound like that of • some large animal moving uneasily andThe Gray Charteris 105 grunting in its sleep. Hogmanay permit- ted thte silence to hold for fully a minute, while he looked the bulky chief over from head to foot. “Huh!” It was not often that Mac- Gregor sneered. His contempt for Agwala seemed to radiate from him in sparks. "You no savez, eh? You’re a small-boy, eh? No get sense in your head?” He reached his feet suddenly. “You he! Savez? You lie like bushman. You trade in Siluko, in Koratown, and at Jardine hulk and in Lagos. You think I be fool?” Agwala’s eyes flattened just a little; then he shrugged his shoulders so that they al- most touched his ears. “You say I lie? Be all righ’. You be big man pass me.” And his glance traveled to Catherine and Garland, as if to inquire whether they did not agree with him that MacGregor was a bigger and more powerful man than he. Hogmanay sat down again and tried another tack. “You trade in Siluko?” “Li’l bit,” Agwala admitted with oily simplicity. “Koratown?” “Li’l bit. My boys go dere. I no savez.” “You lie! You savez, all right. You sent them there. But them time you talk with Missy Cap’n Morrison’s father at Forcados, you talked different mouth. No be so?” Another expressive shrug of the shoulders was the most of Agwala’s answer. To which he added that if MacGregor was so con- vinced that he was a liar, there was not much to be gained by him saying anything at all about it. Hogmanay’s right hand slipped under his beard and Garland’s sought the cover of his four-in-hand tie. Catherine simply sat and watched Ag- wala’s fat, shiny and imperturbable coun- tenance with the fascination of one who was afraid to speak in case it might spoil the performance. Even the bat that whirled eerily past the door in its circling flight over the stertorously slumbering village, did not distract her attention. “When white man make promise,” Hog- manay said slowly, “white man do what he say. When he no do what he say, big pala- ver live. Savez?” Agwala understood that and admitted it; so Hogmanay asked— “You make promise to Missy Cap’n Morrison’s father at Forcados?” “I make li’l bit promise. Missy Cap’n Morrison’s father make promise to me. But he done die. He no fit now_to do um promise. Palaver set.” Hogmanay did not agree with Agwala’s sophism. His hand came slowly from be- neath his beard and descended, without any fuss or demonstration of hostility to his knee. Garland’s hand also appeared from under his tie and came to rest leisurely on the back of Catherine’s chair. “Char It was then, just as she felt Garland’s free hand grasp her shoulder warningly, that Catherine saw Agwala’s expression change. As if the craven fear that lurked behind its smug and self-satisfied shelter of fat were suddenly uncertain of its security, a massive sort of restlessness shook him from head to foot. He sat bolt upright and the whites of his eyes showed for the first time. Catherine stole a sidelong glance at Mac- Gregor and discovered a large blue-barreled revolver resting quietly—but only too sig- nificantly—on his knee. And, following Agwala’s fear-filled look behind her, she caught the glint of another revolver barrel that rested almost indifferently on the back of her chair. Garland’s grip on her shoulder tightened a little and she exhaled a deep breath sound- lessly. Then smiled. This, she admitted, with another glimpse at Agwala’s graying countenance, was most interesting. The shrill whistling of the crickets in the bush seemed to grow louder and louder and the low drone of voices somewhere not far distant made the possibilities of the situa- tion assume an explosive quality that was exciting. “Now, we go talk,” MacGregor said quietly. “And we go talk true talk. Savez?” “E-yaw! Chal” “All right. Keep your seat. This gun, he nice, quiet gun when man talk true. But when man talk lie—” Hogmanay left the rest of it to Agwala’s imagination. The chief knew at once that whatever protection he might be able to afford himself later, he was, just then, in a hopeless position. “I-—I no savez what’s the matter,” he whined, and looking toward Catherine, tried to be friendly and indignant at theio6 Adventure same time. “I no do nothing. I be your friend, all same I be your father friend. Long time I savez your father. He be proper man. Them time me and him talk, we talk sof’ly, sof’ly all same proper man talk. Gun palaver no good. I fear. All man fear gun palaver. Trade go ’way them time gun palaver come. I talk true, Missy Cap’n, for sure, I talk true. Agwala no fit to lie.” Catherine listened attentively enough. Then, appreciating what was expected of her, and being conscious of an acute desire to take a hand in the game, she turned to MacGregor with an assumption of imperi- ousness that disturbed Agwala in no small measure. “Tell him he must keep the promises he made to my father, and that if he doesn’t, we’ll find a way to make him. We’re not compelled to tolerate any shuffling on his part, are we?” Hogmanay nodded respectfully, and Gar- land had considerable difficulty in conceal- ing a grin. Then Hogmanay interpreted Catherine’s dictum, and added a few re- marks on his own account. The effect upon Agwala was most marked. He looked from Hogmanay to Catherine in a bewildered fashion, and it was evident that he did not know what to make of Missy Cap’n Morrison. There was a quality in the steadiness of her gray eyes that bothered him considerably. And, of course, there were the guns that made his fat quiver and upset his powers of thought completely. Suddenly Catherine had an idea of her own. Perhaps it was a dangerous idea, but the moment ' it came to her, she was compelled to give voice * to it. She spoke so sharply that even Hogmanay was startled. “Tell him, on second thought, I may change my mind about the promises that were made at Forcados. If he has forgotten his responsibilities in the matter, we can forget ours, too. Tell him, also, that if he is looking for a fight, with guns or without them, Marsden & Co. will give him one that will make him the sorriest Jakri in Benin River.” Garland wanted to cheer, but Hogmanay, canny as usual and having some difficulty in concealing his surprize at Catherine’s sudden vehemence, hesitated. “Go on. Tell him. Who the dickens is he that we should have to come here at this time of night to argue fine distinctions with him?” Agwala saw the fire in Catherine’s eyes quite plainly. And, as Hogmanay had said, he knew the difference between a mem- ber of the family and a servant. MacGreg- or’s attitude increased the difference, and Garland, standing behind Catherine’s chair in so protecting a position, heightened the effect. “What—what’s matter?” Agwala asked Catherine respectfully. “You no savez I be your friend?” Catherine dismissed the plea with a ges- ture that left no doubt, in Agwala’s mind at least, of her position in the matter. She turned quickly to Hogmanay and said just a little impatiently: “Oh, go on! Tell him what I said. You could also tell him, just for effect, that Abado isn’t dead yet. I’m sure he wouldn’t like to be reminded of that.” MacGregor’s eyes brightened for a sec- ond. He nodded slowly and thoughtfully and finally began where Catherine left off. He asked Agwala to remember that Abado was still very much alive. Also, until the Sobo was proved guilty of Morrison’s murder, he was innocent in the sight of the white man’s law, and his trade, in its sub- stance at least, was just as purchasable as that of any other trader. Therefore, since Agwala had not kept his promises, Marsden & Co. did not feel under any obligation to keep theirs. They could not trust Agwala again and did not care to make a new agreement. If they wished to trade with Abado’s boys, they would do so. Such, MacGregor concluded for the sake of emphasis, was the decision of Missy Cap’n Morrison. Agwala gasped. His fat hands orna- mented with diamonds that splashed rather than sparkled, pawed the air then in an effort to make himself understood. Cather- ine was plainly, as Hogmanay had pre- dicted, an element he had not considered for a moment. And the guns- “But—but Abado! He kill your father!” he stammered at last, while a cold trickle coursed slowly down his spine. “You go trade-” “We go trade with all man,” Hogmanay answered and rose to indicate that the dis- cussion was ended, and Catherine immedi- ately followed suit. “If Abado’s boys bring oil, if all man bring oil and kernels—all be same. Palaver set.”The Gray Charteris 107 Agwala sank among his cushions, as un- moving as he had been, but a minute before, a mass of excited gesticulation. Even the guns seemed to be forgotten. He was not looking at Hogmanay or Garland. His attention was entirely de- voted to Catherine who, he was sure, was altogether responsible for the complete re- versal of the terms of his agreement with her father. Catherine returned his rather blank stare with an ill-concealed contempt. For some reason or other, as she expressed it to her- self, she hated the beast. “Palaver set?” Agwala questioned, as though he wanted to be sure that her de- cision was final. “Palaver set,” Catherine repeated calmly and turned toward Hogmanay and the door. The scuff of a naked foot sounded on the veranda. It was followed by that of some one who wore boots. Agwala turned his head sharply. So did Hogmanay and Garland, who slipped their revolvers out of sight in a trice. First into the lamp light came Roku, chief of Dowadda. After him, with his arms dramatically strapped behind him, came Arthur Garriman Bark. Then Charteris, with Bark’s revolver sticking in his belt. CHAPTER XXII “and this!” AGWALA seems to be popular this eve- -	ning.” Charteris regarded the little tableau as though it earned his unqualified approval. Apparently it did not surprize him in the least. Whether he had managed to escape the vigilance of Agwala’s numerous watch boys altogether, or had simply, by virtue of his personality and the queer company he kept, walked roughshod over them, was a little problem no one thought much about just then. For many heavy seconds there was a flat and breathless silence. Everybody looked at Roku and Bark—particularly Bark— and then rather blankly at Charteris again. Agwala’s face sagged and he sank deeper among his cushions. His dull little eyes narrowed and he waited, more in fear than in caution, for some one else to speak first. Catherine drew a little nearer to Hog- manay and Garland kept his position at her elbow. His power is less than the hours that were yesterday,v and when Abado the elephant stamps upon your bel- lies and on his, the laughter of the gray man will be heard among the people of the Ekoi whose country is in the shadow of the Kameroons.” Agwala started back, just as if Charteris had struck him again. This—” and he indicated Roku and Arthur—“is the gray man’s answer. And thisI” Before Hogmanay had any sort of oppor- tunity to intervene; before Agwala, who squealed a Jakri oath and flung his fat, beringed hands up before his face, guessed his insulter’s purpose, Charteris leaned quickly forward and his snake like right hand darted under Agwala’s guard. With a twisting wrench, he tore the heavy coral necklace and the sacred and all- powerful amulet from about the fat chief’s neck and tossed them out on to the veranda! The shriek Agwala gave then was more the cry of a wounded beast than that of a man; an ear-splitting yell that broadened into a roar, and then died out slowly and terribly in a whine that dimmed into a breath-taking quiet. Below, the squalid village seemed to shudder and shrink from that sound as from a knell of doom. The blackness beyond the lamps rocked and swayed and when the ever listening ears of the bush heard it, the ever whispering tongues were stifled. Even Catherine realized the appalling sacrilege Charteris had committed. Noth- ing could have degraded Agwala more or cast a more ugly smear upon his ancestry. “Stop!” MacGregor’s voice was like a miniature thunderclap. “Up wi’ your hands!” Apparently on the point of turning to face Hogmanay and the others, as the hurried patter and scuff of many naked feet came to every ear, Charteris stopped short. He could see one of Hogmanay’s revolvers.The Gray Charteris 109 The other was on a direct line with the middle of his back. His hands, in simple obedience, went slowly above his head. “Garland. Take that weapon oot o’ his belt.” Hogmanay’s Scotch was broadening in the excitement, and Garland who knew the big trader better than he knew himself, left Catherine’s side instantly. Faces that were all eyes and mouth ap- peared in the veranda doorway to remain there whispering in awe. Bark, filled with a speechless indignation, backed as far out of the limelight as he could, and Roku, sheep-like, followed him. Agwala was leaning forward among his cushions, wiping a speck of. blood from his lips. His eyes were glazed with passion, and when, without even turning his head, he gave voice to a string of harsh Jakri guttur- als, the faces in the veranda doorway melted away in a soundless obedience that was scarcely human. “Mind how ye step,” Hogmanay cau- tioned Garland gruffly, and gave no hint of the fact that he understood the gist of what Agwala had said. “He’s a slippery cus- tomer.” “But, Sandy,” Catherine involuntarily protested, almost as much astonished by the suddenness of Hogmanay’s action as by Charteris’ demoralizing performance of a minute before. “Surely you don’t mean “Keep out o’ this, Catherine,” MacGreg- or warned heavily. “No, Garland, not that side. Go round the other way. Aye. That’s better. Fine!” Charteris’ face, as Garland plucked the revolver from his belt, indicated nothing more than an indifferent submission to the dictates of Hogmanay’s thoroughly well- supported authority. “All done?” he asked and began to lower his hands. “No. Keep them where they are. They’re too busy anywhere else. What have ye got to say for yourself, carryin’ on like that? Are ye daft?” Charteris laughed. “You sound like an elder of the kirk, MacGregor, and I hadn’t half finished with Agwala when you interrupted me.” “No? Do you know that he’s just or- dered his watch boys to be flogged on your account?” “Certainly. He is a nasty beast. Always takes it out on the under dog and always makes them do his dirty work. Our friend Arthur, for instance.” “It—it’s a lie!” “Sh!” Hogmanay cautioned. “Not so loud.” Then to Charteris: “What’s he been up to? Maybe ye’ll answer that now?” “Attempted murder,” Charteris declared so frankly that Catherine gave vent to an involuntary ejaculation. “He tried to pot me tonight with that gun Garland just took away from me.” “It’s a lie. I was-” “He tried to murder ye, ye say?” Hog- manay said ignoring Bark altogether. “He was trying to murder Abado and in- cluded me in the preformance,” Charteris admitted with still more astonishing frank- ness. “And I simply wanted Agwala to know that he had missed, and that it isn’t proper for him to induce his hirelings to take pot shots at me. I was telling Agwala this when you interrupted me.” Even Catherine smiled. Garland laughed outright. The brazen impertinence of the man was something that mere words could not encompass. “I see,” Hogmanay said dryly. “You’re so honest, you’re most likely makin’ it up. What were ye and Abado doin’ -when it happened?” “Having a drink.” “Hunh!” And then principally for the benefit of Catherine and Garland, “Just as if Malkett wasn’t sweatin’ himself to a shadow looking for Abado?” “If Malkett will persist in chasing the wrong man-” “How do ye know Abado’s the wrong man?” “Perhaps it’s because Bark is so anxious to kill him before he catches him. Perhaps it’s because Agwala is such a fat and inde- cent liar— Oh, half a dozen reasons.” “And perhaps it’s because ye know the right man.” “Perhaps. May I lower my hands now? I’m getting rather sick of this.” “Just a minute,” Hogmanay said heavily. “I think I’ll take Bark with me. And you, too, just to make sure o’ things in official circles. Parker, I’m sure, would like to see ye.” “Not tonight. I am much too busy.” Again Catherine smiled. This, though she knew as well as any one in the room that Agwala, squatting like a great black toadno Adventure upon silk, was measuring the manner of his revenge upon Charteris as surely and as callously as he would measure out a cup of salt. “Aye. Ye’re busy enough, I don’t doubt,” Hogmanay agreed. “But I think your business can wait a while.” “Not tonight, MacGregor.” The sudden edge on Charteris’s voice was most marked. “I have stood here with my arms in the air, answering your questions and satisfying your curiosity, in the presence of a lady, simply to save a lot of shooting and the possibility of you killing somebody.” “Eh?” Hogmanay gaped ludicrously while Cath- erine and Garland looked as if they were not sure they had heard aright. “And I won’t be disagreeable unless— look out!” Charteris’ voice, lifting suddenly in a shrill shout of warning, was startling enough. Then, as far as Catherine could see, he disappeared. His uplifted arms and silver gray head went out of sight beyond Hogmanay’s bulk as if he had been standing on a trap door. Something less than a second later, Mac- Gregor found two steel wire arms wrapped about his legs below the knees and he top- pled backward with unpleasant suddenness •	and force into a small wicker table. His arms were thrashing the air like flails, in an instinctive effort to preserve his balance, as the table and he crashed down together. One of the revolvers spun along the floor to Catherine’s feet. The other, in Hog- manay’s right hand, had the wicker table on top of it. A lightning fast streak of silver gray leaped for the veranda doorway and flashed through it just as Garland fired. The shop clerk was faster and cooler than the majority of men would have been under the circumstances. But the bullet whistled wide of its mark and buried itself viciously in one of the veranda uprights. “Good night, again, Miss Morrison.” The voice floated back to Catherine like a derisive echo of the shot. And, while Agwala shrieked wildly in Jakri, while Mac- Gregor struggled in massive fury to his feet, while Garland sprang forward in useless pur- suit, and Bark and Roku muttered nervous- ly to themselves, Catherine drew a long, deep breath, and slowly stooped to retrieve Hogmanay’s fallen revolver. She took her time about it. Not because one revolver more or less would have made any difference then. But she was afraid Hogmanay would have been even angrier than he was if he caught her smiling. SOME time later, when all of his unwelcome visitors had gone, Ag- wala squatted among his beloved silken cushions, holding a broken coral necklace and a cloth-wrapped amulet in his plump hands. He was quite alone. The village below was almost still again; a quaking quiet that had an uneasy dread of the dawn. Agwala’s unctuous bulk was shaking with alternate rage and fear, and it would have been difficult to determine which emotion controlled him most. His hatred was of the sort that strips the flesh from an enemy’s bones, strip by strip, while the victim’s eyes- But, to his old and ever growing fear of Abado, was added a still deeper terror of a force he did not understand. Abado was just a man; trade was largely a matter of price; but the gray man who never hurried, the old, old man with the face of a boy—there was no explaining the why or the wherefore of him! Agwala had witnessed the amazing sub- jection of Bark. He had seen the still more astounding downfall of MacGregor; and he had no difficulty in remembering how Char- teris had struck him—him, Agwala!—just as if he were nothing more than the least of his inofficial slaves. Also, he remembered distinctly what Charteris had said about the “son and the father of dogs.” He could neither read nor write. The Jakri tongue knew nothing of letters or hiero- glyphics. But the writing on the wall can always be read by the man who sees it there. Agwala nursed his broken coral necklace and the sacred and all powerful cloth- wrapped amulet in his trembling hands and spelled out the letters one by one. And, the following morning, after a night that was troubled and sleepless, Hogmanay had a long and private conversation with the Great Dane, who, from MacGregor’s standpoint, was not nearly as grave as he should have been. She looked just a little subdued, and if Charteris’ name were men- tioned, she either ignored it altogether or calmly changed the subject. She allowed Hogmanay to code her an- swer to her mother’s cable; but no more than that. “When mother begins to get our letters, I’m sure she’ll stop cabling and worrying,” she said in defense of her determination to remain. “And she will approve of every- thing I’ve done. She’d do the same thing in my place and probably not be nearly so patient about it either.” MacGregor smiled and did not dispute the point. Mrs. Morrison was a calm, level-headed woman; there was no question about that. But as she had married the late Hugh Morrison without sacrificing an atom of her natural assertiveness, Hog- manay was conscious of no regret that she had had the good sense to remain at home. So that the Bonny continued to rock gently in midstream, while the raw, but soundless intrigue of the bush crawled creeper-like and with lightning speed throughout the length and breadth of the Benin country; silently reached out and up into the land of the Ibo even into the “street of N’ri,” and dipped down into the villages of the chiefs of Brass. In the few succeeding days—days of broiling heat and the weariness of a cramped environment-—a sullen heaviness seemed to descend upon the face of the rumpled waters.' And the natives who plied up and down the river assumed an expression that suggested they^ were . listening—always lis- tening. There was nothing imaginary about this. The sinister, whispering voice of vengeance that haunted the gloomy network of creeks or ran breathlessly along the narrow bush paths in the blackness of the night, was growing louder and louder. In these few days, where the creeks were darkest, men died suddenly and without warning, and their bodies slid gently into Mother Niger’s lap where the gurgling rattle of complaint in their throats was swiftly soothed into silence.	1 Whereupon, their women donned their oldest raiment and their brothers or sons became avengers of their blood. So that it was necessary for every man to look behind him at frequent intervals, and canoes that had formerly traveled close in to the bush, sought the greater safety of midstream. By these signs was it known that, at last, Agwala had moved; and like the great, black toad Catherine had compared him with, he had wallowed from his cushions of silk and the words he whispered into the ears of men were immediately translated into machete blades,.muzzle-loaders, and, here and there, an occasional rifle purloined from the white man. None of this was visible on the yacht, however. Even Hogmanay and the Great Dane had no proof of its actual existence. They knew simply of an unrest; a^-faintly perceptible rustle and stir that would ul- timately express itself, in certain quarters, in a red trickle of blood. Merely as a precaution, the watch on the yacht was quietly doubled. On the hulk ancj Marsden’s beach the Kroo headmen were given monotoned orders to assign four boys to night duty instead of the customary one or two. But aside from this indication of possible strife, the river was, to all ap- pearances, quieter and more sober than at any time since the Bonny had arrived there. In these few days, too, the cluster of canoes about the hulk diminished almost hourly. As Bart Jardine’s stock of goods sank lower and lower; as he scurried frantically about the hold in search of a possible bale or case that might have been forgotten, the nondescript native traders who had poured the wealth of the oil palm into his long, leanThe Gray Charteris 113 hands, drifted away from him to crowd Hogmanay’s breakwater or to proceed to the business of emptying the warehouses and shops of the Siluko and Koratown independents. Up at Grandison’s beach, Ogulo-of-the- long-arms slept by day and prowled more cautiously than ever by night. He was in sole possession of the place, and, from a governmental standpoint, was not sup- posed to be there at all. Therefore, on an evening, four days after the disappearance of Charteris, when a pale moon lurked intermittently behind scat- tered banks of clouds and three indetermi- nate shadows slipped in upon Ogulo from behind, there was no one to whom the un- gainly dwarf might have called for assis- tance; nothing between him and death save the quickness of his ear, the swiftness of his foot and the ape-like length of his arms. From right, from left and from behind, the shadows leaped out of the weed-grown clearing almost at Ogulo’s feet, and struck with scarcely the sound of an indrawn breath for warning. With a low guttural of surprize, Ogulo heard and sprang forward. Two of the machete blades missed his head by a hair. The third gashed his shoulder but did not halt him long enough for a second blow to be struck. He had no means of knowing whether his assailants were three or thirty, and, scurrying out of reach of those nearest to him, with astonishing rapidity, he bolted straight for the waterfront and plunged into the swift running river. His pursuers followed, but only for a little while. Swimming as, in the Jakri country, only Ogulo-of-the-long-arms could swim, the dwarf speedily left the satellites of Agwala far behind and headed directly for Hogmanay’s breakwater. Arrived there, he cautiously became pos- sessed of a canoe that belonged to Marsden & Co., and, without any compunction or apologies, he paddled out into mid-stream and then headed straight up river. IT WAS the following morning that Agwala’s forty-paddle canoe swung alongside Marsden & Co.’s gig- wharf. And Hogmanay did not think it surprizing that the paddle boys were silent. Neither was he in any way astonished by the circumstance of the fat chief’s visit, but he was glad that Tommy Dillingworth, - s who had come over from the yacht with Elsie to play a game of billiards, Was on the veranda at the time. Elsie was in the shop below just then looking for a new pair of mosquito boots small enough to fit her. She did not really need the boots, but she thought she needed the diversion of Garland’s service and ad- miration. And, seated on a low pile of gray flannel trousers at the far end of the shop, trying on mosquito boots, Elsie was unquestionably a diversion worthy of any shop clerk’s most devoted attention, re- gardless of how disillusioned and unsenti- mental he might be. Afterward she was going over to the kernel store to try to make the tall, rather dramatic-looking kernel clerk become confi- dential. From what she had seen of him, he looked as if he were struggling valiantly with a secret sorrow and Elsie liked to listen and try to be helpful in cases of the kind. Agwala waddled up from the gig-wharf and along Hogmanay’s veranda with a bloated look of injury on his face. • When he turned the corner, so that he came into full view, MacGregor put his hand on Tom- my Dillingworth’s arm and said quietly: “No. Keep your seat. He’ll not stay long.” Hogmanay also remained seated, and there were no social or even amiable pre- liminaries. The expression on the big Scotch trader’s face was as dull and dead as a barn door, and he stroked his beard as if he had no interest in the proceeding. He did not offer Agwala a chair and the fat chief did not appear to want to sit down. He glanced questioningly at Tommy Dillingworth, leaned against a veranda up- right, and said heavily— “What’s matter you 110 pay proper price for my oil?” “Your price too high,” MacGregor answered briefly. “Cha. Be same price you pay all time.” “Too high,” Hogmanay repeated simply. “I no fit to sell oil for little bit price,” Agwala declared in the nature of an ultima- tum. “And I no fit to buy it for big price. Palaver set.” “E-yaw!” The exclamation meant nothing. It simply gave expression to Agwala’s sur- charged emotions as he was invited to look the whole truth in the face.H4 Adventure “You no want buy my oil?” he asked smoothly after a while. “I want to buy all man oil—at my price.” Agwala’s smile was sickly. But there was no manner of knowing what was actu- ally passing in his mind. He glanced at Tommy again merely as an excuse to take his attention from Hogmanay, and then he looked out toward the Bonny and the Jardine hulk. “I go trade for hulk,” he said sullenly. “All right. Plenty canoe trade for hulk.” Agwala winced and drew away from the rail. MacGregor’s sarcasm did not miss its mark. Just at that moment the number of canoes about the hulk was something less than half a dozen. Consequently, there was no balm in that direction. Agwala might sell, for high prices, on credit if he cared to. But he knew there was no cloth or gin or salt or rice to be found anywhere—- except in the shops and warehouses of Marsden & Co., and the trading-companies affiliated with it. For a minute, perhaps, he stood flat- footed, facing Hogmanay, his great bulk rocking slowly with a rage that was but faintly expressed in the flatness of his eyes. Then suddenly MacGregor rose, and Tommy made a quick grab for his arm. “Mac!” “Get off my beach!” “Careful, Mac! You’ll kill him if you hit him now!” MacGregor paused and stiffened, and Agwala backed slowly away, muttering to himself. Tommy’s hold on Hogmanay’s right arm tightened. “Och! Why should I haud my hands?” It was almost a lament. There was a look in Hogmanay’s normally twinkling eyes that Tommy had never seen before. Agwala took his departure as hastily as possible, and waddled down to the gig- wharf again. MacGregor stood between two of the veranda uprights, a large hand wrapped about each of them, and watched every scuffling step the fat chief took. He continued to watch as Agwala’s canoe drew away from the gig-wharf and headed, as Hogmanay thought, for the yacht. But Agwala’s destination proved to be the hulk, and when MacGregor was sure of that, he let his hands fall to his sides and observed,if or the first time in several minutes, that Tommy was still at his elbow: “Dod! I hope he’ll no’ tempt me like that again!” TO BE CONCLUDEDN A certain day in the long ago, five gunmen descended from surrounding hillsides and closed in on Henry Madrigal, cowman, who had assumed squatter’s rights in the San Andres Mountains before Government surveyors had parceled out the country for purposes of homesteading and purchase. Madrigal fought a valorous but losing battle, drilling two of his assailants into eternal silence before the combat reached the hand-to-hand stage. Then he clubbed his long-barreled six-shooter and rendered two more temporarily helpless before a bul- let nicked his valiant Old heart. Madrigal lived long enough to demand that some one look after his “children”— a little boy and girl, presumably waifs, who were known to be living with him. They had disappeared, and the supposition was that the killers had included them in their slaughter, but disposed of the bodies, ashamed of their inhumanity. Madrigal also, during the last hours of his life, told Sam Barnhill, owner of the Fying Y, in lurid and specific detail what he thought of him. Then he died, a martyr to the cause of range freedom. In traditions of the South- west he still lives as a criterion of courage. Two of the gunmen, of course, required nothing but burial for their services. The other pair apparently received what they thought they were worth and left for parts unknown. Zeigline, the one killer who remained on his feet when Madrigal fell, squatted on the location until Uncle Sam’s engineers platted, plotted and surveyed the San Andres. Shortly thereafter title to the Madrigal place passed to Barnhill—as Madrigal in his last moments had prophe- sied it would. Zeigline received his “pieces of silver” in live stock bearing a Box X brand—which, analyzed, was nothing more or less than the Madrigal “M” amplified and doctored by the branding-iron. Even at that time Barnhill was con- trolling range factor. His five thousand head of cattle dotted the Flats that spread like a canopy at the eastern base of the San Andres. Madrigal had nested, several miles back in the mountains with a small bunch of live stock. This had made Madrigal’s western range boundary identi- cal with the eastern limits of Flying Y, which was intolerable to Barnhill, who saw in it a design to usurp his empire and steal his cattle. He had dealt with it accordingly, vigor- ously and expensively. “The old Madrigal place” thus became one of the numerous Flying Y camps. Later it was known as “Madrigal place.” Today it is referred to as “Madrigal.” After this experience Barnhill looked to his defenses, determined never again to be afflicted by nesters. Like many great generals, however, he overlooked one strate- gic position. The Hackberry Hills bit a right-angled chasm into the southwestern extremity of the Flats and constituted a JI5Ii6 Adventure “No Man’s Land” into which Flying Y cattle seldom ventured because of its remoteness from water. Barnhill never bothered to acquire title in the Hackberry Hills because everyone took it for granted that his “control” included them. g2{F3 THUS, for almost three decades, Vi Sam Barnhill dominated the range unmolested by nesters. Then Louis Bern returned to New Mexico from the nowhere and everywhere of cattle ranges in Canada, Chile, Mexico and points con- tingent thereto and remote therefrom. Bern’s appearance was not hailed as a “return.” He had departed the country unknown and returned equally so. He took nothing for granted, but looked up land office records and discovered that Hack- berry Hills were “open territory.” He entered into a partnership with the youthful, ebullient and wholly efficient Thad Wyman and developed a ranch con- sisting of two-room bunk-house, corral, wagon-shed, well and several hundred head of cattle which fattened in the Grama- grassed hills and valleys. Among other things Bern brought back with him forty years of ripe experience, the muscularity of youth and a keen desire to plant his wandering foot where it would take root. The tradition that a rolling stone gathers no moss held true with Bern. Likewise the axiom that an itinerant pebble acquires a polish more desirable than any lichenic growth. The physical burnishing was man- ifested in the smooth bronze of his always clean-shaved face where character lines were etched lightly, due to the firm flesh underneath preventing deeper inroads. The mental refinement was reflected in his speech, due probably to a painstaking study of pure Castilian—a sure guide to perfect English—and an ability to quote Shakespere with ease and accuracy. Bern had drunk of the Rio Grande years before and had responded to another tra- dition that “when you drink of the Rio Grande, you must return before you die.” Bern was far from dead, but he answered to the lure of the muddy waters nevertheless. He returned, not to die, but to live. To be sure, the Rio Grande flowed ten miles west of the Hackberry Hills—but tradition is allowed that much latitude. A bond of affection, admiration and re- spect linked Bern and his youthful partner, quite aside from their business relationship. Friends characterized them as “Tiger” and “Cub.” Each was all to the other, and they reveled in their plans, work and play. “Only one place in the world I’d rather own than this,” Bern was wont to comment luxuriously after a hard day in the saddle when he and Thad were enjoying their after-supper smokes, “and that’s Madrigal Place. There’s the last word in cow ranches—big mountains, living springs, pine-trees and range almost unlimited. A real place for a home. And Barnhill doesn’t appreciate it. To him it’s just one of the camps.” And Thad, as regularly, unfolded his secret: “Barnhill only thinks he owns Madrigal. The forty acres he holds patent to are lo- cated nearly a quarter-mile from the springs, accordin’ to the way Land Office records tally out with a secret survey I had made. Why don’t we grab it? “Either the boss chainman miscounted his chain lengths while runnin’ his line from the township comer-stake—which is plum’ easy t’ do—or else the transitman trusted to his memory ’stead o’ his note- book. Mebbe some land office clerk thought he knowed better how t’ describe the land than Barnhill did—an’ put it down ‘south- west quarter’ ’stead o’ ‘southeast quarter,’ range, section, township so-an-so. Half the patents in this country are held on faulty de- scriptions—you know that, Louis—only neighbors don’t gen’rally bother each other because they’re friendly an’ trust each other. Barnhill, he don’t trust any one— an’ I’d sure admire t’ hang his hide on the barn-door. I don’t care whose barn it is so long’s the hide’s ol’ Sam’s.” “What has Barnhill done to you?” Bern would counter. “Nothin’. But you know how he come into possession of it—hirin’ gunmen to kill off old Henry Madrigal an’ then buyin’ a relinquishment from Zeigline with Hank’s own cattle. We’ve got as much right to it as he has.” “If I could be certain that Barnhill was behind the murder of Madrigal-” Bern never finished his soliloquy because Thad was at him immediately with proof, evidence, legend and hearsay, s. Barnhill unintentionally furnished theUncle Henry Would Enjoy This. 117 partners with an important part of their amusement. He hired a cattle detective on the assumption that they were stealing his live stock. Goren, the detective, ear- nestly endeavored to catch Bern or Wyman in the act of rustling, but had indifferent luck because no thievery took place. Furthermore, the partners developed a dis- concerting habit of doubling on their trails and coming up in his fear to rebuke his suspicious nature. The bewildered ex- pression on Goren’s face at such times pro- vided them with merriment for days after- ward. One day, however, Bern tired of this pastime and advised Goren that his presence was no longer, and never had been, desired on B. W. range in an official capacity. Thereafter they saw no sign of him until the September afternoon when Bern cut the trail of Goren’s horse leading from Flying Y	plains country into the rocky inception of B. W. range. “Interesting and undoubtedly true,” Bern mused, studying the tracks carefully— he knew the hoof imprint of every horse in Goren’;* string of mounts. ‘ The trail led through a pass in the red rimrocks and then angled south where it was joined by another. “Thad’s,” Bern chuckled. “He must have seen Goren coming. They both stopped their horses and evidently had a chat. Wonder what it was about?” Wonderment increased to anxiety when Bern noticed that Thad had reined along- side Goren and accompanied him on the southerly course. He looked full at the sinking sun medita- tively. “It’s time that boy was back at the ranch,” he decided, “but judging from the freshness of the tracks and the direction they take, he won’t get there for some time.” The ranch lay to the northwest. “Goren must have gotten him interested in some- thing to lead him down here at this time of day.” Another half-mile south, the tracks of the two horses led into a rocky canon so desti- tute of grass that cattle rarely strayed into it. “Strange place to go,” Bern muttered. “Never was here but once before, myself. And I don’t think Thad ever rode this canon.” He spurred his mount, a magnificent blood-bay, to a fast trot. Daylight would soon fade, and he felt the necessity of follow- ing the trail to its logical conclusion. This was the most isolated part of B. W. range, and Bern was reasonably curious to know the reason for the pilgrimage. He rounded a sharp jut in the canon and solved the problem all in the same instant. Gruya, Thad’s mount, stood riderless, nickering into a yawning prospect-hole which had been abandoned many years before by the discouraged miner who dug it. Bern’s mental eye beheld the scene of tragedy as it must have been enacted; he reconstructed the-events leading up to it: Goren had lured Thad into the desolate canon on some plausible pretext and then, catching his victim off guard, the cattle de- tective had either pushed or knocked him into the pit. Boot tracks of both men offered eloquent testimony, and Bern over- looked nothing. Bern hurled himself from the saddle, his copper-hued face faded to a subdued pink, and bellied his way to the crumbling brink of the shaft. “Thad!” he shouted into the darkness— and the name rose up from the depths in echo to mock him. “Thad!” again he called—and the re- bound of his voice ascended with a force that seemed to smother him. Bern had to summon all his reserve courage to make his third attempt to rouse Thad— “If you are alive, Thad, in the name of God, answer me!” From the confused jumble of noise re- bounding from the bottom and sides of the abyss came the last three words, clear as if shot from a sounding board— “God, answer me!” Bern snapped to a sitting position and clapped hands to ears. The ghastly reper- cussion unnerved him. Action served as a sedative. Knotting the rope which he took from Gruya’s saddle, he tied it to his own.. A heavy rock, secured to the sixty foot length, dangled in midair. Tense despair gripped Bern. “ ‘God, answer me!’ ” he quoted the re- sounding words that had leered at him from the open grave. “What shall I do? Thad is gone! Dead! And I can’t even get his body.” For a moment Bern stood motionless,118	Adventure his active brain grappling with the situa- tion. The last glow of sunlight illumined his features into a semblance of some heroic- sized bronze medallion; the same glint of light caught dazzlingly at the smooth inner surface of his leather chaps where they had been worn shiny by his knee-grip on the saddle. Then decision animated his muscles. A flying leap carried him to Redman’s back and spurs roweled the big bay to a frenzied speed up the hillside where led the tracks of a lone rider—the rider who had been with Thad up to the time they had approached the prospect-hole. SWEAT blackened Redman’s neck as he held the terrific pace, re- sponding to Bern’s commands of voice and steel. Bern knew that he must sight Goren before dark, otherwise the chase would be indefinitely prolonged; Goren might even escape. The trail led down into the next caiion and showed that Goren was traveling easily, apparently without fear of pursuit. The isolated position of Granite canon—where the crime was committed—with the meager possibility that any one would invade it for months, gave Goren a sense of security. The chase led diagonally through Hack- berry Hills toward the Flats, and Redman quickly closed the intervening distance. When dusk faded Bern decided that Goren could not be more than a mile ahead and guided his horse up a little pinnacle that commanded a view of the next canon. The sight of a lazily moving horseman in the hazy depths below inspired Bern to a gutteral exclamation of joy—choked back barely in time to save him from discovery. As it was, Goren turned in his saddle- listening for a sound that he thought he heard. Bern thought for an instant that Goren saw him, but continued to “freeze” in his place of semiconcealment behind the scraggly limbs of a scrub cedar until the man disappeared around a turn in the canon.	, Bern played a one-sided game of hide and seek for the next half-hour. He rode the ridges with utmost caution, stopping every few minutes to listen. As darkness shrouded the hills he followed Goren by sound. Al- ways he rode a little faster than the man he was following, and gradually he widened the circle until finally he was in the lead. Then he left Redman and, taking his saddle-rope with him, crept silently down the hillside, where he stationed himself in protecting shadow of a giant clump of mesquite.	___ Goren’s first knowledge that he was not alone came when a swish of rope warned him—too late. A noose settled and tight- ened about his neck. He loosed the bridle reins to grapple with the strangling hemp and was jerked unceremoniously from the saddle. He hit the ground heavily and, before he could recover, Bern was upon him with the ferocity of a jungle beast and the science of a wrestler. Face down in the shale, rock and sand, the cattle detective fought viciously against a paralyzing leg-hold. Bern shifted to a sitting position in the middle of Goren’s back, always drawing the tortured leg to- ward him until the struggles ceased and agonized profanity began; then he used one hand to snatch Goren’s gun from its holster. “Pardon my seeming roughness,” Bern panted, throwing a double half-hitch around Goren’s ankles with the loose end of the rope, “but I’m in a hurry and can’t stop to argue.” He hastily “dallied” the lariat’s remain- ing slack around his own leg and increased the tension to maintain the leverage on Goren’s limbs, drawing a hogging-string from his belt with which he-trussed Goren’s wrists. Bern made short work of freeing himself from the rope, retrieving Goren’s mount— which had stopped to graze outside the danger zone—and cutting from the saddle the leather thongs that, under happier circumstances, were used for packing pur- poses. From these he fashioned another tie-rope and used it to replace that portion of the lariat binding Goren’s ankles. He also removed the noose from the detective’s neck and delivered a few succinct remarks while coiling up the rope: “This may seem a trifle unnecessary to you, but just now I’m in need of an extra length of rope for the purpose of getting my partner’s body from that prospect hole. Also I want to be sure you’ll be here when I get back. See you in about an hour and a half and then—I’ll settle with you!” Bern started to leave, then hesitated. He came back and observed Goren’s tumble-bug efforts to free himself.Uncle Henry Would Enjoy This 119 “I’ll just fix you so I’ll be, fairly sure to find you when I return,” he remarked. He unsaddled the horse, carried Goren to a scrub cedar and cinched the saddle around both man and tree-trunk. Goren protested luridly. “Anything you say may be used against you,” Bern warned with a sort of deadly playfulness. “Better save it. You’ll need everything you have when I get back.” THE Autumn chill inspired Goren to efforts at escape. He knew that the double-half hitches knotted be- hind him at ankles and wrists, held him se- cure; the saddle held him immovably in place, but wriggling and squirming kept his blood more actively in circulation. Then, the sudden thought that Bern in retrieving the body of his partner would learn defi- nitely how he came to his death twisted Goren’s swarthy, mustachioed face into a grimace of fear, and he set to work in earnest to free himself from bondage. He leaned back on the fleece-lined inner surface of the saddle and pressed down. It slipped a trifle as the circingles stretched. He repeated the operation again and again, sweating copiously with the labor. An hour passed and the saddle was midway past his hips. Goren paused for rest and reflection. If he could free himself of the saddle it wouldn’t be much of a trick to hop to a sharp-edged rock and saw his wrist mana- cles apart. Time was all he needed. He calculated how long Bern would be gone: It would take him at least twenty minutes to ride the five mountainous miles to Granite Canon. Then—how long would it take for two horses, pulling at a ninety-foot length of saddle rope, to drag Bern and his inanimate burden from the shaft? Goren pondered. It couldn’t be done! The dead weight of a yearling on the end of a thirty-foot rope was all the average cow-pony could move. More than double that weight on even a sixty-foot rope would either stall two horses or break the rope. Ninety feet of it would be impossible. Bern would discover this only after experi- ment that might be disastrous. At least it would delay him while he figured out some other plan. Then he would have to dispose of the body. Would he take it to the B. W. ranch? Probably. The county coroner would be called in to determine, officially, the cause of death, and he would find- Goren had enjoyed meditation on the time element, but a forecast of the coroner’s findings, with Bern to assist, spurred him to further activity. He had to escape or Bern would kill him. If Bern didn’t, the law would. The saddle slipped gratifyingly after another fifteen minutes of writhing and pushing; it fell to the ground, and the leather skirts pinioned his spurs. He kicked them free, bent over to find a fairly soft place on the hillside where he could rest his head— and then somersaulted from his saddle prison. A jagged rock freed his hands after several minutes of awkward sawing, and his pocket-knife cut the ankle-thongs. All he had to do now was to catch his horse, saddle him and leave promptly in a southerly di- rection. He started to unfasten the cinch- buckles from around the cedar, wondering where Bern might be and what doing— when he heard the clattering hoof-beats of a galloping horse on the ridge to the north. He knew then that Bern had upset the time calculation in some manner and had returned a full five minutes too soon. Goren abandoned the saddle and rushed toward his horse—which shied at his hasty approach. Goren cursed and made a futile grab at the trailing bridle-reins as Redman came slithering down the slant with Bern swaying easily in the saddle, uttering ironical comment and advice: “Speed up, Goren! You’re slow as a drag cow’s tail. Do I note by the dim starlight that you were about to leave bare-back?” Bern hurled himself from Redman’s back, six-shooter in hand. Goren dashed behind a boulder. “Come out of there,” Bern snapped. “You can’t hole up like a ground-hog. Come out this way and I won’t shoot. Start in any other direction and I’ll fill you full of lead.” Goren appeared, hands elevated. “Now,” Bern told him. “Put your hands down. I know, you’re not heeled. You need killing, but I can use you alive to bet- ter advantage. You’re going with me to my ranch and there, with all the comforts of home at your disposal, write a confession about how you killed Thad Wyman—and why.”120 Adventure A remonstrance surged as high as Goren’s larynx—and died there to the click of Bern’s gun. He thought about his knife, buried snug and useless in the trousers’ pocket under his chaps. “Roughly,” Bern rambled on, “it will run something like this: You met Wyman at the B. W. range-border and roused his curiosity about the shaft, which he’d never seen be- cause none of our cattle ever go into Granite Canon and consequently he hadn’t been there. The two of you dismounted, and Wyman was looking into the hole when you hit him on the back of the head with a rock. He fell in and the rock went with him. I found it beside him. Any re- marks to make?” “You’re so wise,” Goren sneered, edging closer. “Maybe you know that Flyin’ Y cows sometimes stray there.” “You ought to know. You rode B. W. range for months before I ordered you off. Perhaps a dozen a year stray into Granite Canon. I saw one there tonight.” This was interesting information to —	Goren. He hadn’t seen any. He wanted to know about it, but other things were more vital. “An’ what makes you think I clouted Wyman with the dornic you found by his body? Wasn’t there any others near him?” “Yes, but none others with hair and blood.” Goren had maneuvered within striking distance and sprang. His nose collided violently with Bern’s left fist. “I hoped you’d do something like that,” Bern told him, tossing the pistol into a bed of gravel. “My hands itch for the feel of you. Your gun’s in one of Redman’s saddle-pockets; mine is where you see it— behind me. Get them if you think you can wade through me.” This was a chance Goren hadn’t antici- pated. Bare fists against a man twenty pounds lighter than himself, who insanely threw away his advantage. Catch-as-catch can—grapple—kick. Goren knew the rough-and-tumble game. Let him once get his arms around Bern- He rushed. Bern drove an accurate “one-two” into the detective’s midriff and followed with a hurricane of staggering blows to the face. Goren shook blood from his eyes and waded in for more, crowding Bern back toward the faint glint of the blued Colt. His arms flailed the air with a windmill motion and he felt he was making progress in spite of the fearful punishment he was taking. Bern retreated before the onslaught of superior weight until his boot-heel rested on the butt of the six-shooter. Then he planted himself and shot his fist with the full power of shoulder and body behind it to a point slightly below Goren’s left ear. The detective’s feet cleared the ground, and he lay unconscious where he fell while Bern picked up the gun. He was still prostrate after Bern had caught and saddled the Flying Y pony. It required the cold steel of a gun-barrel at the back of his neck to revive him. “Any further remarks before you write the confession?” Bern inquired. “Not none.” Goren mounted. Tears of agony crept from his battered eyes and scalded his abraised cheeks. His nose had been wrecked and faultily repaired at some for- mer date; its condition was now hopeless. “ ’Cept I’d like to know how you got Wyman out o’ that shaft so quick. Saddle ponies never drug you out.” “Of course not,” Bern laughed as he rode beside his unfettered prisoner, with a pecu- liar exultation that puzzled Goren. “I didn’t even try to use the horses. Didn’t it occur to you that I might anchor one end of the rope to a boulder and climb out hand-over-hand with Wyman lashed to my back?” “Nuh-uh,” was Goren’s ambiguous reply. He looked at Bern in the faulty light with a new appreciation. He tested his jaw by moving it clockwise on its hinges and shook his head to determine whether his brains were still in place. He understood now why Bern’s hitting powers had the qualities of a mule’s kick. Prodigious brawn was required to pull a weight of over three hundred pounds, seventy-five feet straight up, with nothing but an occasional toe-hold to assist. £?1PF1|' THE darkest part of the moonless night was upon Flying Y head- quarters when Goren rode behind the high adobe wall that formed the east side of the corral. He spoke harshly to a companion rider, who responded: “Be quiet. Nobody must wake up ex- cept Barnhill. Take off your spurs—take mine off, too.”Uncle Henry Would Enjoy This 121 Goren complied in utter silence. The men tiptoed to Barnhill’s quarters in the north end of the long adobe building that also housed the Flying Y cowboys at the southern extremity. Goren tapped on the window while his associate partially hid behind the north wall. Barnhill was evidently a light sleeper for he responded without delay, opening the window. “Come outside,” Goren whispered after a hasty glance convinced him that the other man was still on the job and keenly alive to his every movement. Barnhill grunted: “Guess I’d better. Mrs. Barnhill’s asleep in the next room an’ might hear us.” Ffteen seconds, perhaps, intervened be- tween acquiescence and appearance. Barn- hill, shirtless and carpet-slippered, stepped through the aperture and sat down on the ground, shivering, beside his employe, his back to the east wall of the house. “I got Wyman,” Goren announced sourly. “Good,” the cattleman growled through the bushy mustache that completely ob- scured his mouth even in daylight. “Did you fin’lly catch him stealin’ stock?” “Nope. I don’t think he’s ever rustled a hoof off you.” “You’re a fool,” Barnhill grunted. “Why else’d him an’ Bern locate where they did— right alongside my range?” “Good grass in those hills,” Goren sug- gested. “Likely place to build up a herd.” “Sure—build it up at my expense. I’m satisfied they’ve been robbin’ me blind— same as ol’ Henry Madrigal use’ to do before he got his. That’s why I hired you—an’ you’d ’a’ caught both of ’em cold if you’d been onto your job.” Silence from Goren. He answered only after his employer inquired testily— “How did you work it to get Wyman, then?” “I led him down a canon in the Hack- berries an’ hit him on the head with a dornic while he was peerin’ down a seventy-five foot prospect-hole.” The detective spoke without pride, almost, it seemed, with reluctance. “Serves him right, the - thief,” Barnhill commended. “Wish his pardner was there, too. But suppose some one finds the body?” “No one hardly ever goes into that canon,” Goren replied peevishly. “An’ what if they do? It’ll look like he fell in. The fall’s enough t’ kill him even if I hadn’t cracked him with a rock. I left his horse standin’ there so’s it’d look plum’ natural if any one should happen along. In case they don’t, the horse’ll wander home after a while an’ the country’s that rough he couldn’t be back-trailed.” “Sounds reasonable,” Barnhill applauded in guarded tones. Then, cautiously: “How’re you goin’t’ keep folks from talkin’ about you gettin’ in so late? The hands’ll all know what time you slip into the bunk- house.” “I’m leavin’ the coimtry tonight,” Goren announced, “an’ want my pay It’s three hundred an’ twenty-five bucks.” “How come?” Barnhill inquired in the manner of one who knows the answer to his own question. “I got twenty-five cornin’ for the last two weeks’ ridin’ an’ three hundred which you promised if I got either Bern or Wyman.” He spoke with some animation, glancing at the north wall of the adobe. “Well—I got Wyman.” Barnhill demurred: “How do I know, except by your say-so, that you’ve earnt the bonus? Besides, what makes you think I’ve got that much money on me?” “You’ve got it ’less you lied,” Goren answered the last question. “You told me this mornin’—or yest’day mornin’—before I left here that the cash was in the safe in your office. You said further that it’d stay there ten days ’less I claimed it sooner on account o’ breakin’ up th’ B. W. com- bination. If I didn’t qualify for it in that time I was to get th’ gate. Three hundred for either pardner'—seven-fifty for both—■ was the agreement. “One’s all I care t’ collect on/ You go get the money, an’ I’ll furnish proof that Wyman’s dead.” Barnhill climbed into the window and Goren found time for a snatch of conversa- tion with the man in hiding. Faint signs of dawn streaked the east when the cattleman emerged from the room. If the Avenging Angel with flaming sword had made its appearance at that moment the effect on the Flying Y owner might have been slightly more enervating—though that is doubtful. Enervation was sufficiently complete to enable Goren to pluck the blood money from his employer’s nerveless hands —convincing evidence that Mr. Barnhill was not his normal, tight-fisted self. Bern spoke first, the flash from his blue eyes—now visible in the semi-light, adding a certain meaning to his softly spoken words: “Mr. Goren neglected to mention, I be- lieve, that he was overtaken by the surviv- ing member of the B. Your recent transaction with him shows the sacredness with which you regard a prom- ise, so I know you will understand why I kept mine—when I explain. “Goren bought his chance to escape by writing the confession and by leading me to this place where I could hear you express yourself with such a charming lack of self- restraint. My impression was that the dirty cowards who hire assassins for their work lived only in fiction. However, the business of this night conduces me that at least one is alive. If that wasn’t enough, your remark of a few minutes ago would be plenty.’” Barnhill snarled again: “We’re dealin’ with the present. The present links itself with the past rather importantly,” Bern retorted. “You’ll re- member the two children, orphans, who were living with Henry Madrigal at the time your men killed him. Cudgel your memory a bit and see if you can’t remember that the children’s names were -Louis and Louise.” It was evident from Barnhill’s ghastly pallor that he did. “Sis and I were back in the hills gathering wild raspberries when the shooting began. She ran, I after her, and we got back in time to see Uncle Henry—yes, he was my mother’s brother—lying apparently dead and strangers in possession of the place. We didn’t know exactly what it all meant, but we decided that men who would kill lovable old Uncle Henry would do the same to us. So we didn’t give them the chance. We tramped to Roswell—and it was a hideous trip for us kids; Sis was nearly dead when we arrived. I got a job as er- rand boy in a grocery store; later, I worked /as a horse-wrangler and sent Louise to New England where we had some distant rela- tives. Then I started out to see the world. I’ve seen it now and came back here to settle down because I love the country—it contains the only memories of a home that I can remember—Uncle Henry had moth- ered Louise and me since we were babies.” “Henry Madrigal’s nephew!” Barnhill found his voice after exercising his Adam’s apple for several minutes. “Yes,” Bern answered levelly. “And you’ve sent your killers against the two men who have been the best friends I’ve ever known. One substituted for my mother—and did as good a job of it as any man could do; the other my pal—you probably don’t know what the word means in its glorified sense—the sort that I’d liked to have had as a son if I’d ever mar- ried. You even cost me the privilege of growing up with my sister—she died ten years ago—by destroying our home. “In view of these things I’m going to sell the B. W. ranch to you—at my price. It’s a hold-up.” “It may be,” Bern snapped in a tone that roused Barnhill to the need for more guarded utterance, “but it’s not murder. I need twenty thousand to carry out a plan that Thad would approve. He’s often mentioned it, but there’s always been an obstacle in the way, and I’m willing, for the sake of accomplishing this thing, to keep quiet about Goren’s confession. “I’d suggest, therefore,” he concluded briskly, “that you make out your check for the amount named and we will then proceed to Marona—the county seat—in your high- powered flivver, where the cashier will do some certifying where it will do the most good; also we can complete the transaction while we’re there and B.
