He knew that Redman, sweat-stained and leg-weary, would betray his nocturnal visit to the horse-wrangler, who would be on the job within fifteen minutes,—but that was some- thing for Barnhill to worry about. Specu- lation would be rampant among the Flying Y	cowboys immediately upon discovery of Bern’s horse in Flying Y pasture—and Barnhill could think of his own explana- tions. BARNHILL’S sense of business acumen was outraged by the price he was called upon to pay for a “two by twice” cattle outfit. The same inner prompting, however, told him that while the monetary consideration was far above the market value, it bought a protection worth infinitely more—if Bern would,keep his promise to remain silent. He decided that the next time he re- quired the private and violent services of a cattle detective he would hire someone who knew the value of a. clean getaway. A lurking suspicion that he was being victimized found expression as he was piloting his runabout along the sandy road to Marona. “Say,” he jerked out, “we don’t do any business till I see that confession you say Goren wrote.” Bern, who had been soliloquizing on the softness of saddles as compared with the once springy seat he now shared with Barnhill, smiled easily. “I’m surprized you didn’t think of that before. While you’re busy with that steer- ing wheel, I’ll just spread it out for your inspection. It would seem, though,” he rebuked his companion, “that you might be willing to take the word of a trusted employe who reported the work done, thus showing great fidelity to your interests.” Barnhill’s jaw muscles moved as if his tongue had been inspired to voice a fitting rejoinder—then relaxed. Bern reached into his inside vest pocket—he wore no coat— and produced the document which he held where Barnhill could read it. The contents evidently made unpleasant reading matter. “He lies,” was the only comment. “Your soft impeachment would sound a trifle more chummy if I hadn’t been an ear— and eye-witness to your cheerful conver- sation with Goren,” Bern remarked return- ing the paper to his pocket. “Perhaps, as additional proof you’d like to inspect the shaft in Granite Canon. I could let you down with a rope.” Barnhill’s rugged features registered irri- tation at the first part of Bern’s comment and a violent distaste at the latter. He was sufficiently in Bern’s power now without submitting to being tied to one end of a rope over which Bern had complete control. The gasoline sand-boat did most of the talking for the remainder of the fifty-five mile trip to Marona, and conversation in that bustling county seat was confined strictly to the business of purchase and sale of B. W. ranch and cattle.	m Bern thanked Barnhill cordially for the offer—which was not made—of a ride back to Flying Y headquarters and requested that his horse be kept in pasture until called for. “I’m headed south on the evening train,” Bern confided, “and you probably won’t see me for some time.” ** Mr. Barnhill’s mobile features again registered a sentiment which Bern inter- preted to mean' that if the Flying Y owner never saw him again, that would be soon enough. THE following week provided in- IjjSgP! terest for all persons who knew Thad Wyman, Louis Bern or Goren. The purchase of B. W. by Barnhill would have caused little comment had it not been accompanied by the disappearance of both partners and the Flying Y rider. Rumor from Marona had it that Bern alone repre- sented B. W.’s interests at the sale and that he used a quitclaim to complete legalUncle Henry Would Enjoy This 125 formalities. It became known, too, that Bern’s mount remained at Flying Y while “Gruya,” Wyman’s favorite pony, had been at the home ranch when Barnhill’s cowboys tallied out the newly purchased live stock. Barnhill, for reasons of personal policy, kept his own counsel with regard to Bern and Wyman and explained the departure of Goren on the grounds of an urgent mes- sage from home folks in Texas. Another phase of Barnhill’s personal policy induced him to drive into Tedler— shipping-point, post-office and merchan- dising center of that territory, eleven miles north of the ranch—to ask Hal Currie, the deputy sheriff, whether anything had been heard about Bern or Wyman. He was anxious tp learn whether any foul play was suspected or discussed. Currie’s mouth being too full for polite utterance at the moment, he emptied it of liquid content before replying. Then he said he “reckoned Bern and Wyman knew what they were doing and would show up when they got good and ready.” The trip to town was not, however, with- out thrills for Barnhill. A letter postmarked El Paso sent him flying to the hotel telephone. After being connected with a Marona number, he gave excited directions and waited perspiringly for a return call. When it came, after a half-hour interval, he swore venomously into the transmitter and ordered that a sur- veying crew be shipped to Tedler on the afternoon train. Emerging sweatily from the phone booth he drew the letter from his pocket and re- read it to assure himself that he hadn’t excited himself unduly. He had not. The letter read: El Paso. Sept. 30. Dear Mr. Barnhill: This will introduce the T. L. Cattle Company, which respectfully requests the immediate removal of all Flying Y cattle from the water known as “Madrigal.” This land, we are informed, has long been con- sidered Flying Y property, but a survey, made pre- liminary to our purchase of same from the Govern- ment proves otherwise. Your holdings, some forty acres secured by patent, are located about four hundred yards south of the ground you claim. Our leases, in addition to what we have acquired by purchase, fully protect our interest in the range. While, of course, your cattle have a perfect right to graze on the forty acres you own, the range will, I think, prove too limited and dry for either comfort to them or profit to you. I will take this opportunity to make an appoint- ment with you at the Tedler hotel for the morning of October 2. I will arrive on Number Four, which is due at 10:30. Better make it about the time of my arrival as a trainload of T. L. cattle will land in Tedler shortly after the passenger. The stock will be driven to Madrigal immediately as the four- hour ride from El Paso will not necessitate their being held for rest in the stock pens. Yours very truly Louis Bern, Manager. Citizens of Tedler contend that Barnhill frothed at the mouth that last day of Sep- tember and cursed lawyers, surveyors and Louis Bern with equal and animated feroc- ity. He talked mostly to himself, but Hal Currie overtook him on the board sidewalk in front of Elliott’s store after hearing the name of Bern mentioned hotly. “Do I gather that you heard from Louis Bern?” Currie inquired sociably, lighting a long, crooked stogie. “Blah!”—or words to that effect—an- swered the harassed cattleman. “Does he say where he’s been or what he’s doin’?” the plump deputy pursued the subject, apparently absorbed in the sput- tering end of his smoke. He looked up, expecting a reply; then, in utter surprize, he craned his neck, owl-like, and saw Barnhill stumping angrily in the direction whence he had come. J BARNHILL’S operations during the next forty-eight hours subsequent to the receipt of Bern’s letter were guided largely by three important con- siderations: (first) The surveyors hired by him verified Bern’s statement that Madrigal was not Flying Y property; (second) Bern was possessed of information and proof that would ruin him; and (third) Barnhill, though an oppressor and a bully, felt a very personal and physical fear of the bronzed cowman who spoke with sardonic gentleness while his eyes glittered with a menace of sudden death. The cattleman, seated on the Tedler hotel gallery waiting for the arrival of Number Four, reflected moodily on the scurvy trick played by the Rio Grande in reaching out to the ends of the world and bringing back the man who as a child had drunk of its turgid waters. This circumstance was no less incredible— and no less true—than the fate that had caused him to cross the trail of Louis Bern, the nephew of Henry Madrigal, twenty-nine126 Adventure years after the old pioneer had laid down his life for the same underlying cause that brought about the original conflict. Most unbelievable of all was that Bern, of the Madrigal blood, should come into possession of the hills and valleys his uncle lost—after fighting and dying for them. Number Four steamed into the station where most of Tedler’s population—some three hundred when they were all at home— was gathered. Amazement and joy held them speechless as Louis Bern descended the day coach steps. Then: “Thought you’d left us for keeps!” “How come?” “Welcome to our fond embrace!” greeted him. Hand clasps and slaps on the back—also a trampling of his toes, which were clad in a pair of new, elegantly stitched riding- boots—followed. Bern’s weathered face relaxed into smile-lines and crows’ feet as he shook sinewy paws, two at a time. “I didn’t think any one’d miss me,” he declared happily. “Only been gone a little over a week.” Some one asked, “Where’s Thad?” and Bern pretended not to hear. He whisked off his gray, undented, wide-brimmed hat— also new—and cleared a path for himself. “See you all later and talk till I’m out of breath,” he promised them, “but I can’t stop now; got an appointment to meet Mr. Barnhill at the hotel.” A half-dozen cowboys and citizens were still at his sides and heels when he ap- proached Barnhill. The rancher, without offering to shake hands, suggested that they retire to his room on the second floor. “More privacy,” he recommended, sig- nificantly eying Bern’s retinue of followers. The lone chair in Barnhill’s room induced Bern to park himself on the bed. “I judge you got my letter,” Bern opened fire smilingly. A wry twist of the cattleman’s mouth answered. He was looking out of the window. “Is there anything further for us to mill over?” Bern inquired. “Yes, there is,” Barnhill stormed, sud- denly jumping to his feet. “I’ve got all possible o’ my cattle moved from Madrigal, but there’s still five thousand dollars’ worth of improvements there an’ you needn’t think y’u can steal that, along with th’ range. I’ll fight y’u to a finish ’less I get paid for it.” “Tut, tut,” Bern chided him. “I have stolen nothing. I’m merely collecting in part for thirty years’ use of Madrigal place —my rightful inheritance. And Uncle Henry paid a thousand times over—with his life—for any improvements you’ve made. For all that, my partner and I are willing to-” “So,” Barnhill sneered. “You cash in on the death o’ your friend Wyman an’ then gather yourself another pardner in less’n two weeks. What kind of a man do y’u call y’urself? That boy—” he waxed sen- timental—“still layin’ in a cold hole in the ground an’ you galiivantin’ ’round on the proceeds o’ his death.” A blue blaze leaped into Bern’s eyes. “It will probably ease your tortured soul to learn that I saw to the reclaiming of Wyman’s body the historic night when Goren, at your orders'-” “Where did y’u bury him?” Barnhill cut in breathlessly. “Won’t some one be ap’ to find the grave?” “That’s for you to worry about,” Bern shot at him. “All I promised to do was keep quiet about the confession.” A cattle-train snorted past the station and both men watched it back on to the switch and move to the railroad stock pens. Bern reached for his hat. “Come along with me to the pens and I’ll tell you something for the good of your soul,” he said. Barnhill concurred grumpily. He must learn where Wyman’s grave was located and make certain that it wouldn’t be dis- covered. “You remember I told you that I needed twenty thousand dollars to carry out a plan of Thad’s?” Bern recalled as they clattered down the hotel stairs. “Well—he wanted to take Madrigal place away from you. He knew about the defective title long before I came into the country. And I couldn’t think of any more fitting way to avenge him than by making good on his favorite idea and using your money to do it. Poetic justice, I’d call it, particularly as it brings Madrigal place back into the family— where it belongs.” Barnhill bristled like an angry dog. He didn’t enjoy being ridiculed—particu- larly by a man who had stripped him ofUncle Henry Would Enjoy This 127 thousands in money and the cream of his range. The fact that he had laid himself wide open to the attacks and deserved the penalty didn’t ease the agony. Bern grinned. “It will interest you to know that my present partner was taking a gang of sur- veyors out to Madrigal place the same day I sold B. W. to you. I waited for him in Marona till he got back with the description of the land and we made our purchase before the land office closed. His name didn’t appear on the papers so your attorney put all the blame on me. As a matter of fact, though, I’ve given him an undivided half- interest in Madrigal.” Barnhill snorted— “Dickerin’ with an outsider while you an’ Wyman were still pardners—or else you throwed in with this other party before Thad was cold in death.” The caboose of the cattle-train was less than ten yards up the track and three cow- boys filed out of the door, clambering from the steps as the brakeman signaled the engineer. The train crunched to a jarring, jangling stop. Cattle bellowed. “Is one o’ those waddies your pardner?” Barnhill asked, looking at the strangers. “No,” Bern puzzled. “I thought he’d be the first one out—oh, there he comes now. Take a slant at the crippled waddie in the doorway.” Barnhill took a slant—and continued at it, stupefied and spineless. “That’s my partner—the ‘T’ part of the T. L. brand,” Bern informed him dryly. “Smart boy; fell down a seventy-five-foot prospect-hole after being hit on the head with a rock, and had the sense to land on the carcass of a Flying Y cow; only broke his collar-bone and had a bad headache.” From the back platform Thad Wyman kissed his fingers coquettishly at Barnhill and paved the way for friendly gossip: “Next time you write to Goren just mention my name an’ tell him that I ap- prove his confession in all respects—even though he did overrate himself as a thorough workman. I was in the bunk-room rubbin’ horse liniment on my head while he an’ Bern were millin’ things over in the kitchen at B. W. Remember it took five gunmen, workin’ overtime, to clean up on Henry Madrigal, an’ while I know I ain’t in his class, still I think you insulted me by sehdin’ only one-” “Meanin’ just what?” demanded Hal Currie, who headed the gathering crowd that swarmed near the caboose steps to bid Thad “welcome home.” The question stung Barnhill’s somnolent powers of locomotion into vigorous activity. The defunct B. W. partner, now present and too much alive, was bound by no pledge of secrecy; and the deputy’s query would, he astutely surmised, bring disclosures in no wise calculated to increase his popularity. He pawed into the throng—there being no other way to escape—with an eye to the two hundred yards of clear space separating the hotel from the stock pens. “Meanin’,” Thad grinned cheerfully as he saw “Balmy” Summers of the Army (R. Some one howled “tar,” to which there was a response, a roar, of “feathers.” Then “a rail” and the bedlam of shouts that characterizes a mob in hot pursuit of its victim. Bern leaped to the platform, beside Thad; watched Hal Currie, with an amazing burst of speed, outstrip the crowd and hurl his portly bulk at Barnhill. They crashed to earth. Above the noise and clamor rose Currie’s voice, shrill with authority and excitement: “Lay off ’im. In the morning when SA/arSs the drowsy sour doughs rolled out from their bear-skin robes they saw the Yukon lying silent and rigid. Invisible fingers had stilled the push and grind of passing ice cakes, building a winding, jagged causeway a thousand miles to the Bering. Another nine months of isolation would pass before the river would tear loose its're- straining ice fetters. From the chill barrens to the north, shrieking winds came scurrying with bur- dens of driving snow. Among the black spruce thickets the narrow trails showed white, crawling tortuously through pass and over summit. Men shivered and began to repair their dog harnesses and snowshoes. But up the river, two miles above the little string of cabins that comprises Tanana, and just below where the Tanana River throws its gritty flood into the Yukon, the frost king had been foiled. Through some prank of the current, which at this point foams against the upper end of Bull Island, the ice cakes, jostling each other on their way to the sea, had been swirled aside, and when the grinding procession had halted, a large stretch remained open, skirting the island and extending out a considerable dis- tance from shore. It was directly across the river from the village of that supersti- tious tribe, the Tananas. Oksook, the old medicine-man, peered out over the river at the distant dark stretch, shook his matted head ominously and muttered of bad signs. But the young bucks laughed. Who be- lieved Oksook? His medicine had lost its power. The medicine-man of the Kan- tishnas had cast a spell over Oksook. He had better go off and die. The old man chewed his toothless gums angrily and crept into his dirty little hovel. How things had changed! Oksook was not an Indian. His was a parentage of a burly Russian soldier and an Eskimo squaw. He had spent his fatherless youth and early manhood on the desolate salt marshes of the Yukon delta. What a terrible struggle for existence it had been, searching the bliz- zard-swept floes for seal and walrus, and crouching through the long Winters in stinking igloos. The hardships had forged him into a powerful, sinewy hunter who threw a harpoon with accuracy and force. Then had come the gold-strike up the Yukon at Dawson. Thousands of white men came hurrying north to the Island of St. Michael. Great river-boats were built to carry the passengers and freight up-river. It was on one of these boats that Oksook got a job as pilot and came to leave the dis- mal wastes by the Bering. There were no eyes keener than Oksook’s to detect snags and sand-bars beneath the roily waters. Time brought him far up the Yukon to 128Oksook 129 the Tananas. Disease and whisky had not yet contaminated this sturdy tribe, and Oksook found the lithe, slender squaws much to his liking. They were not like the fat, greasy Eskimo women on the coast. With his new manners and air of bravado picked up from the boisterous boat crews he soon ingratiated himself into the favor of the tribe. Cunning and patience inherited from his Slav father gained him fame and prestige. He came to be known among the various tribes on the Yukon and Tanana rivers as Oksook, the Great Medicine-Man. Month after month, year after year he sat in his little cabin among his pots and bags of herbs, brewing, muttering and sleeping, while the hunters brought him the fattest salmon and sweetest moose livers. In re- turn for these favors Oksook made charms so that when the countless hordes of caribou migrated north to their Winter feeding- grounds in the Koyokuk they always crossed the Yukon near the village of the Tananas. Always it was his medicine that filled the caches with fish. Therefore as he grew old and fat Oksook smiled and was happy. But there came a Summer when the salmon changed the course of their run and avoided the squeaking fish-wheels. of the Tananas. Farther up the river the drying racks of the Kantishna tribe swayed under their heavy loads of oily fish. Soon Oksook’s tribesmen began to murmur and criticise. Day after day he stewed and concocted strong medicines, but the dipping baskets of the wheels splashed idly and brought up nothing—a few small whitefish and gray- ling. When Fall came the caribou followed a new ridge north and 'crossed the river far above the Tananas. The Kantishnas laughed gleefully and boasted of strong medicine as they traded their fish and meat to the Tananas for fox and beaver-skins. Now came also a noisy little missionary, who told them that Oksook’s medicine was fraud and Oksook was ruined. Thus it was that the old native became the joke of the village and an object at which the boys threw sticks and the squaws scolded when he begged for bits of meat and cast-off moccasins. Each year as he, starved and became more wretched the old man grew more bitter and morose. He had long since ceased making medicine, but every morning left the village with its vici- ous urchins and went down the trail to Tanana where he loitered about the saloons and stores. Often he met white men who had known him when he had been famous, and sometimes they gave him money and whisky. But this Winter promised to be long and hard and the old medicine-man felt uneasy when at night he heard the malemiuts mourn with their pointed noses toward the green sky. J\ ALL day it had been snowing vtgk heavily and with the darkness had come a bitter wind that sobbed down the chimneys and frightened little puffs of smoke out through the stove cracks. The big, round stove in Mickey’s Place glowed in furious competition with the fiery whisky that burned the throats of Mickey’s customers. This night the talk had taken a strange turn. Nugget Bill, a squaw-man, was speaking to the men who clustered about him in deep interest. “Yep,” he was saying, “that bunch o’ Siwashes up at the village are scared to death. They heered a noise three different times last night, before the storm started, across the river on the island by the air hole, but they can’t make out what it was. They said it sounded like a bull moose snortin’ about twenty times as loud and jest once only at a time. A couple of young bucks went over this mornin’ but she was snowin’ so hard that the tracks must been all cov- ered over. They won’t any of them go over any more ’cause they’ve got that old hunch about spirits and that stuff.” The men all laughed boisterously. In- dian superstition was always a topic of much debate and ridicule. “Why don’t they get old Oksook on the job?” laughed a big trapper. “They all used to hand it to him before the salmon wept back on him.” -‘By the way,” interrupted one of them, “where is the old boy? I haven’t seen him today. They’re gettin’ to treat him pretty bad up there. Something ought to be done. He wasn’t a bad old fellow before his medicine gave out. He’s gave many a fellow a square.” “I seed him with the Scurvy Kid today,” replied Bill. “Scurvy’s got a stake. He and Oksook used to be pretty good friends. I guess he’s gettin’ him drunk.”130 Adventure “Wal the old boy used to pull some pretty good stuff at that. He had ’em all cornin’ his way f,or a while.” But Oksook was not drunk. He was crouching over the battered stove in his miserable cabin up at the village. A twenty dollar gold-piece given him by Scurvy still fested in his pocket. He was absorbed in gloomy reflections. Today an old Indian had come chattering with fear to tell him of the dreadful noises that had been heard on Bull Island. The tale had puzzled Oksook, but with devilish cunning he had painted to the superstitious old buck an evil picture. No doubt it was the great magoona which had come to the water-hole to drink. The native knew the magoona to be a terrible creature. Mas- todons the white men called them. Only a few miles down the river was a high sandy bluff, known by the sour doughs as the bone-yard, from which protruded scores of gigantic bones. Often as the natives hurried by this ill-omened spot in their canoes they wondered what must have been the awful ferocity of these pon- derous brutes. A magoona was no animal to trifle with. From a mind inflamed with bitter re- sentment and years of morbid reflection, old Oksook portrayed to the shivering In- dian a frightful vision of gruesome monsters stalking down from the vast unknown bar- rens to the north to visit disaster and death upon villages, over which the protecting influence of a medicine-man was not ex- tended. The old buck had slunk from- the cabin to tell his fellows of this grisly thing hidden in the dense thickets across the river. With the coming of darkness the frightened vil- lagers huddled together to spend a sleepless night. Left alone in the hut Oksook had found it difficult to shake off the mood into which he had worked himself. What could that noise have been? He regretted that he had not heard it. Perhaps it really was some terrible creature wandered down from the wastes above the arctic rim. No man knew what the inhospitable stretches of this un- explored region contained. If only the blizzard would raise its concealing curtain of snow and cease its confusing din. He thought longingly of the reviving whisky to be had down at Mickey’s Place. But on opening the door a whirling cloud of powdery snow enveloped him and he hastily shut it again. It was no weather for an old man to be on the trail. Not alone the Siwashes, however, were destined to be frightened by the mysterious noise this night. Steadily the scream of the winds increased as they filled the air with hissing snow. Down on the unbroken stretch of the Yukon an inferno of flying ice streamed and roared between the high bluffs. Little life stirred abroad. Even the woolly malemiut curled his bushy tail over his nose and forgot to howl. But there was one man on the trail who fled before the frosty hurricane and prayed that he might soon reach shelter. He was Pat Riley, the mail-driver from Fairbanks to Tanana. He was late. The trail had been soft with drifting snow and both he and the dogs were fagged. It was with gladness that he sensed the steep sloping of the trail where it began its plunge down on to the Yukon. Two miles more and he would be at the little store which also served as Tanana’s post-office. He paused to get his bearings before mak- ing the dash across the howling confusion below. When he had gone up to Fairbanks just after freeze-up he had seen the open stretch of water near the trail. Tonight he would have to be careful because the ter- rific wind would tend to crowd the dogs to- ward the opening. Ice had formed on his eyelashes and he drew his hands from his warm mittens to thaw it loose. Once on the river it would be a grim struggle till the other bank was reached.Oksook 131 "Mush Kitty.” The little malemiut straightened out the tow-line and led the team down the steep descent to the river. As the dogs and man emerged from the sheltering willow thicket that lined the beach the storm caught them and drove its stinging blasts of sharp crystals under clothing and into fur. Shriek- ing forces tore and buffeted them threaten- ing every moment to fling them helpless along the wild course. The winds came not with a steady violence but seemed to crouch, to hesitate, and then hurled themselves for- ward with redoubled fury. During these intervals only could Riley see the bushy tail of his wheel dog. Twice he stopped the team, went forward and peered into the whirling blackness ahead; but the flying snow had obliterated the trail, and struggling low against the hurricane to regain the sleigh he cursed the fates that had left the river open. It was Kitty who gave warning of the hole. The little leader stopped and whirled snapping upon the dog behind her. A few feet ahead was an engulfing flood into which the drifting snow sank and disappeared. Riley staggered forward to where the black waters gushed out from beneath the heavy ice. He stooped and patted the dog. “Good girl Kitty. Good girl.” A slight lull had come as if the storm foiled in its design was contemplating new mis- chief. From across the rushing stream the moan of the storm in the spruce-trees swelled up against the wind. Then just before the wind-sirens renewed their frenzied screaming, Riley heard a noise that sent tiny electrical shocks shooting up his spine into the roots of his hair. He seemed almost to feel it. A ghastly, surging sob through the quivering darkness from the direction of the island. The man threw up his forearm and shrank back as if lashed at with a blacksnake. He stumbled among the malemiuts who snapped viciously from where they cowered shivering and whining. Riley was Irish and had guts, but he also had superstitions. In all his years on the trail he had never before heard a sound like that. It was a sound of furious anguish, an unnatural sound, a terrifying sound, “Haw—Kitty! Haw I /ell yuh!” he screamed. A stinging lash ripped time and time again into the faces of the groveling dogs. Kitty scurried frantically in a half-circle and the heavy sleigh whirled and swayed out over the jagged ridges of ice; away from that place, away from that sound. It was near closing time in the saloon and the boisterous talk had subsided to brief monosyllables while the men shuffled and dealt the cards. Only one or two noticed Riley’s silent entry and it was not until the mail-driver had raised a shaking glass to his lips, that one of the players nudged his neighbor and said in an undertone— “Thought Pat didn’t hit the hooch any more.” As the man twisted his head to look, the remainder of the men turned their eyes toward the bar. “Have a tough trip, Riley?” one of them inquired. “Yeh,” replied Riley shortly as he reached once more for the bottle. The men sensed a change in his manner but did not inquire further, resuming the game somewhat reluctantly. Soon the mail-driver came over and stood watching the game silently. They had paused for a new deal when Riley asked very casually— “Any you fellows been up past the island lately?” The men glanced at one another queerly, but no one spoke until at last Nugget Bill retorted seemingly for the entire group: “No. Why?” ‘‘Oh—nothing. Thought I heard a queer noise as I went by tonight, but I guess it must have been the storm.” There was only the irregular clicking of fingers playing with poker-chips until Bill again spoke. “My wife was tellin’ me today that the Indians heered a noise up there las^ night, but they can’t figure out what it was.” “It must have been the same thing I heard,” returned Riley quickly. “Men, I’ve been in this country twenty years but I’m tellin’ you this was a new one 011 me. Even the dogs laid down on me and wouldn’t budge till I’d beat half the hide off ’em. She got my goat for a while.” “Couldn’t been a moose, could it Pat?” inquired one of the men seriously after a moment of deep silence. “No! No! I’m tellin’ yuh it was dif- ferent than anything I ever heard before.132 Adventure It didn’t sound right somehow. Kind of creepy like, but I can’t describe it exactly ’cause the wind picked up right after it started.” A few moments of puzzled silence again absorbed the group. No one doubted the solemn Irishman. When a teetotaler takes to drink something had disturbed him tremendously. With an ever-watchful eye for oppor- tunities, Mickey, the bartender, called them up to the bar where the seriousness of the topic was manifested by a businesslike clatter of glasses. EARLY morning came, and with a vast consumption of stimulants the origin of the disturbing noise ever increased in fantasy and' supernaturalism. Nothing definite could be decided on. It was finally resolved that when the blizzard ceased, an expedition should go through the thick brush on the island and endeavor to learn the cause of this perplexing mystery. Thus the matter stood when a last round of drinks was consumed before the men shuf- fled off to their cabins. Strange to say one or two wood-choppers, who lived across the river, decided to stay overnight at the little hotel. “She’ll be too cold to cut wood in the mornin’,” they explained sheepishly. But with daylight the storm grew worse. Even old French Joe, who upon any occa- sion could always remember a past situation of double the intensity, sat silent and awed by this terrific display of weather forces. Excursions to outlying wood-piles assumed the hardships of polar exploration. Never had such a blizzard been seen on the Yukon. However, in spite of the mountainous heaps of shifting snow and blinding flurries that whipped over exposed portions of the trail, early forenoon saw Oksook wallowing on his ancient snowshoes down to Tanana. The burning possession of a twenty dollar gold-piece and a morbid night of superstiti- ous wakefulness had driven him out of his gloomy cabin to seek companionship. Upon his arrival at the little cluster of half-buried buildings he lost no time in spending part of his money for a generous bottle of bootleg whisky. Neither Indians nor half-breeds could buy liquor over the bar in Tanana, but a detour to the back door always resulted in a flaskful of a mur- derous concoction prepared only for natives. The news of the fearful noises coming from Bull Island and their terrorizing effect on the Indians in the village had spread quickly among Tanana’s few inhabitants. Thus because of his once having been medi- cine-man, Oksook found himself the object of continual banter and some ridicule. At first he took it good-naturedly, smiling sagely to himself; but as the day wore on and he drank more and more of his flask, his good humor changed to irritation and anger which did not decrease the sport but served rather to aggravate it to mockery. This continued more or less throughout the day until evening came with its more boister- ous gathering in the saloons. He retorted to his tormentors with snarls of wounded vanity until at last unable to withstand any longer the continuous attack, he staggered from a chair in the barroom where he had been sitting and shouted frantically to his revilers. “Me show you. Me show you. Oksook he make strong medicine. He stop beeg noise on Bull Island.” With this he stumbled out into the night followed by a great hubbub of drunken jeers and laughter. Outside the door he swal- lowed the remainder of his liquor and crashed the bottle against the heavy logs of the building. Even in his passion-befuddled mind he realized that he had made a fool of himself. Now because of his insane boast he would be jeered and laughed at also among the white men. With the fumes of the poison- ous whisky lashing him to a frenzy of des- pair and anguish he bowed his head into the storm and followed a heedless direction which led him not up the trail to the village, but down on to the wind-scourged surface of the river. No one but a drunken Eskimo could have beaten his way up against that freezing storm. He followed no straightway course, but buffeted from side to side by the blasts, slipped and floundered among the troughs of powdery crystals between the clean-swept ice-ridges. The worn webbing on his old snow shoes soon parted leaving the useless frames dangling unheeded about his knees. He did not feel the bitter winds nor suffer from the stinging particles; his mind para- lyzed from the liquor served but one pur- pose, to impel him forward and forward. But the tremendous heart action induced by the stimulant began to fail the old man.Oksook 133 Gradually his limbs commenced to shake and his pounding brain to cool. As his deadened faculties came back to conscious- ness he became aware of a sinister blackness close to his right. It was the open water by Bull Island. With the immunity of a sleep-walker he had been staggering along its jagged edge. For a time he stood peering vacantly out over the murky expanse. No recollection of1 the terrifying mystery disturbed his half-conscious study. The waters seemed strangely soft and inviting. He felt an overpowering desire to sink into their soothing depths, out of the biting winds. Therb was something hypnotic about the white flakes that silently sought the dark surface to sink gently out of view. Lurching forward he stood swaying uncer- tainly over the very rim. As he watched, the river appeared suddenly to rise before him. Out of the depths was emerging a black, glistening body from which a pair of luminous eyes stared malevolently. Before he could draw back a gushing roar “Shook the air, beating into his ears an old familiar sound of his youth, a sound that Oksook knew and loved. His shout of joy was drowned in a, shower of freezing water. “Ot-si-kee!” he yelled in Eskimo. “She blows! She blows!” It was the old rallying cry of the whale- hunt. Then for a moment he stood silent, watch- ing the black body as it skirted the rim, disappearing at length in the darkness. In spite of the intense cold a warm feeling of joy diffused his benumbed frame. Once more he would be Oksook, the Great Man, a man to be respected, a man to be deferred to. He must capture the great fish this very night before the secret be discovered by any one else. How the whale happened to be so far from its natural habitat did not perplex him; his years as pilot on the lower river told him that. So preoccupied was he with his plans that on his way back across the river he did not feel the biting wind nor mind the rough going. In fact the storm seemed to be diminishing in violence. Once in his cabin he hastily sought an old bundle brought with him when he first came among the Tananas. From it he drew a wicked looking harpoon head to which was attached a long, heavy, native whale- line. It was still in good condition. As he fitted a shaft into the barbed steel he hummed a weird old hunting-chant of his youth, the days when he had been the best harpooner of the tribe. Several times he flourished the dangerous weapon at imagi- nary objects and was surprized to find his arm still retained its former skill. Then arming himself with his rifle and ax he started back to the opening. The furious winds had dwindled to a few spent whispers when Oksook again reached the place where he had seen the whale. Overhead above the scudding clouds, a flat moon disk glanced palely through ragged fringes. A dim light was diffused over the water, but the whale was nowhere to be seen. Without waiting, however, for it to ap- pear Oksook began to prepare for its cap- ture. About midway between the ex- tremities of the opening he selected near the edge a large ice chunk which he hewed down to the semblance of a stump. Around this he wound his line, leaving enough free that he might throw his harpoon a con- siderable distance. This done he laid his rifle and ax near and awaited his oppor- tunity. Long years of hunting on the ice floes in the Bering stood him in good stead. With Eskimo patience he waited hour after hour for the great creature to appear. Once it rose with a high spout, but it was far over near the island, and as it sank once more Oksook wondered how long it would be before morning would bring the Indians and white men to share in the capture and thus deprive him of the sole honor. The malemiuts in the village had begun their morning chorus when Oksook again noted the swirl in the water that betrayed the presence of his prey. It was rising near the rim and on its way up-stream would pass directly by. Closer and closer the glistening back came, gliding swiftly and easily. It would be an easy throw if his arm did not fail him. His first nervousness was passing off. He drew back his arm tensely, leaned forward and then hurled the harpoon into the black form as it surged past. The instantaneous whip of the heavy line almost upset him, but he retained a hold on the snub end. A terrific splashing and floundering turned the whole morning into confusion. Great waves of bloody spray came lashing up on to the ice. He did not dare to let the line out134 Adventure because he knew he could not pull the broad hulk up against the swift current. Had the'whale been in the prime of its strength the line would never have held, but weakened by the lack of food and the effects of fresh water, the beast could not exert its normal power and therefore soon tired. Watching his chance, Oksook secured the line and held his rifle ready to shoot when the spray should clear. Gradually the mighty lashings grew feebler and feebler, until the waves subsiding, he saw the black hide awash. As the loud reports of the heavy caliber rifle echoed and re-echoed between the bluffs a fresh commotion shook the water, but this time it was shorter. Again the enormous body grew still and drifted over against the rim of ice. To in- sure his catch Oksook took his ax and sank it deep into the back, severing the giant vertebrae. A distant shout from the village drew his attention. Down on 'to the white surface of the river was streaming in a black line the entire Tanana tribe. “Oksook! Oksook! What is it?” he heard them cry. The old man cackled grimly. He knew none of them had ever seen a fish larger than the king salmon which blundered into their fish-wheels. “Look!” he said calmly. “Can the Kan- tishnas catch fish like Oksook?” Murmurs of awe and astonishment rose from squaws and bucks alike when they saw the ferocious-looking creature that Oksook had conquered. They eyed with shudders the blood where the great tail had lashed the gory water up on the ice. Truly Oksook was a mighty man. “Go,” commanded Oksook authoritively, “and get lines to pull the fish up on the ice. I will prepare medicine for a great potlach,” he added and turned his back upon the awe- stricken natives. Soon came also the white men to look and wonder. Many were the speculations as to how the whale should come to be a thousand miles up the river; but it was Oksook who told his old friend the Scurvy Kid. “Long time ago,” he said, “me pilot beeg boat on lower river. When tide he back up into river, whale he come up to play and sometimes he get in front of boat. Many times we chase him long way, but when boat stop for wood he turn back. Dis Fell boats he start to burn oil, no stop for wood. Whale he come all way up.”Author of “Flying Cannon,” “Groody Moves Onetc. NE by one the officers of the Mechanics’ School at Donovan Field straggled over to the mess hall. The song, in a manner of speaking, was of a vulgar nature, as Slim frequently admit- ted. Other men, like little A1 Johnson, greeted the flooding sunlight of a new Texas day with eyes yet dull with slumber plus the effects of a large night. Hashaway Lewis, chocolate-colored cook extraordinary, kept a wary eye on the officers’ quarters. Whenever one of the flyers hove into sight around the corner Hashaway took careful aim at a frying-pan with two eggs, and fired without delay. His assistant, the totally black and enter- tainingly useless Taylor, hustled grape- fruit, toast and coffee on the table, under Hashaway’s prodding, before the prospec- tive consumer thereof had entered the door. Taylor’s claim to fame rested on the habit he had acquired of rolling a continuous row of sevens in the crap games down at the corral. Nearly all the bachelor officers had pro- gressed somewhat with their breakfasts when Sleepy Spears staggered in, arrayed hastily in a bath-robe that was a veteran among negligees. His square, good-na- tured face needed shaving, and his thick brown hair was in a tall and uncombed con- dition; but his eyes showed bright and clear beneath the drooping lids. “The sun is shining—it’s a wonder you even bothered to get up, Sleepy,” remarked Evans, attacking his bacon and eggs with gusto. Spears, whose perpetual lack of energy during daylight hours was proverbial, dropped into a chair, and the slow, wide grin that was the Spears trademark spread over his face. “Had a hard night last night,” he said gently. “I’ll say you did,” corroborated A1 "Johnson, who didn’t look as if his own had been so peaceful. Spears looked hard at his grapefruit. “Taylor, you Ethiopian reptile, you’ve le^t half the core in this thing,” he stated without heat. Taylor flashed a dumfounding double row of ivory, and carried the grapefruit back to the kitchen, chuckling delightedly. He always chuckled at Spears. “What were you doing—studying some of those correspondence-school courscs?” queried Jack Carruthers, who was head of the motor department. “No,” replied Spears, leaning a wearied head on his elbow. 135136 Adventure In his fits of ambition, which overcame him periodically, he had invested in many pounds of books on subjects covering a very wide range. The boots are over in my quarters. Hop to it.” The side-splitting hilarity of the dusky Taylor followed him out of the door. He stuck one hand in his bath-robe pocket as he strolled toward the quarters, and felt some- thing soft and folded. He pulled it out and spread it. It was a ten-dollar bill. He had no idea in the world how it had found its way into his pocket; but it was there, and he was not one to look a gift horse in the mouth. He had a habit of finding money in every pocket. He made his way to the bath-house and shaved leisurely. The drone of the air- planes overhead reverberated inside the building as the business of Donovan Field proceeded without the aid of one Lieutenant John Spears. The feel of the lukewarm water on him as he stepped under the shower impelled him to sing, as it always did. In a totally tuneless voice he burst forth into a verse of one of the few songs that he knew: “I used to loop and spin with all my m-i-g-h-t, I thought I’d be a flying star and s-t-u-n-t; Oh, once I yearned to knock ’em dead—my good ship and m-e. Though I was wild, thank God this child was s-a-v-e-d!” He ceased for a moment, to add a little more cold water to the mixture. He al- ways eased himself into a cold shower by degrees. The insistent tinny drumming of the phone reached his ears. He had an idea it was probably for him, but as the water grew colder he burst forth discordant- ly, though with enthusiasm, into the chorus: “Oh, g-l-o-r-y, my friends, I was s-a-v-e-d. I never hit the ground too hard in my good s-h-i-p; I’d rather have white whiskers than die y-o-u-n-g; Give other guys the glory—I’m still a-l-i-v-e!” He turned the warm water completely off and began to dance briskly under a cold, stinging flood. During the brisk rub-down that followed, with a big, rough towel marked with the name of a prominent San Antonio hotel, he gave tongue to the song of the border-patrol flyers, who daily cover the wildest section of the country, from the Gulf of Mexico to its sister gulf on the western rim. “Ten thousand dollars, going to the folks; Ten thousand dollars-” Again he became aware of the telephone and stopped singing to devote attention strictly to business. He lighted a cigaret, taking frequent puffs between the various maneuvers connected with arraying himself for the morning. His room was a mass of conglomerate junk, not yet ordered by the skilful hands of Taylor and his high-yellow wife, who acted as chambermaid, her husband supervising. In one corner, covered by a brown blanket to make the bulge in the room slightly more esthetic, were two huge boxes, unopened, which held complete correspondence courses on banking and mechanical engineering respectively. The shelf above the table held two vividly red volumes authored by Boccaccio and Rabelais, a couple of technical works on airplanes, two represen- tatives of Robert W. Chambers, the Medita- tions of Marcus Aurelius and a whole set of O. Henry. On the table a pile of magazines had lost equilibrium and flooded over all available space, a Police Gazette having reached the floor. The beaver-board walls were cov- ered with pictures hung without particular method. Between two of Frederic Rem- ington’s nestled a framed reproduction from La Vie Parisienne; and some beautiful cloud pictures, photographed from the air, disputed an entire wall with a revealing row of stage beauties. Spears was finally ready to sally forth. He ambled slowly up the road that ran along the line of hangars, stretching for a half-mile along the rim of Field One from the bachelor quarters to the row of build- ings on the northern edge, which included headquarters and the enlisted men’s barracks. A fifteen-dollar, made-to-order shirt that he wore was slightly wrinkled as a result of lying in a corner for several hours. His twenty-dollar Stetson was tilted over his eyes, dented individualistically according to a plan that suited Sleepy, although com- plying in no detail with the uniform regu- lations made and provided. The black cravat was loosely knotted, and there was a considerable interval between it and the. shirt collar. Sleepy walked along as if every step would be his last. The wide shoulders sloped as if burdened with unutterable weariness, and his arms swung loosely at his sides. It took a very close observer to notice that there was a certain effortless smoothness about that seemingly tired walk—one move- ment merged into the next with a panther- like effect of sinewy, rippling strength. To his left, westward, stretched the flat, gray, two-mile square that was Donovan Field. Field One, domicile of the school that trained Air Service rookies to be trained airplane riggers, motor mechancis, para- chute experts, and what not, was a half-mile long and half as wide, the eastern edge a solid line of hangars. Separated from it by a line of unused barracks and the Rio road, Field Two lay huge and level, merging into mesquite on its western and southern sides. The north- ern edge was a mile and a half of big white hangars, paralleled by equally trim lines of barracks, machine-shops, store-houses and officers’ quarters. Above the field the darting little scouts of the pursuit group dodged in and out among the lazily floating billows of snowy mist that were growing ever larger as the heat increased. A thundering formation of Liberty-motored De Havilands from the bombardment group roared its majestic way above the field, its unvarying regu- larity testifying to the flying ability of the pilots. Handling a ton-and-a-half ship and keeping it twenty-five feet, no more and no less, from the ship ahead and in precise position in three dimensions is no task for an amateur. Spears slowly passed the flying-depart- ment hangars. A dozen ships of different types were lined up on the edge of the field, ranging from the tiny Sopwith Camel up to a big twin-motored bomber. The me- chanics, dressed uniformly in coveralls, were busily wiping down the ships in prep- aration for the half-holiday. Dick Cart- mell waved and jeered as Sleepy made his leisurely way past the bench, and Beale, who was in helmet and goggles for a flight, grinned at him cheerfully. The next group of hangars housed the140 Adventure airplane department, where riggers were manufactured from raw youngsters who sought to learn the technique of a still new profession. Sleepy straightened his hat as he entered the first big structure, and looked around him keenly as he walked the length of it. All around were groups of students, each under an instructor, who were doing everything from doping linen to putting the final touches on a big De Haviland which that particular class had assembled and rigged. Sleepy grinned in reminiscent compla- cency at the memory of the hard days of toil he had put in shaping the department so that it was self-running. The task of stealing hard-boiled old Sergeant Correll from the engineering department to act as personal representative, disciplinary non- com, and general energizer had been ex- hausting in itself. The sergeant’s huge, portly figure was ensconced comfortably in its wonted chair in the office as Spears entered. “Here’s that request for cross-country, Lieutenant,” he said, and shoved the type- written missive over to Spears’ uncluttered desk. Spears signed it without reading it.. Two typewriters clicked busily under the flying fingers of uniformed young men who were ever mindful of the near presence of Correll. “All the reports out?” queried Spears without particular interest. “Signed and gone. I put my initials under your name inasmuch as you weren’t here,” returned Correll. “Good. Say, better take this request up to headquarters yourself. Give it to Lieu- tenant Morris, and when it’s approved go down to the flying department with it and see that the Le Pere is ready to go at twelve, will you?” The sergeant snapped his finger. “Get my hat out there on the rack,” he commanded the orderly, “and stick by the phone this morning while I’m gone. I may need you.” Sleepy relaxed in his swivel chair, one foot on the desk. His eyes followed the ser- geant through the window as he strode up the road toward headquarters. Ever since that night when he had made a night para- chute jump and with Al Johnson’s help at the end prevented a tar-and-feather party at the sergeant’s expense Correll had been his right-hand man for any and all purposes. The fight had been very bothersome and had spoiled his evening in Kannville with a certain creamy blonde, reflected Sleepy, but on the whole it was well worth it. He lit a cigaret and pushed his weirdly dented hat back on his head. He leaned forward and grasped the telephone, leaning back again as far as the chair would go. He must call up Major Stratton and tell him he had just stepped back in again, and then he’d catch up on a little slumber be- fore the trip to Gonzara. n	\ THE wicked-looking, low-hung Le Pere was the only ship left on the line at noon. With sturdy wheel- blocks under the wheels, a mechanic on each wing and another on the tail, Correll was warming up the big twelve-cylinder Liberty motor. Sleepy waited until the soft hum of idling grew into the drumming roar which an- nounced that it was running wide open on the final warm-up, and then left his quarters ,to walk the few hundred yards to the line. Correll had already been down after his suit-case, and he noted that the bag was wired on the wing, close to the fuselage. Everything was in readiness. Correll slowly eased the throttle back until the propeller was turning slowly be- fore he climbed out of the front cockpit. Sleepy adjusted his goggles—he wore no helmet—and stuffed cotton in his ears. It was not necessary to ask whether the motor was O. K.—Correll’s silent transfer of seats told the story. The pilot surveyed the crowded instru- ment-board casually as he adjusted the buckle of the wide canvas belt. Oil pres- sure, thirty; air pressure, three; tempera- ture, 70° Centigrade; and voltmeter charg- ing—all was as it should be. He nodded to the mechanics, and they jerked away the wheel-blocks. “All ready, sergeant?” he yelled. Correll waved assent, and Sleepy taxied up the field. The trip was a hundred and forty miles. He figured they would get there in an hour, which would be one o’clock, and just the time that Gib Saunders would be expecting them. He turned around and slowly fed the throttle. The Le Pere gathered speed rapidly, and in a sdcond was speeding acrossRiding Herd- the ground like a bullet. Slow pressure on the stick brought the tail up, and almost before he knew it they were in the air. He held the ship close to the ground for a minute, and then lifted it upward in a beau- tiful 300m that brought them to three hundred feet. The air was very bumpy, and even the heavy, solid Le Pere was bounced around in the air currents as it circled the field three times, getting altitude. At two thousand feet Spears flew over San Antonio, carefully picking out the railroads leading eastward. He verified the one leading to Gonzara by means of a small stream which his map showed running parallel to it. Five miles farther on a fork in the tracks, the southern branch leading to the Gulf of Mexico, was additional veri- fication that he was right, so he settled back comfortably in his seat. Usually flying was merely mechanical with him. There was no particular pleas- ure in it—just sitting there until he got where he wanted to go. Today, however here was a certain satisfaction in it that vas unusual. The swift rush of the air past him was cool and comfortable in contrast to the baking land below, and as the Le Pere hurled itself through the misty lower edges of cloud after cloud the feel of the cool moisture was the acme of physical delight. The Le Pere was the pride of the school— one of the only two of the type on Donovan Field. It was immeasurably faster than any other ship on the field—it could fly circles around a D. H., and left Fokkers, S. E. 5s and even the powerful little 220 Spad hopelessly out of the running. Like a majority of the American flyers, it was a distinct event for Sleepy to get his hands on a real ship. He had not flown anything but frail, clumsy De Havilands or rebuilt British and German scout planes for so long that flying renewed its joy for him as he surveyed the trim, strong wings, without dihedral or overhang, and the solid laminated fuselage, so different from what he had been accustomed to since leaving France. Obeying a sudden impulse, born of his whole-souled flyer’s delight in flying an honest-to-goodness ship for once, he throttled the motor and nosed down. The strongly built ship would stand it almost as well as a Spad, so he allowed the air-speed meter to go up to a hundred and eighty miles an hour the while he carefully handled the •Modern Style	141 throttle until, despite their terrific speed, the 450 horse-power Liberty was only turn- ing fifteen hundred revolutions. Like a meteor the big black-and-white ship shot toward the sundrenched earth two thousand feet below. It was a smiling country of alternate flat cotton-fields and small patches of mesquite, splotched with shadow as the snowy cumulus clouds drifted across the sun. Correll, in the black cockpit, leaned over anxiously and treated the instrument- board, which he could see from his seat, to a careful inspection. There was nothing wrong there, and the motor was certainly firing evenly and without missing, so the portly mechanic leaned back again and devoted all his attention to blowing out his ears to keep the change in pressure from deafening him. Every few seconds he grasped his nose firmly, kept his mouth closed, and blew hard, Spears following his example as they sped ever nearer the ground. They were diving parallel with the rail- road, and at two hundred feet Sleepy grad- ually came out of the dive, easing the throttle on slowly as he came level. At fifty feet, directly over the railroad, the ship was flying in normal position, the motor turning 1550. Spears leaned forward and opened the shutters, which he had closed for the dive to keep the motor from cooling too fast. Then he settled down to fly the railroad— every curve of it. At a hundred and forty miles an hour, a few feet above the wires, the Le Pere roared its way down the railroad. The sensation of speed, almost indiscernible a thousand feet or more high, was now paramount. The little town of Carston heard the ever increasing roar of the motor, and stirred from its midday lethargy to sweep the sky with languid interest. It grew into as- tonished wonder as a black-and-white meteor shot across the housetops, the tiny railroad station and on down the railroad so swiftly, “Here she comes!” followed by, “There she goes!” were about the only com- ments a reasonably intelligent and quick- witted person could encompass before the Le Pere was gone. Sleepy, his attitude still relaxed as he guided the mighty machine beneath him, was smiling slightly, and the lazy gray eyes were glowing softly. It was the kind of142 Adventure flying that has killed many a pilot, for chances of a crack-up in case of motor failure were very promising. However, it was the expression of a spirit without which there would never have been a conquest of the air; scientists may experiment, but some one must take a chance. Ten minutes of flashing progress along the shining steel rails left the motor at 8o° Centigrade—not too hot. A stretch of big, level cotton-flelds on his right tempted Sleepy to bank away from the railroad. He dropped down to five, and skimmed the tops of the cotton plants. When he reached a fence a tiny zoom took him over it. A cluster of houses loomed ahead, and with a world of excess speed he lifted the Le Pere in a smooth, curving zoom that cleared the houses by a hundred feet. He dropped down again for a wild rush across a straight half-mile of waving corn. A BIG, level stretch of closely cropped grass showed ahead. Thirty or more horses and mules were grazing in it. Sleepy obeyed a sudden impulse and dived along the eastern edge of the field, close to the fence. A dozen of the animals who were occupying that section kicked up their heels and departed hastily. Zooming up at the other end of the field, Sleepy noted that one mule had not re- treated far enough, so he shot down and herded the recalcitrant beast into the main bunch, along the western boundary. This accomplished, he circled back of the field a few hundred yards, slowly cut the throttle, and stalled over the fence, landing smoothly on three points. With a few quick thrusts of gas he turned around, taxied back to the fence, and pointed the nose down the field in preparation for the take-off. “What’s the trouble, lieutenant?” in- quired Correll anxiously. “Just wanted a smoke,” returned his chief, stretching mightily as he reached the ground. The sergeant took a full minute to digest this information. He grinned as he looked at Spears, now puffing contentedly as he lay on the ground. “Must be—er—feeling pretty good to- day, lieutenant,” he ventured. “Oh, not so bad, not so good,” replied Spears airily. “Light up—we haven’t got much time.” The ship was idling softly. The airmen lay restfully on the ground, undergoing the severe inspection of the horses and mules, who had retired to the farthermost limits of the big field. Spears moved a trifle to get in the breeze generated by the propeller, and then moved back again with a grunt of disgust as he noted that he would lose his cigaret if he remained there. He lay motionless, his arm over his eyes to keep out the blinding radiance of the noon-high sun. When he had inhaled the last possible supply of smoke from his diminutive cigaret- stub he sat up, yawned, and put on his goggles. Correll threw away his smoke and climbed in the rear. In a moment they were roaring across the field, taking the air before its length was half-covered. They flew straight down the railroad, climbing gradually, until once again they felt the cool air under the clouds against their faces. Spears shook the stick, and Correll leaned forward. Spears cut the motor for a moment as he yelled— “You fly it!” Correll nodded with much satisfaction. He could keep a ship level in the air all right, and took great delight in an oppor- tunity to demonstrate his ability. Spears passed his map back to the mechanic, and with his finger indicated their precise posi- tion. Correll nodded his comprehension. Spears settled deeply into his seat, so far behind the wind-shield that he scarcely felt any of the terrific air pressure compounded of their speed and the propeller’s blast. The motor was running well within its pos- sibilities at fifteen hundred revolutions. His arms rested limply on the cowling, and his eyes gazed calmly on the clouds drifting just above them. The roar of the motor, with its hypnotic monotony, lulled him into drowsiness and in a moment, sleep. For a wonder he awakened just before they arrived alt Gonzara. Sergeant Correll was just deciding that it was time to lean over and shake the pilot when Spears stirred and looked around. As they came over the fairly large town he saw a railroad crossing and recognized Gonzara. His wrist-watch showed 1:10. He was familiar with the landing-field, and quickly picked it up. The wide- spreading grove next to it—the location for the barbecue—was swarming with tiny, ant- like specks, and the smoke of the barbecueRiding Herd—Modern Style 143 fires, rising almost straight in the air, provided a good wind-vane. Evidently there was practically no wind on the ground, such as there was being from the south. The field itself ran north and south, which made it as convenient as could be wished for landing. Correll had climbed to five thousand feet on the way. Spears took the stick and pulled it back as far as it would go, applying left rudder at the same time. The nose came up until the ship was almost per- pendicular in the air, and then fell off to the left in a whirling dive. Spears cut the motor half-way—he did not throttle fully because that would foul the spark-plugs, and it was very possible that he would need a good motor before making his landing. The heavy Le Pere, with a thousand- pound motor in its nose running half-open, screwed downward in a terrific vertical tail- spin. Four hundred feet a turn, and the turns came so fast it was almost impossible to count them. Sleepy watched the ground calmly, oc- casionally blowing out his ears for com- fort’s sake. He kept an eye on the alti- meter, which was wabbling crazily in an effort to keep up with the rushing loss of altitude. Every wire was screaming with the speed, and Correll, experienced passenger though he was, was dizzy and sick as the big, flashily painted ship corkscrewed through the last thousand feet. Sleepy grinned slowly as he caught kaleidoscopic views of the crowd below, now motionless. “Some of ’em are getting the roofs of their mouths sunburnt,-’ he reflected as he jammed on right rudder and pushed the stick forward. A turn more, and the Le Pere straight- ened out in a vertical dive. It took four hundred feet before the nose started coming up as Sleepy eased the stick back slowly. It was not so easy to do against the force of the wind on the elevators. Three hundred feet above the ground the ship was level, and the motor came full on as Spears circled the long, narrow landing- field, eying it closely for possible ditches or crack-brained onlookers who might be in the way. Satisfied that Gib Saunders, or some one equally wise, was warning the crowd to keep away, he made a wide circle and approached the field in a slow dive from the north. 10 “Slow” in a Le Pere means around ninety miles an hour. The throttle crawled back by degrees until it was idling as the ship sped over the trees and dropped down until it was two feet above the ground. Know- ing the gliding propensities of his ship, Sleepy kicked the rudder and sent the plane into a skid that helped kill their speed. They were less than fifty yards from the end of the field when they stopped rolling. As he taxied toward the western edge of the field, which was packed with people who had temporarily deserted the barbecue, the pilot spied a dancing figure in the fore- ground that he recognized readily. Even at that distance there was no mistaking the red hair and somewhat portly form of ex-Lieutenant Gib Saunders. Sleepy taxied toward Saunders, stopping a few feet in front of the crowd that poured out behind the one-time pilot. “Burn out the motor; will you, Correll?” requested Spears. Correll climbed into the front seat and turned off one of the gas pet-cocks as Sleepy tumbled off the wing into the flailing arms of Saunders. “Not so vigorous, Gib, not so vigorous,” Spears cautioned with his widest grin as his freckle-faced, bright-eyed comrade of over- seas days seemed bent on knocking the life out of him with tremendous thumps on the back. “These folks don’t know whether this is a fight or merely an exhibition, you red-headed firebrand!” “Nor is it any of their business,” re- turned Saunders. “I could see by the way you got out o’ that ship—which is a beauty, by the way—that you haven’t got any more life in you than you used to have.” “Not in Texas anyhow. Of all the stewing, fly-bitten, deserted-” “Don’t say another word. I hate to beat up a guest,” warned Gib, whose monologs anent his beloved State were well remembered by Spears. Saunders led him through the curious, ever-growing crowd that had already sur- rounded the ship. “Sure that big mechanic can ’tend to everything?” queried the Irishman as they threaded their way through. “Anything he can’t ’tend to would be far beyond me,” grinned Sleepy. “In that case we’ll hop into the car here and get dad. He had to be at the bank this morning. Mary’s around here somewhere,144 Adventure but you can meet her later. What about your suit-case?” “Oh, we can bring that in later. I refuse to wear a blouse until evening.” “Good. I’ve got a guard for your ship, so your mechanic will be free. Hop in and we’ll jazz down-town and right back.” The big touring-car left a cloud Qf dust as Saunders sped townward, his tongue going ♦	nearly as fast as his car while he tried to cover two years in ten minutes. Ill AT 12:15 P.M. Mr. Daniel Saun- ders, genial cashier of the Gonzara bank, let himself into his house to prepare for a descent on the festivities of the afternoon. The house was deserted, every other member of the family having left for the fair-grounds long before. Mr. Saunders had barely applied himself to the lunch left for him on the kitchen table when the door bell rang. “Come in!” roared the banker. Gonzara was a small and easy-going town, every inhabitant of which was per- sonally known to him. A tall, well-dressed stranger let .himself in, and walked down the hall which led to the kitchen. The open door of the kitchen allowed Mr. Saunders to observe the prog- ress of his guest, and he arose with an apology. “I thought it must be some of the neigh- bors,” he said with an unembarrassed smile. The smile faded as he looked into the business end of a Colt .45. “Listen, and listen close,” said the stran- ger, whose face was minus the tan of the native Texan. “You’re going with me to the bank. I’ll have you covered every minute from my pocket. When we get there you’ll open the vault. Get your coat on.” His words were staccato and strictly business-like, and the impression he made was undramatic. However, there was a certain strength of purpose and cold re- solve apparent that caused the amazed cashier to put on his coat without a word. Temporarily he was incapable of connected thinking. The tall stranger, who looked to be about thirty-five, followed Saunders out of the door. A big black touring-car, holding two men besides the driver, was drawn vup in front of the house. The men were all smoking, and their attitudes were easy and untroubled. The chauffeur started the motor as they appeared at the door. “If we meet anybody that speaks to you, answer them naturally, do you under- stand?” said the stranger in conversational tones as they walked down to the car. His right hand was in his coat pocket. Mr. Saunders nodded mechanically. A quick look up and down the quiet, shaded street showed only three or four people, all of them some distance away. He and his captor took seats in the ton- neau. The man who shared it with them was stout and cold eyed, and was smoking a big cigar. All of them were inconspicu- ously dressed, and all had a young-old look, their faces showing deep lines around the mouth and hard eyes. As the car drove toward the bank at moderate speed Mr. Saunders was ever conscious of the pressure of a gun at his side. “Act natural, and keep your eyes open for anybody you ought to speak to,” ordered the tall man once more as they approached the town square. “Any trouble srfaring him, Joe?” inquired the man who shared the front seat with the driver. “Not a bit. Caught him with his mouth full.” Mr. Saunders had had a wild hope that perhaps on the <lrive through the square it might be possible to arouse some one’s sus- picions, but the robbers took a roundabout route that brought them up to the bank without going through the main part of town. The bank was located only a few steps from the square, on a side street lead- ing from it. The car drew up in front of the small brick structure, and the chauffeur calmly stopped the motor. The thing was satan- ically audacious, and yet well planned, reflected the unhappy cashier despairingly. Ninety per cent, of Gonzara’s population were out at the fair-grounds for the big annual jubilee commemorating the found- ing of the town, and there were so many visitors from other towns that strangers aroused no comment. “Hop out, and don’t waste a minute. If w<p meet anybody, I’m Mr. Smith, an old friend of yours. This other fellow is a friend of mine. And don’t kid yourself that we’ll hesitate to pump you full of holes if you squeal.”Riding Herd—Modern Style 145 The stout man accompanied his accom- plice and the cashier into the bank. Mr. Saunders Unlocked the front door in a daze and let them in. “Pull up the window and door curtains,” commanded the tall man, whom the others addressed as Joe. His stout comrade in crime took up his station by the door, leaning easily against the wall and peering through a crack in the curtain. The men in the car smoked calm- ly, although their hard eyes unobtrusively watched the infrequent stragglers that passed across the street a few steps ahead, where it led into the square. “Open that vault quick. We know the lay of the land, so don’t stall,” said Joe briskly, his gun out of his pocket once more. x The Gonzara bank was small, and it had not reached the point yet where compli- cated time-locks and other safety features had been incorporated into its equipment. With a heavy heart the cashier silently set to work. He watched helplessly while more than twenty thousand dollars went into the traveling-bag that the stout man carried. It was doubly unfortunate that the bank should have an unusual amount of cash on hand. It was ginning-season, and the Gon- zara cotton-growers had deposited a lot of money during the last few days. “Close everything up, and then let down the curtains,” said the tall man as the other thief strolled nonchalantly down the steps and into the car. Mr. Calvin Burton, a gentleman of color who was accustomed to do odd jobs for Mr. Saunders, passed by the bank as the cashier was letting down the shades to their accus- tomed position when the bank was closed. Passers-by had free view of the interior when it was closed, but the big shades were raised to keep out the sun when the bank was open. Mr. Burton was on his way to the barbecue, and yelled a cheerful, “After- noon, sah!” as he passed. “Answer!” came in a deadly whisper from Joe, who was playing a light conversational part for the benefit of passers-by. One would have thought that he and the cashier were close friends indulging in cheerful small-talk. Mr. Saunders forced a wave and a smile. Apparently Calvin did not think it peculiar that the cashier should be at the bank so late, for he passed on, whistling cheerily, vouchsafing the car and its occupants a glance of only perfunctory interest. “Now you’re going to take a little ride with us,” stated Joe as his captive silently locked the door. He could scarcely grasp the situation even then—it seemed like a dream some- how that he should be forced to help rob the bank. He simply could not compre- hend it. At Joe’s last words he came to life with a snap. “Where to?” “Just out in the country a little ways where you’ll be safely out of the way till Monday.” “I might just as well let you shoot me as be suspected of conniving at this outrage!” blazed Mr. Saunders. They had stopped at the door of the car. “Climb in, you - fool,” snarled the chauffeur, a thin-faced man wearing a linen duster. “We can club you over the head and make you go,” snapped Joe. “If it’ll comfort you any, you’ll be tied up tight, with plenty of food and water to last till you’re found.” Mr. Saunders took a last despairing look up toward the square. The irony of it en- tered his soul as he saw George Hardwick, who owned a drug-store, drive his car past the street on his way to the barbecue. Even if the bluff Hardwick had turned around, however, he could have done noth- ing but wave, for Joe’s gun could be felt through the pocket as the tall, impressively cool crook pressed close to him. He silently climbed into the car, which turned around in a leisurely way and rolled off down the street well within the speed laws. IV “THAT’S funny. The house is un- locked, but where is dad?” Saunders had shouted for his father without result. Spears dropped into a chair. “Maybe he caught a ride out to the cele- bration,” he suggested. “He wouldn’t have left the house un- locked,” objected Gib. “Maybe he forgot it, though. We’ll wait around a while and see whether he went over to some of the neighbors for something. Of course I’m making money, but-” “Ambition is the curse of young men,” stated Sleepy. “Why grub until you’re fifty for a hundred thousand that you’ve forgotten how to enjoy?” Gib grinned and glanced at his watch. “Good Lord, it’s two o’clock. He had not noticed the men especially, but was sure he did not know any of them. His description of the car was vague. All he knew was that it was fairly big and that there were two men in it and another one, very tall, in the bank with the cashier. In a few minutes a Considerable crowd of Gonzara citizens were standing around with Saunders, wait- ing for the return of the sheriff and Sears. They did not have long to wait.
