Gib Saunders’ face was very serious as he listened. The thought of his father in the hands of the thieves, and what might hap- pen to him, had suddenly changed the merry young Gib into a flaming-eyed, thin-lipped Texan who would be a distinct factor in anything that might come up. Sleepy looked at him sympathetically, and then his gaze returned to the sheriff, who was talking rapidly. “There’s just three roads they’d be liable to take, specially figurin’ on havin’ a lot o’ time for the getaway. That San Antone road is one of ’em. That splits thirty miles up at Torbard—no tellin’ which one they’d take. -”! “How far apart do the roads run? I mean, when that road splits do the two forks run within twenty miles or so of each other for a while?” It was Sleepy’s voice, with a subtle change in the timbre of it that caught Gib’s atten- tion. The crowd looked curiously at the aviator. “Why, yes. They don’t get further’n twenty miles apart for a good piece, not till the south road bends due south to Corpus. But-” “Here’s what I was thinking,” proceeded Sleepy calmly. “I can take my plane up, and overtake that car in an hour at the most, covering both roads. If somebody has some field-glasses I can do it in less, because by flying between them we can nearly see both roads at once. When I spot which road they’re 011—I can make pretty certain by diving low and looking them over—I can fly back to the automobiles with the posse and signal them which road they’re on. Then I can fly back over them, and go on to the next town. If there’s a field I can land in I can maybe get a bunch of men out to stop ’em. Anyway-” “By-!” The little sheriff slapped his leg in exul- tation. An excited murmur came from the crowd, which now included hundreds of people, as the possibilities of the scheme were realized. “And I’ll go along with a rifle in the back seat—I’m used to flying,” Gib cut in, his eyes flashing with eager hope. The sheriff set to work immediately. In less time than it takes to tell it cars were dispatched to town after high-test gas for the plane, field-glasses from Mr. Sears’ house, and a supply of rifles and revolvers from divers homes. The roar of the air- plane motor, which Correll was warming up before the advent of the gas to save time, was a constant and exciting reminder to the crowd of what was to come. The only calm human being at the bar- becue was Lieutenant John Spears, who was in semi-hiding behind an isolated tree, in Gib’s company, to avoid being bothered with questions. The pair did not appear publicly, so to speak, until the cars had re- turned from town with the needed supplies. As they left their quiet retreat and ap- proached the surging mob around the ship, now silent as Correl quickly filled it with gas, Sleepy looked at his red-headed friend with a slow grin.148 Adventure “I’m sure your dad is all right, Gib—just in hiding, so to speak, or on the way to be hid for a couple o’ days. So let’s enjoy ourselves, eh?” Somehow his quiet confidence was in- fectious. “You’ve said it!” declared Gib. V THREE thousand feet high the big Le Pere boomed along the San An- tonio road. It was three-thirty, and according to Cal’s story the robbers had almost three hours start. Probably about ninety miles, reflected Spears, unless something had happened. Saunders knew every foot of the country, and had said that there were no side roads that the thieves would be likely to take. They came to the fork in the road, and Sleepy guided the plane squarely down the middle line between the two. For fifteen miles ahead the roads were not more than ten miles apart, the gap between them grad- ually widening. They had sighted only two cars driving along the way they were traveling, both very small ones. They had played safe and dropped earthward for a close look. The country was not thickly settled, and such traffic as there was, which was small, was toward Gonzara and not away from it. Saunders was watching the right-hand road, and Spears the left. They had trav- ersed about fifteen miles on a line between the two when Gib reached over and shook the pilot. He pointed down to a tiny speck traveling along the road, toward San Antonio. Sleepy nodded and nosed down. He did not believe it was their car. He figured that to be several miles ahead. What he did not figure on was the stop the desper- adoes had made on a side road to chain Mr. Saunders securely in a deserted shack, where they had left him with sufficient food and water to last three days. They were a thousand feet high when they caught the car, and as he circled over it Sleepy noted that it was large and black and* traveling fast. The country under- neath was mostly mesquite, although iso- lated cleared spots could be seen. The Le Pere, under his handling, was skimming the tops of the trees several hun- dred yards back of the car. Sleepy flew parallel to the road, as low as possible, and throttled the motor slightly as they shot past. There were several men in the car, that was certain. Gib, a long-barreled rifle in his hand, leaned over the rear cockpit as far as he could stretch, trying his utmost to identify some one in the car. They flashed by so fast, however, that they could come to no conclusion. Spears turned the throttle full on, and circled back once more. He could scarcely believe that was the car, and yet it might be. Certainly the passengers had craned their necks to the utmost to watch them; but that would be only natural for anybody to do in view of the low flying he was doing. On the next trip past the car the thieves gave themselves away. Probably it was the sight of Gib’s rifle, and the fact that the circling of the plane denoted suspicion. As the two flyers shot by, their heads turned as they strained their eyes toward the car, they saw two revolvers spit flame at them. Gib’s face was alight with exultation, and his eyes flashed fire through the big goggles. The shots did not bother them particularly—a fast moving plane is about as difficult a target as there is extant. Sleepy pointed backward, and Gib nod- ded. The map showed that the road was without cross-cuts for twenty miles— by the time the car covered that they could be back again after signaling the posse coming behind. With motor turning eighteen hundred and speedmeter showing a hundred and forty miles an hour the Le Pere sped back over the trail. It took less than fifteen minutes to reach the three speeding auto- mobiles that held the sheriff and his deputies. The Le Pere turned around, flew straight for a moment, and then banked steeply to the right. It was the agreed- upon signal to indicate which road the bandits had taken. The flyers wasted no time, but went swift- ly back on their way. They found the car speeding along the road, plainly making every effort to get where they were going as fast as might be practicable. The Le Pere passed it five hundred feet high, and flew on ahead. The next town was fifteen miles up the road, and was only a hamlet. They were perhaps five miles ahead of the car when they struck a very large cleared spot, extending at least three hundredRiding Herd- yards on each side of the road. It was a corn-stubble field, mowed closely, and what looked to be a partially broken-down shack was in one corner. Gib shook Sleepy energetically, and pointed down. He motioned to it with his rifle. Sleepy nodded, his eyes glowing beneath the drooping lids and his face all a-grin at what he conceived to be Gib’s idea. He dipped over the field, ascertaining that the furrows were shallow and that the stubble was very short, and then circled back a few hundred yards for his landing. There was plenty of room, but he stalled down across the mesquite and landed, leav- ing space enough for a take-off from where the plane stopped. Gib had unfastened his belt before the plane stopped, and was out in a flash. “I can get up there behind a tree or some- thing, and pop their front tires,” he said quickly, his freckled face ablaze with ex- citement. “Can you shoot that well?” enquired Sleepy doubtfully. “Born and brought up in Texas, boy,” stated Saunders. “You take off and keep in touch with the posse. I could get every one of them from behind my tree if they started to rush me when I put their car out of commission. If you stay here the plane will warn them before they get near enough for me. They could scatter in the mes- quite then and make the deal a lot more difficult.” Sleepy nodded his acquiescence, and shoved on the throttle without delay. There was plenty of room, and he cleared the mesquite on the other side without trouble. Below him he watched Gib’s somewhat stout form scurry across the field and dive into the woods at the farther end. He kept the Le Pere circling above the field at about five hundred feet, waiting to see what would happen. He took time to look over his instruments closely. The oil pressure had dropped to twenty-two—the oil had not been renewed since leaving Donovan. However, that was all right, for Sleepy knew the Liberty was good for another two hours of flying on the oil sup- ply. Air pressure was correct at three, and the tachometer showed the motor turning a good fifteen-fifty. Centigrade tempera- ture was 87° —too hot, due to the low flying. Sleepy throttled the motor to fourteen ■Modern Style	149 hundred—as low as would keep the Le Pere level—and opened the motor shutters the final notch. In a moment he saw the car approaching, going like mad. He tilted up his ship in a shallow bank, his eyes on the ground below him. The car was a quarter of the way across the big clearing when he saw it lurch suddenly. A hundred yards more and it stopped on the edge of the ditch. Sleepy grinned slowly. Apparently Gib was strictly on the job. He saw flashes of flame as the bandits returned the fire, but there was no answering shot from the woods as far as he could see. Undoubtedly Gib was holding his fire for fear of hitting his father. Four men clustered behind the car. Evidently Mr. Saunders was not there. He wondered where the banker was. Pos- sibly they had bound and gagged him in some nook of his own house, he reflected. Suddenly his eyes narrowed and the wide mouth tightened. The four men were making a break for the woods on the op- posite side of the clearing from where Saunders was hiding. In a flash he saw the fatal mistake he and Gib had made. By running on a course that was a continuation of an imaginary line made by Saunders and the car, they could use the car as a partial screen all the way. When they reached the woods they could circle around, using the trees as pro- tection, and shoot Gib from the rear. He would be totally helpless against four men spread out in the woods behind him. Their only chance for escape, and that a slim one, was to change their tires without being under fire. In all probability they were desperate enough to take any chance that offered even the slimmest of opportunities to escape. ALMOST before these thoughts had raced through Sleepy’s head he had nosed his ship down in a straight dive, motor wide open. The bandits were less than fifty yards from the car as he came roaring across the field, not a foot above the ground, and headed straight for them. The Le Pere was a thing of terrible power as it hurled itself across the ground, and the bandits turned and ran back toward the car. The purpose of the pilot was very evident to them.150 Adventure With a grin Sleepy thanked his stars that they .did not know how fatal it would be to him if he really hit them. It would assuredly break his propeller and pile him up against the trees at the edge of the clearing. No sooner had he gone past them than he lifted his ship in a beautiful climbing turn that was almost a wing-turn. The ship was practically stalled at the peak of it, but headed back the way it had just come. It settled earthward, picking up speed as it dropped until it was once more speeding across the ground, nearer the car this time. The bandits had stopped when it went past, but as it returned they scurried the rest of the way back to the car. Once again Sleepy performed the same maneuver. If there was any flyer at Donovan Field who doubted Spears’ flying- ability, a grand-stand seat that day would have settled his doubts. Wing-turning a Le Pere from a foot off the ground is a ma- neuver that leaves the pilot only a few feet between himself and a bad crash. Stalling the slightest bit too much at the top of his climbing turn would mean a straight drop to the ground, for there was not altitude enough to gain flying speed after it was once completely lost. At best it was necessary almost to pick the ship off the ground as it settled after the turn. Twice more they tried to make a run for it. The second time one man flopped face down to the ground. Like a flash Sleepy nosed down a bit more, trying to hit him with the wheels. In all probability it would not hurt him badly, but it would certainly scare him effectually. His idea was simply to bounce his wheels off the man’s back. He did not succeed, but with a grin he reflected that he had not come far away, for the man’s body had disappeared beneath the wing. The man started to get up as Sleepy passed, to be knocked down by the propeller blast. He dived for the car with- out delay, and for ten minutes there was no further attempt at making the woods. It looked very peaceful down there—the field stretching big and smooth in the sunlight, four men clustered quietly around the car, and the mesquite giving no sign of the dead- ly rifleman within its cool-looking green shelter. One of the bandits showed himself in- cautiously beside the car. As Sleepy watchfully patrolled low over the mesquite he saw the man grab his arm and leap back behind the shelter of the auto. A thin wisp of smoke arose from the mesquite. It was lucky that the bandits apparently had only revolvers as weapons, thought the pilot. Suddenly he nosed down at full throttle. All four men were making another attempt to cross the clearing. For a moment he lost sight of them as the top wing hid them in the steepness of the dive. As he roared across the ground they retreated near the car, and flashes of fire from four revolvers could be seen plainly. They shot continu- ously. As he passed them he felt a shattering . impact in his shoulder. The plane wabbled for a moment and veered perilously as the pilot felt the searing pain of the bullet. With an effort that left him faint he straightened the ship as the wing-skid dragged the ground, and held it level for a moment. He lifted it over the trees and banked around. The lucky shot had left his right arm al- most paralyzed. Every bump that hit the ship caused him excruciating pain. He had only one hand to handle both stick and throttle. The blood was slowly staining through his shirt, and he could feel it trick- ling down his body. Three times more, at intervals of a few moments, the men below tried to outwit him. Each time the roaring Le Pere, speed- ing toward them as they started, sent them back. Apparently they had no doubt of the pilot’s intention to hit them. With his face white under the tan, and his body weaken- ing rapidly as the slowly gushing blood drained it of strength, Sleepy fought to re- tain consciousness. Of all the tragedies that happen in flying, ninety per cent, start within ten feet of the ground. An errot in leveling off—hitting an obstruction in landing—slipping into the ground trying to bank too close to it—those are the things that kill pilots. Spears, forcing himself to keep clear-headed and conscious by main force of will, time and time again wing-turned off the ground to rush back across the field before his prey could escape. Never a time did that terrific dive last a split-second too long and send the Le Pere head-on into the earth, nor did it falter and drop as it climbed and turned together, to be ready for the next dive. Twice he de- liberately tried to move his right arm, so that the agony of it would help him fightRiding Herd—Modern Style 151 off the faintness that threatened to master him. He had no conception of the passage of time. The motor was running wide open— he was too close to the ground to risk taking his one good hand from the stick to cut it. A seductively dangerous languor came over him. It seemed useless to keep watch- ing the men below—they had not made an attempt to break for the woods in some time. It was so silly and futile to go to all this trouble. He banked mechanically. For a moment his hand dropped from the stick limply, and the ship nosed up; hovered on the verge of a spin. A terrific bump that sent agonies of pain shooting through his right side cleared his head, and he caught the ship just in time.	^ The field danced crazily before his eyes. He knew he must go down, or in a little while he would cease to fight off the de- licious drowsiness that seemed like paradise as he thought of yielding to it. Just to let himself go- He kicked the ship around, and took his hand from the stick. He jerked the throttle back, and quickly grabbed the control again. He felt a shock as the undercarriage swept through the top of a tree. As if he were watching some one else do it he uncon- sciously leveled off, five feet off the ground. The nose dropped, and the wheels hit and bounced. For just a second the pain cleared his head—he knew he was eight feet off the ground, but he did not care. He fainted as the ship bounced again, the low undercarriage saving the ship from a nose-on crash. Sleepy was crumpled in the front cockpit as the Le Pere rolled almost to the edge of the woods, and a red-headed figure came bounding toward it, rifle in hand. VI SPEARS opened his eyes and looked up into a genial round face, belong- ing to a bald-headed gentleman of middle age. He was in bed, and evidently in a private house. They all tiptoed out to avoid disturbing him. Author of “The Gladiators,” “Catching a Talar," etc. OMETHING was standing in the trellised moonlight beneath the pine trees. Something half re- vealed, half shadow, half seen, half guessed. And it had not been there a few seconds before. Being in England, the land of pheasant worship and the grouse cult, the shadow ought to have been a dog or a fox—but it was not. It looked like neither of these, there was something differ- ent about it, a suggestion of greater wild- ness. It was a little larger than a fox, a little smaller than a wolf; and when it turned its eyes shone green. Then it stepped into the full light, and became a coyote—a prairie wolf. The reason for so fierce a presence in so tame a land came about as follows. Four months before, this coyote had struck a trap in America—on the south fork of the Stink- ing Water River, to be exact. One month before he was touring England as part of a traveling menagerie. Upon this night of nights he had been fed by his keeper—who was beautifully and unashamedly drunk. They found the man asleep in the cage next morning; the key of the cage they found on the floor, but the coyote they never found. So much of this animal’s history is known, so much is public prop- erty. The rest, that which follows, is here revealed for the first time. It was told to me by the wild folk, and it is not given to all to understand the language of the wild folk. A thin high scream cut aslant the cathe- dral silence of the pinewood, a scream al- most startlingly like that of a child, and the coyote ‘froze’. He sank upon the grass and was still; eyes gleaming, ears pressed close back, thin lean upper lip lifted a little to display the cruel glistening fangs. Only his flanks moved, still heaving to theThe Outcast 153 exertions of his ten mile removal from the menagerie. Then quite suddenly, came the scream again, and the coyote slid away on his stomach. He knew that cry, the last protest against fate of a terrified rabbit. He came to a hedge, and peered through. He saw a shad- owy beast with a big bush-tail—something like his own—dragging a kicking rabbit toward the hedge. Red fox the coyote noted to himself—and waited. By which it may be seen that he knew all about foxes. Reynard came on—knowing nothing in his turn of coyotes—and slid into the hedge- ditch with his struggling prey. He preferred to kill it here than in the open, where its pestilential squeal might attract any horror —a keeper for instance. “G-r-r-r”, said the coyote, a yard above him in the tangle of hedge-roots and the fox stiffened from head to foot. He was fright- ened, that fox, frightened no end, for he had never heard a growl like that before in all his marauding life. And a stoat, that was enviously watching the fox, fell back- ward down the bank of the ditch and fled; while a dry, rustling flutter o’erhead told that the roosting birds had heard that growl also, and were afraid. A head—almost wolf-like in its evil fierce- ness, almost fox-like in its leering cunning— was thrust suddenly from out the matted tangle, and the fox visibly quailed. Then, behind the head, came the coyote; not slowly but like a brown streak. The fox was aware of a burning pain in his near shoulder, and he pivoted cringing back among the damp dead leaves of the ditch— forgetting the rabbit. The rabbit had not forgotten him, however. It forgot nothing. It went, or rather to be more exact, it was gone, for noi|e had seen the going of it. In the wild it is as well to grab your chance while you can; you may not get another. The rabbit must have known. But if that rabbit thought—do rabbits think?—he had check-mated his foes, he counted without the coyote. Check it may have been, but mate was not yet. In a second the fox—badly bitten as he was—had picked up himself and also the rabbit’s trail. Whereupon he went, with- out comment, into the moonlit haze; and soon, as he galloped, the rabbit danced before him. He could see it a fleeing shadow, a bunched up ball and a stretched out streak alternately. Bunny had no illusions. The drumming of the four-footed death on the short grass behind left no room for any. He wanted to get back to the warren, which was not far away, very badly indeed. He would have got there,'if a strange thing had not hap- pened—a thing neither hunter nor hunted expected. The fox was first of all aware of it, a long, lean, silent, gray shadow which shot—it almost seemed to fly so quick was it—past close to him.- It had eyes aflame, that thing, and he shied at sight of it, as a fright- ened horse shies. In a flash it was ahead. The rabbit may have heard it coming; but it is doubtful if he knew what killed him. Followed a glint of fangs as the apparition shot up alongside the luckless bunny, a snap, but no pause. At full gallop this ghostly specter had killed and picked up the quarry, swung round in a wide circle, and vanished away, still going like the wind, into the night. Then at last the fox must have realised that this was his new enemy of the ditch, the coyote, that strange, giant, foxy apparition which had come out of nowhere at him, and gone again as a whirlwind might into nowhere, for aught he knew. Having dined, the coyote set off across country, at the long, tireless, wolf gallop that will run down almost any four-footed thing in the end. He had no idea how far that menagerie might pursue him, and he was taking no chances. A wild creature never willingly takes chances. Once—that was when he bee-lined over a bare blunt-nosed hill—a line of hurdles leaped up in front of him. He took them in his stride and fell among ewes, many and loud voiced ewes, who rose up as one sheep, and ran about complaining in a full throated chorus. Seeing that the poor outcast’s one notion at that time—possibly at all times— was self-effacement, this action on the ewes’ part was unkind. For one thing it caused him to fly the opposite line of hurdles like a bird, and for another it awoke a long, low, blue beast, from nowhere special, which fell in dumbly at the coyote’s heels. The shep- herd called it a bob-tailed sheep-dog, but it looked more like a silent devil in the light of the “cynical moon.” It took the coyote half an hour to shake that speechless horror off his track. Once also—that was when he was gingerly rounding the corner of a cattle-muddied gateway—he fell on to and over a warm,I54 Adventure feathery live thing, which got up and snored away into the night on vast flapping wings, more uncannily than any ghost. The next five hundred yards he must have covered in record time for he was scared almost into rabies. The thing was an owl, a white owl, and a big one at that, though what it was doing in the mud of a gateway no one knows. Perhaps some rat could have told. Rats are generally intimately—too inti- mately for their liking—connected with’the doings of owls. And once—that was the worst fright of all—he barged head over heels into some- thing that he could not see stretched across a gap, and it clung. He fought madly for a space, rolling about, snapping, and tear- ing like a thing possessed, for this nightmare held on all round him. Followed a curse from the shadows, and after the curse a man, and after the man a dog—a thin, lean dog this time. The coyote did not like the appearance of that dog, it looked under- hand, and even a mole could see that it was built for speed. This may explain, perhaps, why he bit the man on the hand—his teeth met, I am told—and went away into the night with half the unseen terror which clung, wildly flap- ping from his hind quarters. The man swore strange oaths and went away in a hurry, which seemed strange. As a matter of fact, however, the coyote had run into a poacher’s net stretched across a gap-way for the entertainment of certain hares, and the poacher was about as scared as the prairie-wolf over the meeting. An hour later came the dawn—cold, un- pitying, matter-of-fact dawn-—and it dis- covered this coyote in the middle of a gorse- and-bog-patched moor. He went into re- tirement for the day in a fox’s earth which he sought and found in a great hurry. The stench of the place—for there are few fouler abodes among the wild folk than the den of the little red rascal—made him very literally, and shamelessly, ill. He knew, however, that anything—even the most appalling effluvium—is better than for one of the night prowlers, the outlaws, to be found abroad when the sun is up. WHEN evening fell, and the shad- ows hunted the raw, red sun home to his lair, the coyote thrust out an inquiring muzzle, and thrust it back again in a hurry. A man was coming through the gorse—a jet black shadow carved out, as it were, against the crimson sky. Came he to the fox’s earth and dropped a rabbit by the entrance. He gave no reason for so strange an action, but the reason—seven of them— had been found killed in his fowl-house three nights before—and the rabbit was poisoned. Evidently this tit-bit, this gift gratis, was intended for the entertainment of the rightful owner of that den, and he happened to be away upon a blood raid, in the opposite direction! The coyote waited ten minutes after the man had gone, then he wriggled out to the rabbit. He gave one sniff, only one, then he went away, also, straight as a die, and very swiftly, with every hair along his back sitting up all bristly. You see, he was ac- quainted with poison. He had cause to be, for he had once on a time watched his mother die beside a poisoned carcass, and the terror of the taint which had hung in the air round that carcass lived with him ever after. It had been a nightmare, and the mere whiff of poison a hundred yards to leeward, had the effect of terrifying him for hours. Like a hunted cat, this wolfish beast, that was not a wolf, panicked across the face of the sullen moor. Then he caught sight of his own shadow cast by the dying sun—• a blue figure against blood red grass—danc- ing along at his side. He never waited to think, but shot away ten yards in three bounds—and stopped. There was no question about the stop- ping. One moment he was racing at I dare not say what speed, the next he just—wasn’t moving. And the suddenness of it nearly wrenched him to pieces. He had simply bounded full into one of those quagmires, one of those abominations which pock-mark so many moors. But no flies settled on this beast. He did not wait even so long as one second. If he had he would have been a very dead coyote; and he knew it. How the miracle was accomplished, how, without appearing to pause or think or hesi- tate, he was able to rebound again exactly as if he had landed upon a spring-board in- stead of a quaking, shaking, sucking, death- trap, is beyond mere human knowledge. From this bound he reached a tuft of deer- grass, at least one presumes he reached it for he barely touched it, rebounding for the second time and on to firm ground at last. Being somewhat scared, he ran on for a fewThe Outcast 155 yards along the edge of the bog before he discovered that he was exhausted some- what, and sat down. It was the hour of brooding calm, when the sun’s last rays, lingering, charmed the pur- ple heather into a deeper tone. In the pine- wood behind him—such was the silence— the coyote could hear the hollow purr of the air in the upper branches, like the humming in a shell, and the tiny tumult of an ant city: the indescribable slithering undersound which can only be detected in very still places. Night was stalking westward swiftly over the purple lakes of heather. A jay screeched ‘gratingly, somewhere ’way back in the wood; the last woodpecker had fled laughing into the shadows; the last family of titmice had trailed by, twittering and “twee-jeeing” to each other, through this hall of a thousand columns. Presently came a long-drawn sigh echoing down the pillared aisles, and after the. sigh a big brown bird, flapping like some gigantic moth; that was a long-horned owl, and it flew without sound as an owl should. Suddenly the coyote turned his head. Something—some swift moving shadow— was coming across the bog. How any beast heavier than a mouse could cross that quak- ing abomination was past understanding, but the shadow proceeded leisurely and un- flustered enough, and it did not sink. In- stantly the coyote, the outcast, slid to a heather patch on his belly, and lay there crouched; his green glazed eyes fixed and staring at the shadow. The apparition came on, bounding lightly from tussock to tussock of deer-grass, half insolently, half playfully, it seemed toying with the suffocating death that it only es- caped by a hair’s breadth at each fairy-like bound. The coyote’s lips dripped, as he watched; his eyes took on a crueler glow. The thing was a deer, he knew the breed: the slender legs, the arched hind-quarters, the graceful step, as if the beast were walking on air. As a matter of fact she—it was a she—was a roe-deer but this he did not know. He took her for a prong-horn antelope, perhaps, or a white-tail deer—but, no matter; his tactics would be the same. She passed him—he had taken care to lie well to leeward—quite close, so close that as she vanished like some spirit of the wood beneath the trees, he saw the transient gleam of her white rump quite plainly. For a minute he waited—time enough for her to penetrate the wood well out of sight. Then he dropped his head, and, with tail slightly lifted, settled into the long easy wolf-gallop on her trail. He must have known by the scent how close he was—or how far—for never once did he sight her or blunder on to her heels in the pine gloom, nor was she once startled, or aware of her tracker. Thus nicely did he judge his pace. As the trail broke from the wood and led, straight now as a die over the open moor, the pace increased. The lone night wind whis- pered in his ears, and the night-hawks and bats did their own small hunting above him. But he never lifted his head. His eyes were glued on the way ahead. Only his restless ears took note of that which went on around. Once a fox halted in the middle of trailing a rabbit, to stare at this big wolfish beast loping by with gleaming eyes; and once, something, which was not a fox—though it was as big—stood in his path chattering obscenely, forcing him to go round. That must have been a badger. Finally—this after an exhibition of clever windings, twists and turns—the trail led to,a spruce firwood, black as a well, and still as a well, or death. Here was needed great caution, and the coyote slid belly flat. The whorls of the trees came down and brushed the needle-carpeted ground, diffi- cult to avoid, hard even to creep through, hard that is, when even the faint sound of brushed back branches, would have ruined all in that desert-like silence. Suddenly, as if some cold hand had seized him, the coyote sank where he stood, sank and was still, like one that is stricken sud- denly by death. In front was a clearing, no more than a gap, shadowed as to its center by one lone, big spruce fir. And beneath that lonely shade stood the doe, and she had business with something that was there. She was anxious, that deer, anxious and happy, making odd little noises in her throat—and beside her were two more shadows. Then she stood out before the moon, and there followed her, two fawns, their great beautiful eyes shining like stars. Dainty spirits of the shades—sleek, shining, satin- eared, velvet-nosed, dappled and light as thistle-down, upon legs “slender as hazel wands.” How long the coyote lay and watched that wonder is not known; some hours perhaps.Adventure He might have been a figure^carved out of dark and light oak for all the signs of life he gave. Only his eyes lived, those green orbs, with the strange coldness of cruelty— cruelty is assuredly a cold passion—ap- parent in their depths. At last a badger—clumsy, shuffling, furtive, and morose—passed in the gloom. He was in a hurry that beast, and when a badger is taken that way dawn is not far off. This the doe knew—or seemed to know. She vanished with her double charge slowly, under the pall-like shade of the giant and isolated spruce, and when she came out again she was alone. As a smoke puff fades, so faded this elfin like haunter of the woods. The flash as of a faintly gleaming lamp caused by her light rump as she whisked away, was all the indi- cation a watcher would have had of her departure. The coyote followed, a slinking evil form in the gloom. Followed her to the edge of the wood, far enough to see the roe-deer safely on her way—but no farther. He had other work on hand. In five minutes he was back again by the miniature clearing, crouching motionless as before, watching always the gloom beneath the spruce that stood aloof. And the sky paled to gray even as he crouched. So in- tently were his cruel eyes fixed upon this spot that he never saw a shadow—surely it was no more than a shadow—which drifted up from nowhere special behind him— drifted and stopped. The minutes dragged on painfully, almost as if the dead silence, the stagnant air, was waiting for something to happen. A FOX passed, slinking and car- mine tinted about the jaws. A pine-marten “rippling in faultless curves,” the incarnation of living, moving grace, went by in the dawn, and his jaws also bore the blood stain, the badge of out- lawry. A squirrel slid down perkily from some tree near at hand, and slid up again in a hurry, when it saw the couched coyote. The fox was red, the pine-marten was red, the squirrel was red, the watching, waiting shadow behind was red, all of them red, against a background of reddish tree-trunks and pine-needles, lighted up all together and suddenly by the fiery red of dawn, that wonderful dawn which comes after the gray, and is followed again by a day of gray rain. Suddenly the coyote moved, sat up, and the shadow—the shadow was gone. Only a gleam as of twin stars, a flash as of a low burning lamp, hastily extinguished, marked its disappearance, and made one half suspi- cious as to whether it had really been a shadow after all, and half doubtful as to its ever having been there at all. This also, the coyote did not see. Then the wolfish beast moved forward— almost flat he proceeded—-and his caution was evident at every careful, creeping stride. There was still darkness under the great spruce, and for a moment he stood con- fusedly. Then, in a soft place, just the least noticeable spot, exactly where they would be most difficult to get at, he saw the fawns. They were lying down, but not asleep; they were looking not at him but past him, behind him at something that was there—the shadow had come back again. A gleam of white fangs scintillated in the growing light, but there was no sound as the coyote half rose and dived forward headlong upon the helpless fawns. They tumbled to their feet. They could run fairly well, fast enough to avoid a man, but not a coyote. And they fled. Then, without any kind of warning at all, the shadow took unto itself life. It hurled itself forward head down, preceded by short, but rapier-sharp horns, at the speed of a powerfully shot arrow; and its mark, its goal, appeared to be the coyote. It was a full-grown roe-buck, and of all the cloven- hoofed ones the roe-buck is the least troubled with fear and flurry. The coyote was behind one of the fawns— just about to deliver one of his quick, chop- ping bites on the flank, in fact—when he became aware of a small-sized whirlwind hurtling down upon him from behind. He realized his danger, and pivoted upon himself, presenting a fanged and snarling front in one and the same second, so it seemed; being specially designed to do so. But in the next second he was removed quite twelve feet by the sharpest pair of horns, and the most impetuous buck, he had ever run up against. When he regained his feet— which was practically the same moment as he touched the ground—he was furious; for this buck was only small—no larger than a pronghorn antelope, and the wound in his shoulder pained abominably. But he had made one blunder; he had not reckonedThe Outcast 157 upon the peculiar character of the roe-buck —there are deer and deer, and the roe-deer is unique. Next moment the whirlwind was upon him again; the fury of the charge was ex- traordinary, and amazed the half-recovered coyote. He did not—being only an outcast wolfling—stand up to it, but skipped to one side, and delivered one of his chopping flank bites. A whirl of pine-needles followed, for the roe-buck can turn as can no other deer, and the coyote fell backward, as the quick- est way to avoid a hissiiig slash of those terrible forked little horns. Thereafter things happened, and hap- pened quickly. The coyote was no amateur in the matter of keeping a whole skin by agility, and agility alone, but the deer he knew were not like unto this little fury. He leaped, and side-leaped and sprung back- ward, pivoted on his tracks, spun like a top, rolled from under the flying hoofs, raced like a greyhound, and generally performed on an average thirty acrobatic feats a minute. His foe, however, plunged and stabbed, jabbed and hacked, stamped and tore, and raged, and fumed, and bucked, and backed, and thrust, and cut, and slashed, and foamed, like unto some four-footed fiend. The coyote might have been a magnet, and the buck a piece of steel, so close did they follow one another. Now a coyote, unharried and unpressed, is many kinds of a coward, but a coyote cornered and hanging on to his life by his whiskers, is just any sort of a devil you like to name. This may be why, at the end of five minutes of this death dance, there were triangular gaps on the roe-buck—chiefly about the flanks—where the flesh had been removed in chunks. On the other hand the coyote was striped much as is a tiger; only the stripes were red, not black. Followed a collision. I don’t know how it happened. One of the combatants must have made a mistake somewhere. Any- way, they met. It was a bundle, that is all you could say; a huddle, and it was not nice to behold. The needles flew all whither, and the earth followed suit. Which beast was where, it was impossible to say. Save for the horrible low and continuous snarling of the coyote, there was no sound, but one knew that death hovered round that jumble of legs and bodies. Then, with startling suddenness it ceased—the fight was over. The roe-buck was standing rocking in his tracks, as a tree rocks in a gale. The coyote lay on his back ten yards away; and both were unpleasant to behold, were unclean to the sight. Slowly the coyote heaved on to his feet, and stood staring stupidly—himself rocking to and fro—at his enemy. Then, like a broken thing—reminding one in a singular way of some half-killed insect—he dragged himself away into the red eye of the rising sun; a shocking nightmare of a beast, a blot on the beauty of the dawn. And even as he went the roe-buck toppled forward on his nose, and lay still—for ever. As for the coyote he blundered drunkenly, half blinded, and half choked, straight ahead to the open moor and its scattered bogs, and into a bog, and under it for ever and all time. And that is why the dozens and dozens of people, who scoured the country for that lost and outcast coyote, never found him— and never will.Author of “A Shot at a Venture,” “Evil Starbuck,” etc. R. TUDGAY, second mate of the ship Yattaroi, stood at the shore gangway gazing irritably along the stretch of wharf at Dunedin. His ship was a big handful for the crew at his disposal, and getting ready for sea was too big a job; five apprentices, two of them first-trippers, constituted the entire available gang. Of the ship’s com- pany sailing in the YaUaroi from Lon- don, only these and the captain were left, and Captain Kirk was even now scouring Dunedin for a crew of any sort to carry his ship to sea, for he feared he might yet lose his boys, who knew little, and cared less, of the legal tangle of words which demanded a cash forfeit from the parent of each ap- prentice defaulting his indentures. There was a lure for healthy lads in New Zealand, the land of Pink Terraces and tattoed Ma- oris. As for the rest of the defaulting hands —crew, cook, steward, and the first mate himself—they had only followed countless examples and struck off to seek fortune in their own way. “Blessed good mind to hop it myself,” grumbled Mr. Tudgay. He caught sight of the youngest ap- prentice grinning as if he had overheard the shameful confession, and was tempted to turn loose his wrath in physical visitations on the youngster’s curly head. The sight of a cart approaching the ship, laden with boxes and bottles of the skipper’s private i stores, swung his mind from an unhealthy subject to a more wholesome one. He aroused the boys with a resounding deep- sea roar, and sent them scampering down the gangway. “Bear a hand and put those stores in the captain’s stateroom, and watch yourself if either of ye broaches anything,” he ordered, and turned away, as a decent mate should, to hide a grin; for the boys, almost before the cart had stopped, had filled each a grimy fist from a basket of peaches with the devil- ish skill and uncanny instinct possessed by all sailing-ship boys since the first wreck on Ararat. The chief thing was that the boys were employed, and would be for half an hour, which gave Mr. Tudgay a legitimate excuse for standing idly gazing up the wharf in case the captain arrived. The Uttered decks were not so littered as they appeared, for most of the rubbish was broken dunnage and useless bits of discarded staging, which were to be put into the cook’s bunker or hove overboard as soon as they were beyond the harbor; and the hawsers were ready, the topsails and courses, jibs and spanker were bent—sufficient canvas to carry the vessel to sea—and the staysails and t’gal- lants’ls, royals and gaff-topsail could be sent up and bent while under way, providing the captain had managed to scare up a crew with two arms and legs apiece. The second mate’s cogitations were brokenThe Cook of the Yallaroi 159 off suddenly by the heaving into sight of an imposing procession, led by Captain Kirk in person, looking a bit out of place with his dignified air and gait—the more so by contrast with his following; and the straggling line was flanked by two hand trucks containing a collection of nondescript sea-going gear that would have disgraced a rummage sale. A little way apart, but still with the crowd, a big, darkly handsome man strode along, carrying a bright new portmanteau in his capable hand as if it were a lady’s handbag instead of a man-size piece of luggage weighing sixty pounds or so. “Shake a leg down below, boys,” yelled Mr. Tudgay through the saloon skylight. “On deck wi’ ye, and bear a hand. Oh, corks! There’s a full crew for us, lads, and a bloomin’ Don Juan packing his own dun- nage that looks the spit of a liner mate!” “Mr. Tudgay, this is the mate, Mr.— Mr.-?” “Galway,” the new mate supplied, smil- ing brilliantly and making the second mate squirm with the grip he gave him. “Hope we’ll get along all right, Mr. Tudgay.” “Ye’d better,” grunted Captain Kirk darkly. “Get a move on wi’ yer sea gear, Mr. Galway, and we’ll off to sea. The tug’s due now, an’ we’ve lost plenty time.” “Crew signed on, sir?” asked Tudgay as the new mate vanished to change into more suitable attire for working the ship out. “Yes!” snapped the skipper impatiently, then saw the reason for the query. The men were slouching about the gang- way as if none of them had the least notion what part of the ship they belonged to. Of them all, only two showed intelligence. The cook went directly to his galley, and already the rattle of pots could be heard; one seaman had gone direct to the fore- castle, and now appeared dressed for work. “Get for’ard, you!” the skipper roared to the rest, “Are ye thinkin’ to make it a pleasure trip? Get changed and tur-rn to. Can’t ye put life into them, Mr. Tudgay? Are ye second mate or am I? Here, you parson-looking son of a waterspout! Aft wi’ ye and start yerwor-rk! Are ye a steward or are ye not?” A weedy, round-shouldered, pale-faced biped, who looked as much like a washed- out parson as anything else, shambled aft and followed th: skipper into the cabin with eyes upturned in shocked amazement at the manner of his greeting. His lips muttered soundlessly, and his fingers writhed as if he were passing a benediction upon a crew of cannibals the moment before he was in- troduced to their cook-pot. The new mate came to time smartly, whatever his appearance might indicate, and Tudgay fell into line easily after Mr. Galway had been on the deck of his new ship two minutes. He was certainly a full- rigged chief mate, if he did look rather more suitable for the high bridge of a shiny liner. “The Old Man’s picked us out a ripe bunch, sir,” grinned Tudgay as he fell to work on his half of the gang and started singling up the shorefasts. “Overripe, Mr. Tudgay; very much over- ripe. Did you take a slant at the cook?” “Only man in the crowd who seems to know which end of a ship goes first,” grunt- ed the second mate. “Aye, but take a good quiz at his hand' some dial, man!” The mate plunged into his own duties, and speedily made a working gang out of the indifferent material at hand. The cook was already peeling potatoes, seated on the bitts abreast of his galley door. Tudgay scrutinized him keenly. After the first real look he could scarcely take his eyes away. The man’s face looked as if the two ugliest human faces extant had been twisted up together as two wet dishcloths might be, then wrung out, flattened, and prodded into shape roughly with the prongs of a tor- mentor until something like a face was evolved. The mouth was a slash, the eyes were set in two slits—but the eyes them- selves held the fire of the tiger’s. One ear was half-gone; upon the forehead was a livid scar; the uncouth head was set, however, upon a neck like a tree, which in turn rose out of shoulders and a chest that would not have shamed an eight-foot Hercules; and the arms, hands, legs and hips were in harmony. “What’s your name, cook?” asked Tud- gay, curiously regarding the man. “Peters, sir; Joseph Peters.” “You’re a regular he-man, ain’t you? You ought to be in the fo’csle instead o’ peeling little spuds. You’d make a top- hole bosun.” “I don’t like the forecastle, sir. The men are so rough,” replied the amazing cook in a voice which would have reverberated over the harbor if given range, but which sound- ed muffled and dumb as if through sheer apprehension.i6o Adventure The second mate was to experience a big- ger surprize yet. When the ship was bow- ing to the great ocean swell just before let- ting go her towline, he was at the monkey- rail overseeing the placing of the booby- hatch, and the new steward emerged from the main-deck saloon door bearing a dish of ingredients for the cabin dessert. Tripping slightly over the high door-sill, the parson- like steward gave vent to what sounded suspiciously like a real, ripping, back-o’- beyond curse and then abruptly resumed his solemn aspect and gait and proceeded to the galley. But the surprize came when the cook, the big, Herculean cook, got up from his own door-sill with amazing alacrity at a sharply flung word from the steward, and took the dish from him with the air of a subdued underling. At the moment the chief mate, having seen the tug’s line let-go, was passing to superintend the making of more sail, when the steward turned aft again and almost collided with him. There was a very sub- dued exchange of words; even the second mate could detect that, since he was watching keenly. Then the mate loudly warned the steward to look where he was going in future, and they separated, each to his job. Then the cook’s grotesque visage was thrust out of the door, and for five seconds he regarded the pair of receding backs with a gaze of such bitter intensity that Mr. Tudgay involuntarily turned away with a shiver. The incident might have suggested a mystery to any one except a buoyant, youthful second mate scarcely out of his time, to whom the world was a round ball made in such fashion that good square- rigger sailors might be developed on out- and-home voyages to the colonies, which constituted a circumnavigation of the globe every time. As it was, Tudgay was far more concerned in thinking what the luck of watch-picking would bring him out of the frightful mob of incompetents struggling with uncertain hands and feet at the appalling task of putting sail on a full clipper. ONE of Captain Kirk’s good habits was the holding of deck church every Sunday forenoon, and per- haps it was all the better as a habit inas- much as his ship always carried a number of boys out of decent homes. With Sunday arriving, and his ship stretching away on a composite Great-Circle track for the Horn; with his hulking, scowling, incapable crew at least hammered into some sort of shape, and ship’s business going on with more or less smoothness through sheer capacity of himself and mates; Captain Kirk set the boys to work laying out reading-desk and books on the poop, and cast speculative eyes upon the parson-like steward. “Skeets, ye look as if some time ye had taught Sunday-school,” he said. “Can ye conduct a sairvice for me?” “I could have done a while ago, sir,” re- plied the steward in a voice simply soaked with humility; “but I’m a backslider, sir, a sinner and a renegade, sir; and J can not make a mockery before those young, inno- cent lads. Please excuse me, sir.” Mr. Tudgay turned away with a face convulsed. “Innocent boys!” he choked. “I wonder how, much o’ the cabin delicacies the inno- cent boys ha’ robbed him of already!” He might also have wondered what such a smug-looking steward, such a model of humility, had said to that tough-looking cook on the first day aboard to send him in- side his galley so hurriedly. But since there was no substitute, Captain Kirk would not permit service to be delayed; the skipper took up his place at the desk himself, at the break of the poop where all hands could hear, and bade the mate muster the men aft. The men came dubiously. Only half an hour before they had all been turned out, on a Sunday morning top, to brace up the yards a trifle, and were sullen about it; for there were enough among them who had made at least one voyage who knew that immemorial custom at sea gave Sunday to the crew. And but for crass stupidity they would have enjoyed the rest, too, except for standing wheel in turn, for the weather was fine even if the wind had not yet settled down true. They came and stood beside the mizzen hatch, sullen and muttering, while the captain opened his book and began to read service. Midway to the end, the wind came ahead again, and without stopping longer than to give the order, the skipper sent both mates with half the men—those whose regular watch on deck it was—to trace the yards sharp up. Even while Mr. Tudgay howled hauling chanteys in the waist to encourageThe Cook of the Yallaroi 161 the men, the service droned along its inevit- able course; and never once did Captain Kirk seem to take his eyes from his prayer- book. But he was the shipmaster even while he played parson, and the end of the service was more than a little broken in con- tinuity. “As it was in the beginning— That’ll do th’ foreyard!— Is now and ever shall be— ’Vast hauling that!— World without end— Well th’ mainyard, Mr. Tudgay!— Amen. —Turn to, ye useless lumps, and coil up the gear!” The skipper finished, flung his book down and stamped the poop, cursing to high Heaven the fate which had sent him such a double-left-legged lot of sons of blind tailors for crew, and audibly wondering what sort of mates he had inherited that they could not lick a crew into shape in three days at sea. Perhaps the crux of the matter was overheard by the second mate at the pantry skylight after he had resumed charge of the deck and captain and mate had gone below to prepare for the noon observa- tion. Under the little square of barred glass the steward stood in his tiny pantry handing out to the mate a cup of cold black coffee left over from breakfast. “Ever see anything like it, Skeets?” de- manded Mr. Galway with a queer chuckle. “Never! The old fool ought to be easy!” returned the sometime meek and lowly steward in tones not at all like those he habitually used. Mr. Tudgay walked away. He scarcely noticed the change in the steward’s tone; but he did notice the lack of respect in the steward’s attitude, and wondered no longer why his own watch were already far ahead of the port watch in ability. BOUNTY ISLANDS, and Antip- odes Islands, both the merest groups of specks in a waste of blank chart, lay on either side of the YallaroVs legitimate course; both groups are desolate, both have Government food and clothing caches, there being in those old clipper days ample need for such to succor not infrequent sailing-ship castaway crews. On some groups a boat is left in some secure crevice. On the Auckland group two boats were al- ways available; on the antipodes it was harder to keep such valuable aids, and the single boat? alloted to the group had been stolen more than once by passing whalers, 11 though, to do those splendid seamen justice, they nearly always left an addition to stores to pay for the boat. Only the exigencies of ’ whaling, and the stem necessities for sound boats of the capable type used on' those island stations, tempted an occasional whaler to accept the gift of the gods and sail away with a new boat. Mr. Galway seemed well versed in all such matters. He would talk for an hour to the second mate about the time when he was in one of the Government vessels depositing those stores; he even went so far as to talk for an hour with some of the unseamanlike crew about the islands; once, so the ugly- visaged cook asserted, he had shown a chart of the ship’s track to two of the seamen, and chatted to them about it in a manner very unlike that of a good chief mate. The cook told the steward about it, and the steward spoke to the skipper, bring- ing the inevitable result; the Old Man sent for the cook, and raved at him from the head of the poop ladder. Mr. Tudgay and two of the boys stood a little aft, and could not avoid hearing the rave. “The Old Man was peeved, wasn’t he?” Tudgay asked of the mate with a grin when watches were changed later. “He ought to be!” retorted Mr. Galway with a fine air of indignation. “You want to watch that plug-ugly cook, Mr. Tudgay; if I’m any judge of men he’s cooking trouble as well as poor grub.” “ I thought it was about chatting with the men that the Old Man ragged-” “Well, it wasn’t. And I’ll thank you to mind your own business. The captain called the cook down because he carried yarns about me, the chief officer, and said I was hobnobbing with fo’csle hands for’ard o’ the galley. You know, as well as any- body, that I was trying, as always, to teach ’em a bit o’ sailorizing.” Mr. Tudgay ought to have known better, of course; he should certainly have known better than to carry his indiscretions so far as he did after supper, when he was alone for a moment with the skipper on the poop. He was rash enough to disclose to Captain Kirk his suspicions that Mr. Galway—the immaculate mate of such wonderful hand- someness, as the steward had been heard to call him—and some of the least seamanlike of the crew, were too thick for the welfare of any sailing-ship bound home Horn ward. The skipper glared at him for a full thirtyAdventure seconds, puffing up like a woman on the verge of hysterics; then he opened out. “Mis-ter Tudgay! I ha’ seen traces o’ insubordination in ye and the boys since leaving Dunedin, and I’ll not have it! I’ll ha’ ye to know that Mr. Galway is as fine a fir-rst mate as I ever had, and a sight better than ye’ll ever make. Ye’re talking the same guff as yonder criminal-lookin’ cook, and I war-m ye, an’ ye can pass it along to yer ugly friend, that if I want to know o’ aught amiss being prepa-ared against the ship, I shall seek for ’t in th’ galley and among galley friends rather than among the loyal par-rt o’ the after-guard. Ha’ no more o’ it, ma man!” “All the same, sir, I’d watch that steward if I had command,” the second mate per- sisted rashly. He really ought to have known better. He was not a first tripper.] “Go to yer room!” roared the skipper, prancing. “Go to yer room for twenty- four hours. And if I see no sign o’ yer retur-min’ sanity then I’ll disrate ye! -me! A-boy would ha’ more sense!” “You’re right—than a blasted thick fool of an Old Man!” muttered Tudgay, and ducked below for a full round of watches of sleep and meals, scarcely hoping that his punishment would be carried out, but de- termined to endure it in real comfort. At a quarter of an hour before middle watch that night one of the apprentices tapped at Tudgay’s porthole and found a wide-awake listener to his report. “The helmsman,” he said, “was that. quiet-looking chap that talks to the cook. He’s the only one as does, sir. He heard all the Old Man said to you, and when I went for’ard to rouse up Lamps for the binnacle lights I saw ’em both very deedy in chat. The cook caught me by the arm and dragged me inside and told me to come along for a dish o’ collops—here it is, sir—” passing a covered dish of savory, steamy viands through the porthole—“and told me if I blabbed a blessed word to anybody else he’d scrag me and clap me into the copper. What’s up, sir? Isn’t trouble, is there?” “Trouble?” repeated Tudgay, sniffing hard at his favorite dish and setting the food carefully into a snug corner of his locker-top. “Don’t talk so - silly! See what I get for mentioning trouble?” “Yes, sir, I know. But that quiet A. B. that was in the galley, he pinched my arm till I nearly hollered, and told me in a voice like a blessed snake’s that he’d skin me and eat me raw after the cook got finished with me if I said a yap either about your grub or about the Old Man’s outbu’st. And he ain’t one to chatter without doing, either, Mr. Tudgay.” “I never saw him do anything,” grumbled Tudgay, eager for his meat but more .eager for news. “•Well, nobody else did till tonight,” chuckled the boy, full of his subject. He was standing within the covering shelter of the half-deck, speaking through a port, hidden and inaudible to all except an intending eavesdropper, and that he had apparently guarded against. “But,” the apprentice continued, “I saw him take on that big whale of a murderer- lookin’ cross-jack-eyed sailor in the port watch and hammer seven bells out of him, sir, and-” “What?” burst forth Tudgay. “Lick that tough? You’re as mad as-” “It’s a fact,” insisted the boy excitedly. “Same tough as Mr. Galway and the steward have been so thick with. Cookie was referee, because all hands o’ the watch wanted to chip in on the side of the plug- ugly. You betcher, sir, the cook said about two words to ’em, and they stood back. Hide the dish when it’s empty, sir; here comes somebody.” The boy vanished into the shadows of the main-course like a bit of itself, and left Mr. Tudgay to his reflections, which were none of the clearest. Youthful as he was, yet his sea time had been spent during a period when it was nothing unusual for a sailing- ship to change crews three or four times in a round voyage; and in Australia, New Zea- land or the West Coast there wer£ strange characters always ready to take the places of deserting crews, and that with the sole idea of getting as far and as fast away from their shipping port as possible. Runaway convicts, weary of chain-gangs and breakwater slavery; unfrocked priests, beyond shame at their fall; deserting sol- diers, defaulting, smug crooks from places of trust, and sheer crooks—all these went to make up crews of those glorious old clip- pers that gave all the romance and spirit to the glorious old sea. Mr. Tudgay had seen every type, and though he had never experienced any deeper trouble from such crews than apprehension, he was still youngThe Cook of the Yallaroi enough to retain a sacred belief that one day big trouble must burst right under his nose. Now he was certain that the trouble- laden voyage had come. He disliked many of the crew; he distrusted the parsonlike steward, and through him the too friendly chief mate; he wasn’t sure about the alarm- ingly ugly cook, but had been inclined to like the man, especially so since receiving that steaming dish of Scotch collops, the spoon of which he was even now licking. But since devouring the savory mess he had begun to think deeply, and now he recalled the amazing subsidence of the powerful cook at a single word from the sharply different steward; he recalled with growing astonishment the boy’s report of that gor- geous forecastle fight, in which the quiet, slightly built seaman who was a friend of the cook’s had whipped the toughest-looking bully in the gang. The threat of the cook to scrag the boy and make stew of him was lost in the newer puzzle of the skipper. Captain Kirk had never failed to show a subtle power in deal- ing with refractory crews before; but here was the one time when he apparently fell down hard, for Tudgay could attribute his own temporary disgrace to no other source than the mental aberration of the Old Man. He lighted a pipe, turned into his bunk and prepared to thresh out the whole matter in the peaceful solitude of his snug prison. He found his tobacco unaccountably difficult to keep alight; he lighted the pipe a half-dozen times, then concluded that there was some- thing amiss more than damp weed, for his pipe was cold the last time he put match to it, though he was convinced that the five minutes’ strenuous drawing at it following the previous ignition must have made the bowl hot— Then the poop bell struck four bells. “Me sleeping like that?” he growled, and slung his legs over the lee-board, rubbing his eyes foolishly and trying to remember any- thing queer in the taste of those collops. Nothing that any man could have said to him at that moment could have altered his opinion that he had been drugged. That sheer sleepiness could make him fall asleep was absurd, to him. He was still puzzling when a yell pealed up on deck that brought him to the floor and at the cabin door in a flash. “Fire!” arose the yell. “Fire!” Almost immediately the key was turned im his lock, and the door opened wide. He burst through into the warding arms of the skipper, himself rubbing his eyes and mak- ing for the companionway in his pajamas. “On deck wi’ ye, Mr. Tudgay, and see what’s amiss!” the Old Man cried. “I canna let ye bur-run, though I believe ye’ve been the cause o’ this, wi’ yer insubordina- tion. Up wi’ ye, man!” Tudgay went, choking back the hot re- tort that rose to his lips, and found the ship’s company milling in a strange sort of frenzy about the poop ladders, while the mate, with two sleepy boys beside him, bore himself more like a liner captain at a fake fire-drill on a swagger passenger steamer than the mate of a prosaic old butter-and-mutton wagon caught on fire in the wastes of the extreme South Pacific. Captain Kirk went past the chartroom on his way to take charge, and glanced at the ship’s last position as pricked off. The run since previous noon brought her within reach of the Bounty group of islands and rocks. He joined the mate at the poop rail. “What ha’ye done? What are ye doing?” he demanded. “Have ye ascertained the source of the fire? Where is it, man?” “Mr. Tudgay is seeking it now, sir,” re- turned the mate with exquisite calm. “He has two A. B.’s and a boy. But I’ve seen these fires before, sir, and if I might make a suggestion I would say we had better run for the islands in case of disaster.” “I ha’ never yet pairmitted slack mates to lose me a ship, Mr. Galway, and if the Lor-rd’s willing I shall not pairmit such now,” retorted Captain Kirk with a hard glance at the debonair mate. “Get ye down yon wi’ Mr. Tudgay and bring me yer repor-rt, shar-rp!”	— The smoke reek smelled like nothing recognizable, though Captain Kirk sniffed with all the sagacity of a forty-year sea- mellowed nose. For a moment he lost sight and sound of the crew, failed to catch the ominous note in their deep mutterings, and strove with all his faculties to put a name to the amazing aroma carried by the swirling smoke. Tinned mutton and tub butter alone make a cargo hard to set fire to; the butter might make a blazing inferno of the fire when started, but neither it nor the canned meat would be likely to catch fire without strong inducement from outside; besides, that puzzling reek was more remi- niscent of very boyish days than of a grave164 Adventure marine disaster. He was still puzzled when the mate rejoined him shrugging his shapely shoulders. “Afraid the ship’s doomed, sir,” Mr. Gal- way reported. “The butter is melting, and soon it’ll catch. The ship will be like a pow- der-mill then, sir. I would suggest we make for the Bounty Islands while the ship floats; then if we have to take to the boats safety isn’t very far away.” In the lantern light at the main-hatch the crew’s faces were turned aft expectantly. Expressions ranged from brutal indifference to sullen ferocity; like a hideous mask the ugly face of the cook appeared among them, and as he faced the poop his features were awork with some undecipherable hope, long- ing or fear. He caught the captain’s eye just at the moment when, together, the mate finished speaking and that strange reek explained itself. As glance met glance, the cook dis- appeared through the hatch, and the mut- tered growl of the crew rose fiercely. “By Christmas, I have it!” roared the skipper, thrusting past the mate and leap- ing down the ladder. “Mr. Tudgay! Do ye keep the poop and hold the course. I’ll see f’r maseP-” “Better let me go, sir,” the mate called out, and started to follow. The skipper flashed back: “ Stay where ye are! I sent ye to seek, and ye smelled a trouble I believe is not here!” He reached the hatch, and the mutter- ing ceased through sheer wonder at his ex- pression. “Touchwood! Punk! Rags!” he mum- bled as he flung his leg over the coaming. In another instant his eyes were water- ing, and his lungs labored in a thick blanket of pungent smoke through which his smart- ing eyes made out a red glow against which an uncouth monster shape silently toiled at some devilish task. He recognized the cook, and in a fury of blind resentment for the trouble on hand hurled himself forward with hands groping for the man’s throat. He gasped sharply when his own strong hands were seized in a grip like a vice, yet as gently as might be, and thrust aside. From the cook’s crooked lips came the soft warning: “Easy sir, easy! Don’t be hasty. I’m helping you. I knew what was the matter, but the crew would have murdered me if I made any move to let you know. I took a chance when I saw you coming, and I’ve nearly got it out now. Here, sir; see the trick?” The man’s eyes glittered and his entire face writhed in horrible mirth. Captain Kirk stared at him dubiously and bade him stand aside. Then he flung himself upon the smoldering pan of smoke-making ma- terials and killed the last red spark before ordering the cook to precede him on deck. A grimace of still intenser subtlety con- vulsed the cook’s face, and he seemed to hesitate; then with a shrug he obeyed, and ran up the bulkhead ladder like a great cat, leaping on deck with a crouch and with guard- ing arms as if expecting a vicious assault. Nothing happened; and the skipper came up to find the entire crew following the mate around the ship, pulling on a brace or sheet here, turning up a coil there, growling the while like a cageful of surly bears. The cook halted as the skipper reached the deck, waited as if to hear some comment on his own case, then at a brief nod turned and slipped into his galley and shut the door silently. Captain Kirk stared after the man with open mouth, then shook his puzzled head and joined the- second mate on the poop. The helmsman was the same quiet seaman who had whipped the forecastle tough, and the faint light from the binnacle played on his face as he raised his eyes to the star he was holding course by. His lips moved as if he too were muttering, and a spasm of rage swept through the skipper from toes to fingers. “The fire’s out, Mr. Tudgay,” he snapped. “ It was a dir-rty trick played for some hid- den pur-rpose which I shall seek and find yet. Will ye keep close watch on yon ugly devil of a cook? I ha’ doubts o’ his pair-rt in this queer business.” “The cook’s all right! He’s about the only one as is!” burst from the helmsman, and the skipper regarded him blankly, amazedly. The man reddened and fixed his eyes on his star again, shifting uneasily on his bare feet, conscious that he had permitted his tongue to carry him into the unforgivable offense-—forcing way into a conversation of his officers without warrant. As if aware of the gravity of his fault, and in desperation determined to be hanged, if at all, for a sheep rather than a lamb, he blurted out again—The Cook of the Yallaroi ‘Tt’s the mate you want to watch!” Captain Kirk peered into the second mate’s face, suspicious that a sound he had caught was too much like a chuckle for respect. Tudgay’s face was wooden, but he could not hide the gleam in his eyes; and the skipper’s mouth was opened to demand attention to duty, which would include punishment of the presumptuous helms- man, when the mate ascended the poop ladder and briskly approached the skipper with a smile that might have meant any- thing from congratulation to amusement. Captain Kirk stepped forward to tell him what the helmsman had said, but Tudgay reached out, touched his sleeve, and said quietly but penetratingly: “Don’t tell him yet, sir. I can tell you something that’ll give reason for the man’s charge.” “Mystery on mystery!” growled the skipper angrily; but he merely received the mate’s report with a nod, and so far ac- ceded to Tudgay’s request as to refrain from telling Mr. Galway what the seaman had exclaimed. But he was reminded of Tudgay’s recent confinement to his cabin, and permitted himself at least one safety valve. “Ye’ll be able to carry on now, Mr. Galway,” he snapped. ‘Mr. Tudgay, retur-rn to yer room. I’ll ha’ chat wi’ ye in the mor-rning.” Tudgay dipped through the companion- way doors like a flash, too well pleased to think of anything but unbroken slumber for the rest of the night. He had no idea what the theatrical fire meant, but placed small importance upon it since it had been dis- covered and smothered. The thing might even have been a monkey trick of some of the boys, perhaps to gain access to the but- ter in the hold; for apprentices got no butter in the Yallaroi. Anyhow it could be nothing serious. Hi£ decision was shocked back upon his mind in the next instant. Entering the saloon to go to his room, he came upon the steward working feverishly to bundle food and blankets anywhere out of sight. As he stood watching the man in wonder, the skipper entered, and uttered a gasp of aston- ishment as genuine as Tudgay’s. “What in the wor-rld are ye doing, steward?” “I heard the cry of fire, sir, and consid- ered it my duty to prepare stores and blank- ets against a boat venture, sir,” replied the man with a greasy smile on his pallid face. Tudgay passed on to his room, unwilling again to risk the skipper’s displeasure; a glance at the dour Scotch face showed him that anger and irritation were very near the surface. “And why are ye not retur-rnin’ the stores and blankets to their proper places now that there’s no need for them?” “I have been laboring under stress, sir. I believed it would not matter if I simply put the things out of the way tonight and asked for some of the boys to help me in the morning.” ‘Well, maybe, maybe,” grunted the skip- per. ‘Go t’ yer bed, then. I doubt ye’re a poor body o’ a man anyhow. Get awa’ wi’ ye.” Growling deep in his honest breast, Captain Kirk crossed over to his stateroom and scowled fiercely as he passed the second mate’s cabin at finding the door a little open and hearing Tudgay’s voice accosting him. “I told ye to tur-rn in, Mr. Tudgay!” he snapped, and turned the key in the lock again. The irrepressible Tudgay popped his head through the fanlight over the door. “I tell you, sir, you must listen!” he said. “I have to tell you something you’ll be sorry if you miss. It concerns the safety of the ship and all hands—” The skipper had passed the door, but the words arrested him. He stood still for a moment, fingering his stubborn chin. Then he stepped back, unlocked the second mate’s door and entered, closing the door softly behind him. “Well? Be shar-rp wi’ yer croaking. I ha’ small patience left for ye.” Tudgay went to the porthole which looked out upon the recess of the half-deck wing, and peered out. The port opened in- wards and was hooked open to an iron de- pending from a beam; a stout iron cover, for bad weather, was fastened to the bulkhead outside. “Come on wi’ yer yarn!” snapped the skipper impatiently. Tudgay turned to him with a queer look. “I thought I caught sight of somebody listening outside, sir,” he said. As he spoke, the skipper’s head jerked around toward the door; he leaped to it with something like belief, and as his hand touched the handle the key outside wasAdventure turned in the lock. At the same moment the iron cover to the port fell with a heavy clang, and the turning of the big screws could be heard plainly. “There, sir!” cried Tudgay excitedly. “I told you-•” “Silence, man!” retorted the skipper furiously. “Why did ye not mak’ me hear ye, instead o’ blatherin’ by little bits about mysterious troubles? Here, clap yer shoul- der to the door!” One assault they made, and a voice from the saloon cried: “Don’t try it, cappen! Next time y’ bang on the door I’ll put a brace o’ lead slugs through the panel!” “ What d’ye mean?” roared Kirk. “Keep civil, and maybe no harm will come to you,” returned the voice. Then came the sound of conflict some- where forward, the report of shots, the clash of weapons, the curses of savage men. The skipper and Tudgay stared at each other in speechless awe, but gradually into the junior’s eyes there crept the inevitable “I-told-you-so” gleam which even the des- peration of the moment could not prevent the skipper detecting. What he might have said about it was halted by a violent im- pact of bodies against the'half-deck bulk- head, the shrill cry of a boy in pain, and the roaring chorus of men in pursuit. Farther away a seaman swore bitterly; a shot rang out; then came the sound of running feet, and the ghastly, suggestive crunch that an ax makes when biting into flesh and bone. “Oh, man!” groaned Captain Kirk in horror. “Why did ye no’ mak’ me listen? What’s to be done? Poor Galway! Poor-” _ “Poor swine!” retorted Tudgay savagely. “I told you to watch him, sir. He’s at the root of this, I’ll bet my life—if I save it.” He hurriedly related that little conversa- tion at the pantry door, when the steward confided to the mate that the Old Man ought to be easy. Captain Kirk’s keen eyes glittered coldly, and his face grew hard and stern as he listened. Outside the cabin all seemed quiet; but forward, far in the bows, sounds were not wanting to indicate that at least some of the crew were loyal to the ship and were putting up a fight. “And d’ye say yon parson-faced steward that’s so thick wi’ the mate frightened the cook into silence wi’ a wor-rd?” cried the skipper, bewildered. ‘I thought the cook ‘I know, sir. And I told you he was all right; so did the sailor, Tom Peters. Now we’re in a boiling stew. What’ll they do with us?” ‘We’ll know soon, ma poor chappie,” replied the skipper with unwonted quiet- ness. ‘The noise is finished, and I doubt there’s no more o’ oor friends on deck. Poor, poor laddies! Look yon!” He pointed out through the small round port-glass in the ship’s side. The stars were sweeping past in a line of glittering splendor. “They’re swingin’ her north!” a A knock came at the door, and a rattle of the handle. “Take yon fire-ax!” ordered the skipper fiercely. Then he crouched beside the door, of which the key was outside, and yelled in answer to the knock: ‘We ha’ an ax! The first man to show his head loses it!” The silence was suddenly intense. It seemed to Tudgay’s strained hearing, as he stood on his toes with every muscle tensed to bring down the murderous blade, as if a sibilant murmur, as of quietest whispering, ran through that awful quiet of the saloon. Then the knock was repeated, and a piti- fully small voice, the voice of a boy, called quaveringly:	_ ^ “It’s only me, sir. Let me in.” ‘Who’s wi’ ye, boy?” demanded the skipper suspiciously. The key was outside, and there seemed to be no reason to ask admittance. ‘Only me, sir!” The assurance lost its value by reason of a sob wrung from a tortured body at the end. “They’re all here, captain!” the lad screamed. “Don’t-” A savage curse accompanied a heavy, chopping blow, and the boy’s shriek rose and fell into the silence of death. ‘By -, Tudgay, burst the door!” roared the skipper, and hurled himself at the panel. The stout oak stood with a quiver. ‘Gi’ me the ax, then! Oh, the devils! The filthy devils!” ‘Don’t, sir!” pleaded the second mate in a whisper, “if they want to get in, and can not, somebody has taken the key sinceThe Cook of the Tallaroi we were locked in. They’ll murder us both if we burst out, before we can pick ourselves up; and we can’t save the lad, sir. We may save the ship, with cunning.” The argument barely reached the furious skipper; but it told, and he fell back, con- tenting himself with a final warning howled through the fanlight: “If a man shows his head I’ll pit th’ ax to it shar-rp!
