Let the mate come doon and talk ta me, ye murderin’ scum!” A savage voice swore, and a head, arm, and gun were thrust in through the opening above the door. “Drop that ax!” the man growled, and swung the gun upon Tudgay. “Brain him wi’ it!” yelled the skipper, and with the words leaped like a cat at the protruded gun-hand and forced it down- ward. Tudgay struck with the back of the ax- head, and the gun clattered to the floor, in- side the cabin; the man fell back among his fellows with a hollow grunt, leaving the white paint of the door streaked with crim- son. “Cap’n, you’d better submit,” came the mate’s silky voice with a suspicion of a harsher fiber woven in the silk now. “The key is lost; Tudgay must have a spare one inside. Throw it out, and I’ll see if I can make terms for you. If you’re stubborn we’ll starve you first, then burst through and handle you.” Captain Kirk sputtered with anger; the hot retort hovered on his tongue when Tudgay seized his arm, put a finger to his lips, and pointed to the side porthole. A soft tapping sounded on the glass, and the face of Brooks, the oldest apprentice, frightened but still plucky, appeared in the circle. The tapping came from a door-key in the boy’s hand. With a gasp of startled gratification at the knowledge that at least one of his lads was safe, the skipper motioned Tudgay to open the port, while he himself answered the mate * through the fanlight. The tone he used efficiently drowned the small noise of opening the port. ‘I have no key. Tudgay has no key—” his canny Scotch conscience urged him to mutter “yet” under his breath—“and if we had all the keys in the ship I’d make no’ ter-rms wi’ a dir-rty mutineer. I ha’ a gun and an ax and a detairmination to see ye all hanged for gallus-crows, as ye are. Do yer wair-rst, ye ruddy pirate, and be - to ye!” “You’ll starve!” “We can do that—but I doubt we won’t.” “The men are desperate; they’ll-” “They may do their-est!” “We’ve two more of the boys. We’ll let you listen to them squealing outside here.” Kirk wavered at this. He cast a poignant glance at Tudgay. The port was open, fresh air came in, and the second mate held up a door-key. The boy, who was hanging precariously over the ship’s side with one hand to the poop gunwale, shook his head vigorously and whispered sobbingly: “I’m the only one nigh, and they ain’t got me yet, and they won’t!” The boy stopped abruptly, clapped his free hand alongside the other, and wriggled upward out of sight as if he had caught a warning sound on deck. Skipper gazed at second mate, and both saw in the other a resolve to fight. “I’ll hear one squeal fair-rst,” he replied to the threat. A deep rumble of voices seemed to fill the saloon; but Galway made no retort, and silence fell again, leaving the captives in a quandary as to its meaning. “Maybe this is the only gun, sir,” sug- gested Tudgay. “Likely. But I widna believe any man fool enough to try to carry a ship o’ this size wi’ one weapon. What’s yon screech- ing?” Both listened; then Tudgay darted to the window that opened on to the half-deck recess, which the mutineers had bolted outside. The screws of the iron cover were turning. Tudgay shut the port, gave the in- side butterfly nuts an extra twist, flung the thick red curtains over .the glass and pinned them, then stood aside, motioning the skip- per to the other side, and both waited for the new assault. “We’ll soon know about the guns,” mut- tered Kirk, his eyes aglitter as he watched the saloon fanlight against surprize from that direction. Soon the outer cover was raised and hooked, and a hearty volley of deep-sea oaths followed the discovery of the blinding curtain over the inner glass. A port glass is a thing of marvelous toughness, and yields to nothing less than terrific force. So far as forcing it, unless with gunfire or blows of aAdventure maul, the outer iron cover might almost as well have, been on. A few futile blows with the port-key followed, then Galway’s voice sounded in peremptory command, the voices outside receded, and soon the greater angle of the ship’s list told of freshening wind and work on deck. “Pairhaps they’ll leave us alone a while now,” remarked Kirk. He was peering in every corner of the little cabin, and his gaze darkened every moment, while the little circle of the side port grew lighter with dawn. “Ha’ ye no outlet—no hatch to yer room?” he growled. Tudgay raised the square of faded carpet. “There was the hatch into the old sail- locker, sir. But when you built the sail- room on deck you had the carpenter calk this up.” The removal of the carpet re- vealed a square hatch, flush with the floor, and the line of pitch around it was hard and shiny with age and the chafing of the carpet. “And what’s beneath now?” “A wing of the store-room, sir. Spuds and flour mainly, I believe.” “Har-rd stuff to shift wi’ such small scope,” mused the Old Man with a shake of the head. “But man, we must do some- thing! Gi’ me the ax, and do ye get busy wi’ yer knife. Tak’ oot the calking. Mak’ no noise, and keep yer eyes peeled at yon fan- light.” They fell to work as a yell echoed through the ship from the direction of the foremast. AS THE horizon silvered with the first light of day, three dark figures huddled in the foretop silently helped a fourth on to the platform beside them. “I got the key to them, cook!” the new- comer, Brooks, said gleefully, panting and bright-eyed from his perils encountered in passing the key through Tudgay’s port- hole, his subsequent Indian-like avoidance of enemies, and his climb aloft. “Good boy!” muttered the ugly cook, clapping the lad on the shoulder softly. The other larger figure resolved itself into the quiet sailor who had so manfully pro- claimed the cook’s honesty to the skipper while at the wheel. Besides these, and the boy just welcomed, another boy crouched there, wide-eyed with fright, but quiet, and willing to share whatever might come. The lightening decks beneath them were deserted apparently except for a small hud- dle of men right aft by the wheel; but here and there, about the galley and forecastle, dark splotches lay on the clean planks which looked queerly like sleeping men, yet sug- gested more than sleep. The cook laughed bitterly as he scanned the ship; and his laugh was a thing of horri- ble mirth, for his head was roughly ban- daged in bloody canvas, and one powerful arm still dripped blood from a hastily tied handkerchief at the bend of the elbow. The seaman, Tom, was in no better case, for his blue check shirt was a ghastly plaster of red, and his hair needed no pomatum to slick it to his forehead. The smaller of the boys quivered with the pain of a long, shallow flesh cut down his face; and of them all only Brooks appeared unhurt. Still the cook could laugh. “Well, lads,” he said, cautiously stretch- ing a cramped foot over the edge of the top. “something’s got to be done right now. Here’s daylight, and they’ll see us in a little while. Then we shall be targets for their guns whenever we try to move. They’ll shoot us if we go down and starve us if we stay up here—unless the captain and Mr. Tudgay bust loose and surprize ’em, which I don’t exactly count on.” “The Old Man and Tudgay’d be mutton by now if it wasn’t for Johnny Brooks here, as pinched the key out o’ the second’s door,” remarked Tom. “I don’t understand what it’s all about. They never took me into their conflabs.” “They did me, though, at first,” grinned the cook. “Looked more like one of them- selves than you, maybe. Didn’t you see how they waited on me coming out o’ the hold, when I showed up the fake fire?” “But what is their notion?” piped Brooks. He shivered in the cold dawn; and his companion, a first-voyage boy, cowered between the men, fearful of what daylight might bring to add to the horrors of the night. “They’ll be copped sooner or later,” the boy went on, “and murder’s been done. They’ll all stretch rope, won’t they?” “Aye, surely. Meanwhile they’re in charge of a fine ship, well found, full o’ grub apart from the stores, and heading no’th far away from any shipping track, toward the littlest known part of the Pacific.” The cook hesitated with a keen sidelongThe Cook of the Yallaroi glance at the sailor, who met his glance and held it an instant, then nodded. “Might as well out wi’ it, Joe,” he said. “ ’T won’t make no difference that I can see. If we get clear, you’ve done your bit, enough to square yourself anyhow; if we don’t-” The interrupted sentence was more ex- pressive than many words. The cook glanced down upon the still silent deck, and went on: “Short and sweet, then: Every man-jack o’ this fine ship’s crew, from mate and stew- ard down to me—that’s exceptin’ Tom and you lads, and the skipper and^econd, o’ course—is a wanted bushranger and sheep- thief. Yes, me as well, sonny,” he grinned in answer to a look of incredulity on the face of the younger boy. “Only difference is that they all cut and run and got away, while I got jailed but was let out by the front door. Never mind why, now. Galway and the steward was the two bosses of a gang o’ cattle-lifters, and I knew ’em all years ago, but quit ’em when I got pinched while out on business for the gang. They’d just reached Dunedin in a stolen boat when the Old Man found me and shipped me for cook. Tom already had a job here, and steered me to him. In five minutes Galway steps up, asks for a chance and gets the skipper jumpin’ for joy by offerin’ him a full crew, steward and all, complete. “Soon as they see me, they thought to rope, me in along with them, and told me their scheme. They was to take the ship, though they didn’t say how at first, sail her nigh to Bounty Islands and shove the skip- per and boys and second mate ashore there where they needn’t starve because of the Government stores, and where the visiting ship would be sure to call in a few months. Tom, here, they figured on either taking in along with them after he knew the plan, or slipping him over the side if he refused. “Soon as I see that fire, I knew it was the first trick; and when Galway proposed standing up for the islands I was certain of it. This mutiny is the final bid, and blood’s been shed. “There won’t be any landing on well- stocked islands for the Old Man now. They’ll kill us all, starve us or shoot us, unless we get top dog pretty quick. They won’t stop for nothing, with the wide sea before them and a stout ship to carry ’em to some place where nobody knows ’em and shipping men ain’t overparticular about real ownership when a fine vessel’s offered for sale at a foolish price.” “You ought to have told the captain at first!” cried Brooks. “I tried to,” replied the cook simply. “Didn’t ye see how I never moved a peg on deck wi’out somebody dogging me? Did ye not see how the steward always brought or sent the galley stores along for me, so that I never went aft? Laddie, I was worth more to you and the rest alive than dead.” “That’s no lie, neither!” growled Tom, bal- ancing his sheath-knife on his palm and glaring hungrily down upon Galway, who was just emerging from the saloon com- panionway. At the same moment Galway, glancing aloft in the way all good sailors have on coming on deck, whether they are good or bad men or only indifferent, caught sight of the huddled figures in the top, now, in growing light, clearly distinguishable from the bulk of the masthead and the main- topgallant-staysail tied up there. A ripping oath escaped him, and he cursed his helms- man and the men on the poop luridly for failing to detect and account for the refugees before. Pointing to them, he or- dered them brought down. “Aye! Come get us!” roared Tom, and his knife flashed in a deadly streak straight at Galway. Only the swaying out from the mainmast of a main-buntline—the mainsail was furled —deflected the knife and sent it quivering into the planks two feet wide of its aim. “Now you’ve spilled the soup!” growled the cook angrily. Two men started forward and climbed on to the rail at the fore-rigging; but in the nearer crisis the cook appeared to see some cheering factor, for he chuckled. “Tom, blast me if you ain’t swapped that knife to some purpose!” he said. “Now we know they ain’t got any more guns, or them men wouldn’t dare try to get us that way. Look!” At the other rail two other men started to climb, and four seamen together approached the futtock rigging with knives in their clenched teeth, while, below, aft, Galway flogged them on with vicious oaths. The cook produced his cleaver, suspiciously dark as to blade, and spat on his hands; but his face grew stony as he noticed that his was170 Adventure the only weapon. The sight of it, however, protruded over every edge of the top in turn, caused the four climbers to be wary before setting foot on those overhanging futtock-ratlines. “Go on!” roared Galway savagely. “He can only get one o’ you!” “Come up yerself then, an’ let him play wi’ you!” yelled back a dubious mutineer. At the same moment he made a deter- mined upward dash, grabbed at the top, then swung downward again as the cleaver sang and bit deep into the structure of the top. But the men on the other side had seized the opening; one darted upward, reached over, and grabbed one of the boys by the leg, wringing a cry of terror from the lad. Like a big ape the cook twisted around, flung himself full length across the plat- form, and brought down the cleaver upon the outstretched arm. Hand and forearm parted from the rest at the elbow, and the man fell headlong to the deck below, show- ering those in the top with spurting drops. Galway roared, but the man’s shriek drowned his roar. On the other side again two men made an infuriated rush, and now the cook saw the hopelessness of it all. “Up, Tom!” he gritted, darting from side to side with chopping blows. “Up with you by the main-t’-gallant-stay! Up, boys, after him. Can ye climb, sonny?” he asked, pausing a second to place his hand gently upon the bowed head of the younger boy. “Sure ye can, laddie! Up wi’ you all now, and if ye make it we have them!” One by one the boys swarmed aloft on the stay, Tom taking last place instead of first, and immediately under the trembling boy in case he needed a cheering word or a help- ing hand. The cook kept the top inviolate through sheer animal tirelessness and agili- ty, until the attackers drew back for breath. No man can hang indefinitely to futtock rigging and fight with one hand. Glancing aloft, the cook saw Tom reach the topgallant masthead and make the boys secure; then, peering over the edge of the top to be sure of his distance, he slung his cleaver to his neck with a rope-yarn stop cut from a coil of small rigging and swarmed up the stay after his fellows. “Now if they follow up the stay I can chop down a bloomin’ army!” panted the cook, unslinging his fearsome weapon. But Galway was watching, and as soon as the first man crossed the foretop and gripped the stay to swarm to the mainmast, his voice rose in a savage bellow: “Come down, you blasted lunatic! All of you come down, and get after ’em by the main rigging.” One man remained on deck by the fore shrouds, to guard against swift descent; another stood by the main, and Galway himself watched the mizzen; then every available mutineer swarmed aloft on the main, determined to overwhelm that little bunch of clinging figures high up on the slender topgallantmast. And a new influ- ence entered into the game; the parson-like steward, Skeets, emerged from the cabin door, glanced over the ship, and spoke in a sharp tone to Galway, who glared fiercely, but made no retort. “Come down, all but two of you!” yelled the steward, who had apparently assumed command. The men climbing, almost under the main-top, paused. In the breathing-space the cook, seeing the precariousness of his position on so frail a hold, slithered down a backstay and gained the main-top, where he might still do stout service against his attackers. The boys were safer farther aloft. “Come down and work the ship!” cried the steward. “Leave ’em up there; they’ll come down when they get hungry or thirsty!” The men hesitated, some descending, others seeming to hang upon Galway’s orders. The steward flung his arms about in rage, and cursed the mate. “Leave ’em up there, and who’s to stop them spotting a ship and signaling her be- fore we can?” yelled Galway. “Shoot ’em down then.” “Aye, I might, if you hadn’t sent the Old Man a present of the gun!” bawled Galway, thrusting his convulsed face close to the pallid visage of the steward. Up aloft the cook and his companions beg^n to take keen interest in the row; the hesitating seamen in the rigging began slowly to follow their descending mates. j Tom, at the maintopmasthead with the boys,icalled down softly,land the cook heard—j “Joe, there’s smoke to the s’uth’ard!” “Up to the truck wi’ ye, then, and bend yer shirt to the royal-pole for a signal o’ dis- tress,” returned the cook. Then, sure that it would be done, he turned his attention to the little scene on the poop.The Cook of the Yallaroi 171 It swiftly reached a climax when the steward* having lost completely all vestige of his godly aspect, brought up his fist in a lightning-like uppercut to Galway’s chin that knocked him clear off the poop into the arms of the first hurrying men. In two jumps the steward was upon his foe again; but now the men took a hand, some hauling Galway clear, others rallying around the cock-a-whoop steward. And with Galway on his fe,et again, pale with rage, coldly mur- derous of eye, the fight closed in grim silence; not three blows had been exchanged before all hands closed in a milling, savage melee. It was one of the boys who first caught sight of a cautiously protruding head at the lazaret-hatch abreast of the wheel, the wheel that swung as the ship plunged along, un- attended since Galway, who had taken it to send every available man after the fugi- tives, had left it in turn to beard the steward. “Tom! Joe! There’s the Old Man!” the lad cried, scarcely lowering his voice from its piercing treble. Had he shouted the news none of the furi- ous mob on deck would have heard it; for blood was flowing now, knives and belaying- pins hammered and cut, while Galway and Skeets were locked in a death grapple, thumbs at eyes or fingers sinking into throats. Aloft at the main truck flew a blue check shirt, bravely proclaiming to all who saw it, in the ancient language of the wind-driven navy, that a seaman wished to quit his ship. Tom slid down, and as he passed the lads he bade them follow him, for the cook was calling them softly, and all the while he was striving to attract the attention of that peering head aft. Huddled in the main-top, they saw Cap- tain Kirk hoist himself swiftly through the hatch, and turn to give a hand to Tudgay. When both stood free, plastered from head to foot with flour like millers, armed with ax and revolver, one of the boys excitedly cast to the winds all caution and threw his leather-vizored cap at them. It pitched fair at the skipper’s feet, and in a moment com- munication was established by arm sema- phore. Sure of the situation now, the skipper and second mate advanced to the poop rail, each snatching up an iron pin from the mizzen rigging as he passed, in case of need, and stood gazing down upon their foes battling for them. The brutal fight had reached a point where no outside interfer- ence could break the intense application of the combatants to the business in hand. The mate and steward swayed and stum- bled blindly among the others, knees tot- tering, breath wheezing through red froth at the bps. Galway’s handsomeness had departed like a Summer cloud under the red disfigurement of an empty eye-socket; the steward’s lips were drawn tightly back from grinning teeth, but his pale face had grown purple. “Come on, Tom!” gritted the cook. “Lads, stay here. You can do no good. The thing’s a’most over anyhow. Now, Tom!” This time he gripped the cleaver in his teeth as he swarmed dowft by a backstay, closely followed by the seaman. As they reached the deck, the cook darted into his galley, and emerged wielding the “tor- mentors”—a fearful weapon, that huge salt- meat fork, in capable hands. “Take the cleaver, Tom,” he said, grip- ping the great two-pronged fork, “and let’s clean this thing up.” They rushed aft, and reached the edge of the fight without notice. The deck was lit- tered with groaning ruffians, and stained in great dark patches. The skipper and Tud- gay leaped down the ladder to join their allies as the two chief combatants pitched headlong and brought down others in their fall. “Tap ’em, lads! Gi’ ’em a bit wallop ta quiet ’em,” sang out the Old Man, and suited the action to the words by knocking all the fight out of a cursing scrapper with a blow of his iron club. So far had the fight gone that there was no call for cleaver or fork, ax or pistol. In less than half a minute the two belaying-pins had scattered all the remaining wits of the mutineers, and then the lads were called down to fetch rope to bind up the fallen. “Put the ship on her proper course, Mr. Tudgay, till we ascertain damages,” said the skipper, coolly turning over the mate with his foot. Galway breathed stertorously, his limbs twitching. The steward breathed too, but faintly. “Tom sighted a steamer making t’wards us, sir,” said the cook. “We flew a signal to her.”172 Adventure He pointed aloft where the shirt fluttered brightly against a clear sky. “I doubt if any steamer’ll want to relieve us o’ such cattle,” muttered the Old Man, shaking his head sorrowfully as he remarked the state of his ship and thought of round- ing the Horn with such a crew, even if he got them in hand again. “If so be as it’s a Government steamer, sir, as is by any chance looking for us, she’ll be only too glad to take care of ’em,” said the cook simply. “Why? What do ye know, mon?” Tom chipped in and told the skipper briefly the history of the crew, and gradu- ally Captain Kirk’s eyes were opened wide. He said nothing until he had taken the binoculars aloft and regarded the distant steamer intently for several minutes. Then he too used tRe backstay for speedier descent, and sent Brooks to take the wheel while Tudgay looked up the code-book and deciphered the several signals repeated to him. “What d’ they say?” “New Zealand naval steamer, sir, telling us to heave-to until she looks over our crew.” “Then ye were r-right, cook!” cried Kirk with emotion. “I ha’ been blind. We’ll ha’ ta go back now for a crew, I doubt, un- less yon steamer can spare us men. Bring her by the wind, lads. We ha’ no men to heave her to. I’ll hoist a signal masel’ to tell him so. “But, cook, I dinna want ye ta be taken wi these gallus crows. Ye say ye were wi’ ’em once. See, mon, if ye hide awa’ below, I’ll tell ’em ye were killed and chucked owerboard. Awa wi’ ye. Yonder comes the steamer.” “Joe don’t have to hide, sir!” Tom blurted out. “Let the mon speak f’r hissel’.” “He won’t. I’m his brother, and I speak for him. Joe’s done his bit in chokee, but he ain’t wanted. He got his pardon same time as he got his face all chopped up like it is, wi’ the shovels of mutineerin’ convicts as was trying to murder the warders. Get yer parchment, Joe, in case ’tis wanted.” The cook, now that the emergency had passed, resumed his air of unobtrusiveness that had marked him since joining the ship. He went to his sea-chest and returned aft, clutching a folded paper with much the same expression as a boy wears when presented with a certificate for good conduct in front of his mates. The look sat queerly upon his marred face, but he broke into a quiet grin as the steamer swept around and lowered boats, and the dour Scotch skipper remarked: “I’ll ha’ ma eyes owerhauled before I sail a ship ta sea again. I thought yon steward, Skeets, looked like a pure-white woolly lamb, and ye looked to be a shaggy wolf, cook. I asked the mon ta read a sairr-vice the fairst Sabbath oot, d’ye mind? “A-ah, weel! ’Tis the frailty o’ us poor humans, I doubt. Ye’re all wool, cook; all white, fleecy wool, f’r all y’r har-rd mug, and—” the steamer’s boats were alongside, the officer hailing—“and if oor friends ower the side tak’ us in tow, or lend us hands ta wor-rk ship, but leave us the dead ta bury, I’ll ask ye ta r’read the sairrvice afore we dump ’em to their brither shar-rks!”Our Camp-Fire came into being May 5, 1912, with our June issue, and since then its fire has never died down. Many have gathered about it and they are of all classes and degrees, high and low, rich and poor, adventurers and stay-at-homes, and from all parts of the earth. Some whose voices we used to know have taken the Long Trail and are heard no more, but they are still memories among us, and new voices are heard, and welcomed. We are drawn together by a common liking for the strong, clean things of out-of- doors, for word from the earth’s far places, for man in action instead of caged by cir- cumstance. The spirit^of adventure lives in all men; the rest is chance. But something besides a common interest holds us together. Somehow a real com- radeship has grown up among us. Men can not thus meet and talk together without growing into friendlier relations; many a time does one of us come to the rest for facts and guidance; many a close personal friendship has our Camp-Fire built up between two men who had never met; often has it proved an open sesame between strangers in a far land. Perhaps our Camp-Fire is even a little more. Perhaps it is a bit of leaven working gently among those of different station toward the fuller and more human understand- ing and sympathy that will some day bring to man the real democracy and brotherhood he seeks. Few indeed are the agencies that bring together on a friendly footing so many and such great extremes as here. And we are numbered by the hundred thousand now. If you are come to our Camp-Fire for the first time and find you like the things we like, join us and find y.ourself very welcome. There is no obligation except ordinary manliness, no forms or ceremonies, no dues, no officers, no anything except men and women gathered for interest and friendliness. Your desire to join makes you a member. A WORD from J. D. Newsom in connec- tion with his story in this issue: New York City. The custom of allowing captives to live a fairly free and independent life until their heads are needed may sound queer, but it is an actual fact. Such men are allowed to marry into the tribe and usually go to their deaths quite cheerfully when the time comes. Their children, however, belong to the mother’s clan, .group or moiety and are allowed to grow up under the tutelage of their maternal uncle. It is a rather “quaint” custom.—J. D. Newsom. TO MY relief and surprize, my explana- tion to Camp-Fire of our magazine’s use of dashes instead of cusswords was strongly backed up by nearly all the letters that came in, and there have been a lot of these letters. I’m glad. I can’t see my way to any other course in the case of this particular magazine and, on the other hand, I don’t like to follow any course to which the majority of you are opposed. But I forgot to cover one point. Two of you raise this point vehemently in letters to me and they are, I think, quite right. Dashes for cusswords, yes, but when it comes to dashes for “God,” “hell,” “devil,” etc., used, not as cusswords, but in a per- fectly legitimate way, there’s no need of dashes. Yet our magazine has, on various occasions, used them in such cases. That use of the dash is as offensive to me as it is to any of the rest of you. I’m “agin’ ” it and always have been. But there are seven of us on the staff and, while most of us have been here for years, there are changes now and then. As there are a few thousand other things to watch out for, this distinc- tion in the use of dashes isn’t always fol- lowed or even clearly seen. 173174 Adventure For that matter, it’s often hard to draw the line. Those of you who have read proof know that “style and consistency” are often hard to maintain. On one side the line you’ll get a series of cases, each case lead- ing logically on to the next, demanding for it the same treatment, and all of a sudden you’ll find you’ve crossed the line—and done it logically too. Or, stated the other way, you’ll put one case on one side the fine, later put a second case on the other side, and then find there’s no logical distinction between the two. We have lots of arguments on the staff over cussword cases. When is a cussword a cussword? Sounds easy to answer, but sometimes we don’t find it so. Also, as stated already, sometimes we just make mistakes. Our intention is to use the word itself whenever it is not plainly a cussword as used in that particular instance. Some- times we’ll make mistakes, or at least our judgment will differ from yours. Just be easy on us and credit us with good inten- tions. Another thing. In poetry we’ll “let ’em ride” even as cusswords unless they get too dog-goned bad. That isn’t quite logical perhaps, but when the word is essential to the rhyme it is pretty violent stuff to stick in a dash. AN INTERESTING report from one of ■ our comrades of the gentler sex regard- ing Daniel Boone’s brother and his family in general: The name and fame of Daniel Boone are insepar- ably connected with the history of Kentucky. The old pioneer has been honored in many ways, and at the present time a fine statue of him, clad in hunt- ing garb, coon-skin cap, rifle in hand, stands in Cherokee, one of the most beautiful parks about Louisville. Y/f ANY of the descendants of Daniel and his broth- ers still live in the State, the old Boone home- stead being located at Boone’s Landing about thirty miles below the city on the Ohio River, and not far distant is the site of an old mill built by the Boones at a place caEed Booneport. The descendants of Hiram Boone, a brother of Daniel, live at Boone’s Landing and a member of the family, the late Grandison Boone, used to relate that when a small boy he saw Daniel Boone, who stopped there on his way to Missouri to bid his kinsmen good-by. Grandison Boone had but a childish recollection of the old trapper, but his description of him tallies exactly with that of others who saw him at that time. Boone had blue or gray eyes and brown hair, and at the present time many of his lineal descendants have the same, while the collaterals are brunettes, their dark hair and eyes probably inherited from Boone’s wife, who was a Bryan. T HAVE been present at many of the family gatherings of the Boones, which country-bred folks would call “dinings.” Early in the day the members of the various families would begin to ar- rive. Men, women and children, horses, mules and hound dogs; the squawking of chickens would be heard; huge baskets of vegetables would be borne in from the garden, long rows of cakes were outspread in the Summer kitchen while in the inner kitchen, where the chefs of the household were on duty, the most luscious odors would fill the air. City folks would stand aghast at the generous supplies of food prepared and its infinite variety. Meanwhile the men sat under the trees discussing crops and politics over tall glasses of cold mint juleps. Some chewed tobacco, some smoked reeky pipes, while the horses and mules, taken out of the traces, browsed contentedly close by. The hounds tumbled in the grass with the youngsters, or loafed about the kitchen door, slobbering in delicious an- ticipation of the feast. When dinner was served all the guests sat about a long table loaded to the guards with eatables. Fried chicken, rosy old country-cured ham, chicken and dumplings, corn pudding, field beans and bacon, bacon and greens, tomatoes, raw and cooked, butter beans, spoon com bread, rhubarb, heaps of snowy mashed potatoes, buttermilk and hot coffee, pie, cake, preserves of all kinds, pickles, all the riches of field, garden and orchard were outspread, while the women of the immediate household waited on the table and purged their guests to help themselves bountifully—“Uncle Hiram you must take some more cake,” “Cousin Squire, I want you to sample my cornbread,” and “Aunt Becky, I made this lemon pie after your own recipe.” 'JpHE conversation was Boone from post to finish: all the happenings in the family, births, mar- riages and deaths. Old stories were revived, par- ticularly one to the effect that when the State of Kentucky brought the remains of Daniel Boone and his wife back from Missouri the real bodies were never given up, but weights placed in the caskets so that the coffins, buried with so much pomp at Frankfort, hold no honored dust, but as most of the Kentuckians know no better they are perfectly satisfied. Another story revived and retold was that of Daniel’s brother, Squire Boone, who, it is stated by historians, was killed by the Indians. The family indignantly disclaim this statement and declare that he settled in Indiana, on the Ohio, near Boone Township, and there raised his family. There was a small cave near his home and on the walls of this cave Squire carved religious and worldly-wise sentences. One of the former ran, “I sit and sing my soul’s salvation.” One of the latter was to the effect, “Beware of intentions for fear of preven- tions,” possibly intimating that it was foolish to tell what you intended doing last some one frustrate your plans. When old Squire felt his end was near, he got his coffin, and selected the cave for a place of burial. He made his three sons promise solemnly to place his remains there, sit up with them for three days and nights, then close up the entrance forever.The Camp-Fire 175 This they did and for many years the old man re- mained unmolested in his rocky sepulchre. QOME time in the 60’s--purchased a farm on the Kentucky side of the river, below Louis- ville, and it was there that he died on a black storm- swept night. His son,--, a thoughtless, rollicking young man spent some time on this farm, having for his companion a member of one of the oldest and most aristocratic families in the State. One day these two, accompanied by a negro servant and their hunting dogs, crossed the river in a skiff and went hunting in the Indiana woods. During the trip they imbibed freely from their hunting- flasks and, after wandering over a big stretch of country, finally stumbled on the Boone cave. I have never heard whether they knew the story of this cave or not. Anyhow in a spirit of reckless- ness they broke open the rude entrance, dragged the skeleton of old Squire from its resting place and scattered the bones about everywhere. It is whis- pered to-day that a distinguished member of the Boone family, now living, has one of the leg-bones of old Squire, though he can never be persuaded to admit the fact, even to his own household. -and his gay companion finally withdrew to their boat and the negro rowed them back to the Kentucky shore, fully convinced in his superstitious mind that the party was damned forever. It is certainly true that —— had persistent bad luck after this escapade, and died a violent death, while the Boones regarded his taking off in the light of a retributive justice. The waters of the Ohio still glide peacefully past the cave, the forests have disappeared and prosper- ous settlements sprung up close by, but the memory of old Squire Boone lives immortal in the traditions of his descendants and those of his brothers. One and all they snap their fingers at the historians and their story that the Squire was killed by Indians, and declare that he died peacefully in his bed, wear- ing his scalp to the last.—Elvira Millie Slaugh- ter. OLLOWING Camp-Fire custom, Leon- ard H. Nason rises and introduces himself on the occasion of his first story in our magazine: Chicago. So now I am come to take a place at the Camp- Fire, to meet with other read dr s, adventurers and authors. The first I have ever been, the second some little time, and the third have but now be- come, hence this standing up and this bow and this present writing. For all that I am of tender years, I have been with the most of those about this Fire, what time they were in the far places of the world, upon their different occasions. He that went down to the sea and reefed topsails in the gale and passed the weather earing, I have been with him, albeit I stayed in on the yard, in the bunt, and clung to the jack-stay with both hands, for which I was beaten about the ears by the second mate. He that hath been to the Spanish Main, and on the beach thereof, I have been with him, and have been cast out of the American consulate, whither I had gone for food, on my ear, and gone to the British, and been be- trayed by my accent, and then sought a missionary, and been given the grubstake that my own Govern- ment had denied me. What so cheap in the Central Americas as the life of a Yankee? Those among you that know the feel of a squee- gee, and a marine’s boot, and the joy of smuggled cigarets in the brig, and no tables down for days, and a tompion lost in a seaway, with the North At- lantic coming in through the breech-block like a shower-bath, I have been with you, and have pol- ished brightwork and seen the world through a port- hole and strained shellac through bread for the alco- hol therein like the saltiest shell-back among you. And last, I was with those who went to war. Hos- pital, huit chevaux, replacement camp, M.P.’s, ship- wreck, vin ordinaire, submarines, I knew them all. From Mezy to Montfaucon I went, I and my com- rades of the Rock of the Marne, with our battle cry of “When do we eat?” on our lips. Two bites of the dust I took, one at Mont St. Pere, and one in the Argonne, which last was near to being the death of me. And then at last I was cast away on Fire Island with the Northern Pacific and beat a watery grave by a pig’s whisper. So much for the past. I have hung up my sword, and made my old tin derby into a flower-pot, and given myself in marriage, and will henceforth live in peace, and so to my grave, to which I expect to go in a hearse, and without boots. It is in my mind that this can not be fulfilled, for it hath been said of me that I was bom to be hanged. Now then, greeting to my new comrades of the Camp-Fire. And to the many gallant gentlemen and true adventurers that I have met upon the Road, to whom these words may come, greeting. May they read my efforts with a kindly and not too critical an eye. How!—Leonard H. Nason. COMRADE MARINO forgot to give his own address, so I couldn’t get in touch with his friend. If the latter sees this, will he please tell me in just what way this magazine is printing British propaganda? Or will any one else who has discovered British propaganda in our magazine write me the details? Generalities on the subject mean nothing except probably some one with eyes larger than his brains or else some one who’s busily engaged himself in propa- ganda on the other side. Give me concrete, detailed facts or don’t bother to talk at all. Also, kindly give me your word that you yourself are not a propagandist for either side. Those who accuse us of anti-British prop- aganda are given the same invitation. I’m very eager to find out whether this magazine is pro or anti in this question. So far I don’t know. While we’re at it, let’s everybody send in detailed charges as to the magazine’s being pro or anti on all the other foreign nations. Might put it to a general vote and then we’ll know just which nations we’re rooting for or against. The next step after that is to get the Secret Service to work finding out176 Adventure just how many millions of dollars are being paid to subsidize us and then make us stop taking money from both sides. •	Seriously, I’ll venture that an appreciable percentage of these propaganda-hounds on various subjects are getting real money for writing these letters of protest. Still seri- ously, I’ll plead guilty without hesitation to friendliness toward our Canadian neigh- bors to the north. If our country and the British Empire ever do go to war, I—and this magazine if I’m in charge—will do our little best against them. I hope there never is such a war—nor a war between us and any other nation, and meanwhile I can see no earthly reason for not being friendly to the Canadians with whom we’ve lived in peace for over a hundred years. Yes, in the magazine I’ve stood against attempts to breed ill feeling between Cana- dians and us. Why not? You who try to breed trouble are the ones to justify your- selves. We’re at peace with Canada; why should any one do other than wish to pre- serve that peace? As to the British Empire in general, it’s done a lot of splendid things and a lot of rotten things, has splendid points and rotten points. So have most other countries, in- cluding ourselves. I have no brief for or against it. Let’s judge other nations chiefly by what they do and are from now on. As to these people with their heads full of charges of propaganda against our maga- zine, they make me sick. If they’re such - good Americans they’d better put in their time doing things for America instead of talking against other countries. If they’re not good Americans, they are prop- agandists for some other country and have no business talking to Americans at all. If any misguided fanatics do send in charges, as facetiously urged above, no such generalities as “such and-such a story is anti-this or pro-that” will get any consider- ation. Get down to cold, hard, detailed facts or don’t talk. If this magazine is being made the unconscious tool of any propa- ganda Pll be might glad to learn about it. If it’s the conscious tool, I don’t know about it. If I’m a liar, produce the facts. INCIDENTALLY, since this friend of comrade Marino’s charges us with British propaganda, he won’t object, not to charges, but to questions, about himself. He and his father are 100% Americans. Was his mother 100% American? Was his father, mother, or any of his four grand- parents born Irish? Or German? Or Austrian? Or what? Is he himself an Irish sympathizer? German sympathizer? Or sympathizer with any other foreign nation? Is he employed in or engaged in any kind of anti-British propaganda? If so, is he paid for it? By whom? How much? Interested in any other kind of propaganda? Religion? What service rendered to Ameri- ca? By his father? By any other relation? If war service, state whether through draft. Understand, I’m making no charges. Merely asking for facts. In other words, 'what right has any one to slander and libel this magazine with an untrue charge that is damaging to it and for which he can be haled into a court of law? Oh no, I’m not going to hale him into any court, but I’m tired of loose talk. Also, is there any reason why he should call me up on the carpet and not come up on it himself? Propaganda—this country is rotten with it. All kinds. I’m very little worried over these unfounded charges against the maga- zine, but—how much propaganda is being spread by those who bring the charges? Here’s the letter. Be sure I have no irri- tation against comrade Marino. He talked facts, talked straight—heard a charge of propaganda and went frankly after the truth. That’s good Americanism. I hope he has also asked his friend for detailed, concrete support of his charges. New York City. I tried to make a friend of mine interested in your magazine some time ago, and he told me that, while your stories may be very good, he never became a subscriber, because your stories for the greater part were nothing but thinly veiled British propa- ganda. He is 100% American as was his father before him, and he insists that should war break out with Great Britain your magazine would be one of the first to be suppressed. I told him I would write you about it and he said yes and suggested that I give you his address. I am not enclosing it, but if you request it you can have it.—James Marino. SOMETHING from Captain Dingle con- cerning the “makings” of his story in this issue: Pitts Bay, Bermuda. The story is fiction in the lump. Yet there was a cook, an ex-convict, pardoned for bravery, who afterward beat off a band of crooks seeking to rob the hotel he was working in. Since I heard the story from his own lips, aboard the ship Yallaroi in Mel- bourne some time around 1894-6, in which ship he *The Camp-Fire 177 was sailing for home, I made a sort of literary cock- tail out of the pungent ingredients, it is to be hoped with some measure of appeal to the taste of Adven- ture’s readers.—A. E. D. S. DURYEA, of Brooklyn, sent Edgar •	Young several copies of the Marconi- gram published at Porto Velho, Rio Ma- deira, Brazil. The copy is dated September 2, 1911, and Mr. Young tells me the Mar- conigram was published by the contractors who built the Maderia-Mamore railroad. Like Mr. Young and Mr. Duryea, I think it deserves wider publicity. Who wrote it? Was the Marconigram the first to put it into type? The Lay of the Tropical Tramp We’ve tramped ’round the earth from twenty north, To twenty or more degrees south— We’ve lived in the gilded lobster halls, And we’ve lived from hand to mouth—, We’ve struck old London when flush with coin, And roamed through New York’s streets— But we’ve always hit the tropical trail, When we find our bank rolls cease; We’ve drunk all drinks, from Pommery Sec To pulque and White Label— We have smoked dried grass and poros vile, Or perfectos fine, when able— We’ve borne mosquitoes, chigoes and fleas, Screw worms have claimed attention— Malaria, ague and “Yellow Jack,” And diseases that need no mention; But even though we parch and bake, There’s joys to balance the scale—■ A pair of dark eyes at every place, And good men along the trail; Men with stories wild and strange, Yarns that beat creation— Sweet-scented liars from every clime, Drifts from every nation; Tropical tramps, tropical bums, Tropical sour-doughs all— We hit the pike on the tropical hike, And expect to, ’til we fall; Through chapparal, mesquite, bamboo and palm, ’Til the sunset of life doth fade— We’ll tramp till we’re lost, ’neath the Southern Cross, And get put to bed with a spade. HEN Talbot Mundy gets into touch with his library again maybe we can get some more dope from him on the Ark of the Covenant and kindred matters. Mean- time he gives us some more data and tells where we can get books of reference. Lots of you have written him and I know that he has, so far as able, answered every one of you—no small piece of work and I don’t know how he gets time for it. I must begin by apologizing for the delay in an- swering the scores of fellows who have taken such 12 a surprising interest in the whereabouts of the Ark of the Covenant and the whole mystery connected with it. Even now I’m writing at a disadvantage, being three thousand miles or so distant from my library, which is in storage, so I can’t even send a friend very well to write down the titles and authors’ names of the score or so of books that I have on the subject. T'HE names of three books that I can remember are “The Coronation Stone,” “Judah’s Sceptre and Joseph’s Birthright,” “The Ten Lost Tribes.” The name of the author of the second one is Bishop Allen, and < f the third is Willis. But there are a great many more, and they are all kept in stock by my young friend A. A. Beauchamp, publisher, 603 Boylston Street, Boston, Mass., who has been young for half a century or so because he dares do what delights him. He even runs a magazine on the sub- ject. I guarantee him to be dead white, and I don’t doubt he will send a list of everything on the subject that he has for sale if requested to do so. What he knows about it compared to what I know is as in- finity to a small dog barking. However, I’ll set down as much as I remember without notes, and trust any of you who know any more about the subject to correct misstatements. HPHE English Coronation Stone in Westminster Abbey is the stone on which Jacob laid his head at Bethel, when he had that famous dream, which some thinkers say explains the whole metaphysical foundation of the universe, and others say was probably produced by dope. (Myself, like Disraeli, am on the side of the angels.) In that stone there are still the iron rings, which Moses caused to be fixed in it so that the men who carried the Ark and the other temple paraphernalia could pass rods through the rings and handle it easily. It also has a crack in it, which is asserted, not plausibly I think, to confirm the story of one of Moses’ miracles. I believe that the Ark of the Covenant is certainly in Ireland, and that it will one day be found there, at or near Mount Tara. I know the name of the man who brought it, along with Jeremiah and the Ark of the Covenant and the Princess who was Zedekiah’s oldest daughter, to Ireland by way of Egypt from Jerusalem, after Jerusalem had been destroyed. HTHESE arc dangerous days in which to say how much you believe, and why. It’s so easy to be accused of opinions exactly opposite to your real ones, and anyhow this seems hardly the right place for a metaphysical discussion. Science and super- stition seem to be almost as confused as they ever were. What you kqow, however, is what you can prove; neither more nor less. No man’s opinion or say-so is really proof of anything. So in answer- ing the question as to whether the Ark of the Cov- enant if rediscovered would kill whoever touched it I must be understood to be stating my own opinion and nothing more. I believe that it did kill any unauthorized person who touched it “in those days.” I believe it would not kill anybody now, although it could be made to kill instantly if there were any one around who knew as much as Moses did, for instance, or as much as the Israelitish priests knew, who inherited the knowledge of Moses and gradually let it dwindleI78 Adventure into superstition until it finally became mere legend. I suspect that men like Edison and Tesla (hundreds of others, too, of course) have approached attain- ment of a Similar knowledge, although they have gone about it in a different way. But I am a heretic. No orthodox person agrees with me. Turning on the light by pressing a button looks like a miracle to me. So does an oak growing out of an acorn. I have never ceased thinking since a Sikh once asked me “If the Creator is omni- present, where are you? And if He knows every- thing, how much do you know?” To my mind everything is miraculous or nothing is. I really don’t care which end of the stick you hold to hit back. I know high tension wires will kill if touched, for I’ve seen it proved, so I’m able to believe that the Ark of the Covenant would kill, if touched with- out gloves and if any one had “turned the juice on.” 'THERE is a book in two volumes called “The Great Initiates,” written by a very intelligent Frenchman and ably translated into English, which is sold by Brentano of Fifth Avenue, New York, and throws more light on ancient miracles and the men who “made” them than anything else I have found. It is rather an expensive book—six or more dollars, I think—but an awfully good money’s worth. I brought my copies West with me and lent them to a Pullman porter, who seems to have con- sidered them worth “adopting” and who is probably the high priest of a new religion now. As difficult a task as any I can think of is to keep to one phase, angle, issue or point of this prodigious subject, for it’s as big as the Bible, as broad as the universe. Let’s try another look at it: A JEW is an Israelite, but an Israelite is not neces- sarily a Jew. Abraham was neither Jew nor Israelite. The Jews are descendants of the tribe of Judah, who was Jacob’s, that is to say, Israel’s, fourth son. The English-speaking nations, together with the Scandinavian, Saxon, Bavarian and certain others are descendants of the other, so-called “lost,” ten tribes, who were carried away into captivity about 800 B. C. and settled in a country that came to be known as Scythia. Those ten tribes never did get along well v:tli the Jews or Judah-ites, whom they despised and were often at war with. The ten tribes included the tribe of Dan. The tribe of Dan had several character- istics, one of which was that they were sea-farers, another that they liked to leave their name wher- ever they went, and a third that they were rather more prone to piracy and raiding in general than even any of the other rascals were who mr.de up that amazing group of mother-nations descended from Jacob’s sons. HpHE tribe of Dan had established colonies in the Isles of the Seas long before the invasion of Canaan by the Assyrians. The Isles of the Seas were Great Britain and Ireland. They are repeat- edly referred to in Old Testament writings. The tribe of Dan’s “long ships” used to sail there regu- larly. So did the ships of the Philistines, who established rival colonies, worshiping Baal and naming such places as Ballyshannon, which is Baal- Shannon, after their horrible deity. Heremon is a Hebrew-root word. The Heremon who became king of Ireland and married Zedekiah’s daughter, thus providing for the continuance of the royal line of David in accordance with prophecy, was undoubtedly either an Israelite or descended from an Israelite who emigrated in one of those long ships. When the Assyrians carried off the ten tribes into captivity they did not find them so easy to keep as to capture. They refused to stay put. Their route across Europe was blazed, and has continued blazed, by that enterprising tribe of Dan, who left their tribe name all over the place. There are scores of instances: The rivers Don (Dan), “D(a)niester, D(a)neever, Danube—Denmark, which is Dan- mark or the country of the Danes, or Dans—-place- names such as Danebrog. They are easy to find on the map. Taken in the aggregate and studied in the light of the Old Testament, they constitute evidence which nothing much less turgid than pig-headed scholasticism can obscure. THE tribes, of course, intermingled, but certain of their characteristics have continued in certain countries, together with the most astonish- ing coincidences: such, for example,’as the fact that the arms of the United States are exactly those of the tribe of Manasseh! England is the tribe of Ephraim. The arm/of the King of England, who is descended from King David and who therefore actually is by heritage a Jew, include the Lion of the Tribe of Judah and the Harp of David. They say the word Saxon means Isaacson. Throughout the Old Testament the prophecy is made repeatedly that the “throne” shall remain in Judah—that is to say, in the keeping of the Jews. It was also prophesied that there should never cease to be a descendant of David on the throne of David. That leaves those who take the Bible literally in an awkward predicament unless they accept the explanation that the stone under the coronation chair in Westminster Abbey is the same on which David was anointed king. It is a fact that in every generation since any sort of history was written a king has been crowned on it. It also is a fact that the kings of England are anointed, although doubt- le s less oil is used nowadays than formerly. T3UT hang it, I have only touched on the subject. If you’re interested in the fulfilment of prophecy there are scores of books, including Redding’s “The Three Churches,” which is very scarce, and the same author’s “Mysteries Unveiled.” As I said, Beauchamp conducts a magazine devoted to that subject exclusively. He calls it The Watchman of Israel, and the subscription price is only a dollar a year or so. There are authorities who link up the Great Pyramid with all this and who claim to interpret the peculiar pyramid marks as corroborative of the Old Testament and the “inside” history of the English-speaking people. TO WRITE the whole story in one volume would be a task as great as Wells’ history, and at that would leave the job just plowed up ready for the next man. I	would like nothing better than to handle it as fiction and construct a story out of it. But where should a fellow begin? And how go on? And where’s the end of it? Besides, how many are there who really care enough for adventure in the realm of fact to stomach the ancient names and all the Bible references that would be essential? To me the Old Testament is a library of gorgeousThe Camp-Fire 179 entertainment. But I would hate to inflict my views on it on an Adventure audience without per- mission. And even if I had permission I rather doubt that I possess the energy and skill required to make an entertaining tale of that four-thousand-year- long and ten-nations Odyssey.—Talbot_Mundy. A letter bearing on the death-dealing powers of the Ark of the Covenant: New York City. Yesterday I met a Hebrew scholar, bom in Palestine and but one year in this country. I related the gist of Mr. Mundy’s Adventure letter to him and he informed me that he had heard the same story before leaving Palestine. He was inclined to believe it. I found the following in Rev. Robert Taylor’s “The Devil’s Pulpit”: “We are told that the magnetic needle, the mariner’s compass, was first discovered by the Venetian Marco Polo, in the year 1260. But the priests of Jupiter Ammon carried the magnet with them, in a compass box, as the Ark of the Covenant of their God, which it was death for the unsanctified to look into.”—Leon Rains. Another letter advancing the theory that history is exactly fulfilling the ancient prophecies: Omaha, Nebraska. The enclosed is based on history and prophecy and may interest Camp-Fire. Cut out any part you do not like. I notice they are filling the gaps in the Pan- American R. R. Some 45 miles southeastward from Salvador were opened for business a few days ago, which ought to carry it as far as Lake Nicaragua or thereabouts. Some 25,000 people were present at the opening. Several years ago Nicaragua wanted to build south from Managua to the Costa Rica boundary, but I don’t know what, if anything, they have done. This would carry the southern terminal to Port Limon, C. R., about 200 miles west of Colon. It is hard work to arouse our people to appreciate the necessity of completing the line to Buenos Aires and«Rio de Janeiro—east of the Andes, at once, or as soon as possible to do so. I	WAS much interested in reading Mr. Talbot Mundy’s comment concerning the “Anglo-Saxon people.” He says somewhere about 800 B. C. the Ten Tribes were carried off into captivity by Tig- lath-Pileser, king of Assyria, from whence they migrated northward and spread all over Europe. Usher’s chronology gives this date as 721 B. C. and II	Kings XVII, 6th, says “In the ninth year of Hoshea the king of Assyria took Samaria aiid carried Israel away into Assyria, and placed them in Halah and in Babor by the river of Gozan, and in the cities of the Medes.” This was in the region South of the Caspian Sea and east of the Tigris River, northeast of Babylon, and northwest of the Persian Gulf in the land of Cuth or Cuthah. They were replaced in Samaria by men from Babylon, Hanath, Sepharraim, Avah and Cuthah. These places are, in some cases, easily found on the ancient maps. The Cuthah were the descendants of Ham, through Cush, the father of Nimrod the mighty hunter, the beginning of whose kingdom was Babel (Babylon) Erech, Aecad and Calneh in the land of Shinar. At a later date they evidently migrated through southern Arabia to Africa and settled in Ethiopia (Abyssinia) along the Red Sea, on the South of the other Hamite colony of Mizraim in Egypt. In the reign of David they were known as Cushites, and “The Cush" was one of the mes- sengers to the king announcing the deatli of Absalom. The meaning of “Cush” is “black” or “blacka-moor,” hence by striking off the prefix “blacka” we may be able to identify them as the modern Moors in the land of Morocco, hence it is easy to believe in the existence of an Israelitish colony, among others, in the British isles—England, Scotland, Wales and Ireland, with travel and traffic communications with Phenicia, Egypt, Philistia and Israel by sea. AS TO the captivity of the Ten Tribes, one of the books of Esdras says they left the land of their captivity, moving through Arzareth or Ararat'— (Armenia), through the passes of the mountains into a land where they were free to worship God, where they remained in large numbers in Russia and the Caucasus. As Russia is or was known as “the land of the north” and “the land of the great prince,” both Jeremiah and Zechariah predict they are to return to the land of their fathers from “the land of the north.” Daniel 12.1 speaks of “Michael the great prince,” who announced himself as the friend of the Israelites, and predicts their release, from captivity at the end of the great tribulation now prevailing in Russia “the land of the north” and “the land of the great prince.” Under the empire these people were designated as Israelites and all of them were registered, or as Daniel 12.1 says, “At that time thy people shall be delivered (from their oppressions), every one that shall be found written in the book.” As to the return of the children of Israel, Hosea says they shall abide many days without a king and without a prince and without a sacrifice and without an image and without an aphod and without teraphim, and in the latter days shall seek the Lord their God, and David their king. And they shall be gathered together, and appoint themselves one head —for great shall be the day of Jezreel in the valley of Megiddo—modernized as Armageddon. In the sixth Chapter the prophet states when the restoration of Israel is to be, thus, “after two days will He revive us; in the third day He will raise us up, and we shall live in His sight.” Taking the Biblical day as a thousand years, this period will cover three thousand years. These years being Scriptural, with 360 days to the year, and 3,000 years from some notable event in the history of Israel, we find according to one chronologer that David became king of Israel and Judah, as one kingdom, in 1049 B. C. Deducting 1049 years from 3,000 leaves 1951 (Scriptural) years. Reduce this to our calendar of 365J4 days per year, we have— 1951x360 days=702,360 days — 36534=1922 years 11	months and some days, or, say, about Novem- ber, 1922, A. D., when Israel will begin as an or- ganized government. ANOTHER way to interpret this prophecy is to consider 2% of the 3,000 years as past from which deduct 721 B. C. the date of Hoshea’s sur- render, leaves 1945 Scriptural years, reduced asi8o Adventure follows: 1945x360 days=700,200 days -f- 36534= 1917 years—when the work of restoration began, according to the prophecy of our Savior as to “the end of the times of the Gentiles”—when on December 10, 1917, Allenby entered Jerusalem and put an end to the oppressions of the Turks. “The times of the Gentiles” were symbolized by the Seven Times Jacob bowed down before Esau (Gen. 33.3) a time consisting of the prophetic year of 360 years—• making 360x7=2520 years these times were to continue with Israel subject to the Gentiles. They began about 603 B. C. (our time) or about 610 B. C. Scriptural time, when the king of Egypt dethroned the king of Judah and carried him captive to Egypt and Judah lost its independence, making the term of “the times of the Gentiles” thus 2520— 603=1917, A. D. This is verified by the 1335 days (Dan. 12) which are 1335 Mohammedan years. Reduced to our time, the period is 1295 years, a month and 24 days. The Mohammedan year 1335 expired October 16, 1917, leaving an overplus of 14 days in November and 10 days in December to complete the period of “1335 days.” The Moham- medan era began in 622 A. D., to which add 1295 years, one month and 24 days and the resulting date is December, 10, 1917, A. D. These three prophecies are especially important, as to the restor- ation of Israel. J N “BEN HUR” Lew Wallace refers to the Ark of the Covenant as being shut up in a cave in Mount Nebo and the entrance closed so none could find it. In one of the books of the Maccabees (apocryphal) the story is told at length, with Jerenuah informed that the Ark is to remain in its hiding-place until Israel is restored. I am inclined to believe Mr. Mundy is in error when he speaks of the extinction of the male line of David by the murder of the sons of Zedekiah. David had a num- ber of sons, other than Solomon, among whom was Nathan, the next oldest brother to Solomon. Nathan was not of the reigning line. But his descendants were of more than passing importance when Zechariah says (12, 11, 12) “the family of the house of David shall be set apart and the family of the house of Nathan shall be set apart.” As we turn to the genealogy of our Savior, as given by Luke, we find He was of the house of Nathan, the son of David and jointly through Zorobabel the Son of Salathiel, of the line of Solomon, as written by Matthew. AS FOR the rulers of Great Britain descending through the marriage of the daughter of Zede- kiah to King Heremon of Ireland, through the influence of Jeremiah, tradition says Jeremiah died in Ireland. But history says he was carried to Egypt by the Jews, after the destruction of Jerusalem, where he was put to death. As to the British being the People of the Covenant, there is good ground for such a belief in the parable of the wicked hus- bandmen, when our Savior, as he was teaching in the temple, said to the Pharisees, the chief priests and the elders of the people, “The Kingdom of God shall be taken from you and given to a nation bring- ing forth the fruits thereof.” HPHE mission of Israel was to lead the world. The kingdom was given to them to place them above all the nations of the earth. They rejected it. Ulti- mately it was given to the British Empire, whose long career in spreading Christianity and civilization justifies this belief. It shared this honor with the United States when our great President uttered the sublime precept: “the world must be made safe for democracy” which finally made three empires bite the dust and pass into oblivion as accursed, while the devils of imperialism, Caesarism and fanaticism were driven from the earth by the triumphant hosts of republican 'liberty. Israel in its blindness rushed to Calvary and was ruined. But, in “the regeneration” (Math. 19.28) so near at hand, this blindness will be removed, and as a nation it will rise again with a king or prince of the house of David governing his people while the Son of man sitting in the throne of his glory at Jerusalem shall rule the world in liberty, peace and justice. “For out of Zion shall come forth the laws and the word of the Lord from Jerusalem!”—E. F. Test. WHO knows the number on the gun Eilly the Kid carried when he was captured? Wilson, Oklahoma. I have a few pieces of kindling for the old, but good Camp-Fire. About myself, I am from Louisi- ana, and the shores of the broad Mississippi River. The only real adventure I’ve had was in the jungle of Panama, the Chepo River in and around Agua Dulce, R. de P. The greatest adventure I ever had was drowning in the Panama Canal and being pulled out by a Mr. Spillman of Cheyenne, Wyoming. My next trip is Russia or China if my plans work right. The main idea of this butting into Camp-Fire is to find out of any of its members can tell me Billy the Kid’s pistol number. I saw a .45 Colt single action, model 1871 or 1872; the owner claimed that this gun was owned by Billy The Kid when cap- tured.---. *	I *HE rope trick. A word from one of our writers’ brigade on this interesting stunt: San Francisco. I’ve justfinished the June 10th Adventure, which contains Mr. W. E. Besson’s inquiry regardingethe famous “rope trick” of the Indian jugglers. If a bit of dope drawn from memory and not capable of sub- stantiation at this time is admissable, I’ll unburden my chest of the account of my own encounter with this yarn: COME ten years ago I got hold of a book of travel —one of Mayne Reid’s, I think—in which was a description of the trick as follows: A fakir and a boy appeared in the streets of Cal- cutta (?) carrying a large covered basket. When a crowd had gathered, the fakir produced from the basket a rope and a big knife. The rope he coiled and heaved upward, and one end went out of sight and stayed, while he held the “bitter end” in his hand. The boy, at a signal from his master, climbed the rope hand-over-hand and disappeared. A few mo- ments later the fakir called for the boy to come down, but received no answer. He called several times and at last, apparently irritated by the boy’s silence, placed the knife between his teeth and him- self ascended the rope; disappeared. From aboveThe Camp-Fire	181 came a blood-curdling yell, then in rapid sequence the severed arms, legs, head of the boy hurtled to the ground, followed finally by the hacked trunk. The fakir descended rapidly, his knife dripping blood. He gathered together the pieces of the body and placed them in the basket, covering it again. After a mumbled incantation he opened the basket and out stepped the boy, unhurtl "HAT’S the account I read—and wondered over. But a little later—in the same year—I came upon a description of the trick in some magazine. It purported to be an expose of the trick. An Englishman, who had heard of the feat, who was a dabbler in mesmerism and hypnotism, waited for a fakir to appear. One came and the theater was a street lined with blank walls—so that none but the immediate assembly, which was to a man under the fakir’s, influence, might see(?)—and the Englishman, with a camera, found a hole in the wall behind the fakir. He took picture after picture and, when the fakir had finished and gone, questioned as many of the spectators as possible. Without ex- ception these described the performance much as it is described above.. But the pictures, covering every moment of the performance, revealed only the crowd gaping fixedly upward, while the fakir and his boy-assistant stood quietly by the basket on the ground. Mesmerism of the spectators en masse was the explanation advanced by the Englishman. He was not susceptible to mesmeric influence, he stated, and so was the only person to witness the performance who “saw just what he saw” and not what the fakir made him believe he saw. T WISH I had the names of the book and maga- zine from which the above are taken. But there was no reason at time of reading to think of this point and I made no notes. But I’d be willing to take oath that I’ve given here the essence of the two accounts; they made a marked impression upon me and my memory, I’m told, is “inconveniently long.” Whether or not the expose of the trick was au- thentic, I would like to learn. Perhaps both ac- counts are mere travelers’ tales on a par with Herod- otus’ account of the crocodiles’ tears. sQuien sabe? At any rate, I know that some where in the books is mention of this particular trick and what I saw surely others of Camp-Fire saw also. Some of the experts on mesmerism might also be able to say whether or not such group-mesmerism is possible.— Eugene Cunningham. YOU’LL remember I asked you for opinions as to the “blurbs” following titles on our contents pages and in “Trail Ahead” when we began cutting out even more of the “ballyhoo” than formerly. Quite a number of you responded and we went over the “vote” carefully. The majority favored having the “blurbs” re- stricted, as now, to a brief suggestion of the general nature of the story, but. there were many opinions, all the way from the majority opinion to a frank liking for para- graphs of “ballyhoo’’ as to what good and interesting stories they are. The majority, too, were for a bit of “ballyhoo” in “Trail Ahead,” through against it on the contents pages. All in all, we believe we’ve now got the form for both places that most of you prefer. Any time you want it otherwise a majority of opinion will get what you want. OUR Camp-Fire stations are spreading steadily over the map, but plenty of room exists for more. Help make them grow. A STATION may be in any shop, horrte or other reputable place. Tne only require- ments are that a Station shall display the regular Station sign, provide a box or drawer for mail to be called for and preserve the register book. No responsibility for mai! is assumed by anybody; the Station merely uses ordinary care. Entries in register to be confined to name or serial number, route, destination, permanent address and such other brief notes or remarks as desired; each Station can impose its own limit on space to be used. Registers become permanent property of Station; signs remain property of this magazine, so that if there is due cause of complaint from members a Station can be discontinued by withdrawing sign. A Station bulletin board is strongly to be recommended as almost necessary. On it travelers can leave tips as to conditions of trails, etc., resident members can post their names and addresses, such hospitality as they care to offer, calls for any travelers who are familiar with countries these residents once knew, calls for particular men if they happen that way, etc., notices or tips about lecal facilities and con- ditions. Letters to resident members can be posted on this bulietin-board. Any one who wishes is a member of Camp-Fire and therefore entitled to the above Station privileges subject to the Keeper’s discretion. Those offering hospitality of any kind do so on their own responsibility and at their own risk and can therefore make any discriminations they see fit. Traveling members will naturally be expected to remember that they are merely guests and act accordingly. Keepers answer letters only if they wish. For local information write "Ask Adventure.” _ A Station may offer only the required register and mail facilities or enlarge its scope to any degree it pleases. Its possibilities as headquarters for a local club or resident Camp-Fire members are excellent. The only connection between a Station and this magazine is that stated above, and a Keeper is in no other way re- sponsible to this magazine nor representative of it. Alabama—50—Fairfield. Percy E. Our experts will in all cases answer to the best of their ability, using their own discretion in all matters pertaining to their sections, subject only to our general rules for “Ask Adventure,” but neither they nor the magazine assumes any responsibility beyond the moral one of trying to do the best that is possible. These experts have been chosen by us not only for their knowledge and experience but with an eye to their integrity and reliability. We have emphatically assured each of them that his advice or information is not to be affected in any way by whether a given commodity is or is not advertised in this magazine. 1.
