In Three Parts Old Songs That Men Have Sung Fishing in North America Mountains and Mountaineering Standing Information Two-Bladed Knives A WICKED addition to anybody’s arma- ment, looks like: Question:—“I note in ‘Ask Adventure’ that you kindly offer to give information in regard to antique edged weapons, and I am presuming to impose upon your good nature. I have a double-bladed weapon which was willed to me by a friend. I did not know that he owned it, and I have no idea where he got it. It is 21 inches long. The blades are 1 inch wide at the broadest place. Both blades are thin, and sharp on both edges. Both sides of both blades are covered with in- scriptions, which are raised above the surface. Evidently from the appearance of the metal the background was etched away. 183184 Adventure The surface of the steel was rusted when the weapon came to me, and from its appearance I do not think it was ever highly polished. The handle, between the two blades, is of a green- ish-white bone, the like of which I have never seen before. It is carefully finished, but not highly polished. On each side of the handle is engraved a row of circles, with a dot in the center, about one- fourth of an inch in diameter. The sheaths are evidently of rawhide of some sort, covered with what looks like cheetah skin— tawny yellow, fur with small black spots. This fur, though evidently very old and much worn, has never been attacked by moths. The blades look very old, and are of primitive workmanship, though the lette~-ng is bold and free, and drawn by no uncertain hand. If you will examine the black-lead tracing which I am enclosing you will see that the inscription is the repetition of one word, over and over again. I do not know the language, though it looks something like Turkish, or possibly Jagatai, or even Persian. The surfacing of the blades is much like that of a Turkish boarding-cutlas now in my possession, which was presented to my father by Gen. Grant. I would much like tojknow your opinion on the nationality of this weapon, and the probable limiting dates of its manufacture. Also the kind of fighting it was used in, and against what sort of weapon it was used in defense. Ancient weapons interest me much if I know something of their history and method of use in offense and defense. Collecting them is a fine avoca- tion in which, I understand, you are an adept. I have a straight double-edged sword, on one side of which is engraved in fifteenth-century Spanish, “Draw me not without cause,” and on the other side, “Sheath me not without honor.” Do you know if the engraving of mottoes was common on Spanish arms of that period? Have you ever seen the above mottoes on a sword-blade, and if so, of what country and period? Thank you much for any information you can give on the above matters. Drop in and see me whenever you have nothing better to do. My office is near South Station. Stamped, self-addressed envelop enclosed.”— Allan V. Garratt, Boston, Mass. Answer, by Mr. Barker:—As to the double- bladed weapon you speak of, I think it is in all probability Persian, and its date may be any time, probably prior to 1800. As to what kind of fighting it was used in, that is impossible' for me to say with any certainty. Demmin does not mention or give an’illustration of such a weapon, nor do the illustrations of the Meyrick Collection contain one. One was sold at auction in New York in 1914 for $5.75, a sketch of which I enclose. (No. 1.) It was there described as a “TWO-BLADED REVERSE CURVED DAGGER, with bone handle in center, one side engraved with Hebrew characters, the other with Arabian, blades curved outward in opposite directions, each 9 inches long, triangular in shape, scabbards of leather. Length 23 inches.” Blades and scabbards seem to differ from yours. The engraving of mottoes was common on Span- ish arms. I have seen a Spanish officer’s saber, the blade being Solingen, made by Schimelbush & Foest, on which is engraved in Spanish— “Draw me not without a Reason, nor Sheath me with Dishonor.” I am enclosing a sketch (No. 2) of a short, heavy Spanish sword, bearing date of 1818, on the double- edged blade of which is, in Spanish— “Draw me not without reason, nor sheath me with dishonor.” SEE TEXT FOR DESCRIPTIONS Also a sketch (No. 3) of a very ancient Spanish sword with a very curious hilt, made of solid silver, with places to fit the fingers. It has a very broad blade, grooved on both sides, on one side of which is, in Spanish— “Draw me not without reason, nor sheath me with dishonor.” I shall certainly do myself the pleasure of dropping in and seeing you, and arrange to have you come out to my house and see my few pieces. Names and addresses of depart- ment editors and the exact field cov- ered by each section are given in alternate issues of the magazine. Do not •write to the magazine itself. Just Floatin’ WISH I could slough this magazine job for six months and go along: Question:—“I want information on the following points. ,It is understood that the following questions apply to boats without propelling force other than oars or sweeps. Is the middle of September too late to leave Pittsburgh and float down to New Orleans? If so, would it be advisable to start from St. Louis? What is the approximate time to float from Pitts- burgh to Cairo, floating days only? Same from St. Louis to New Orleans? Is it safe to float nights, with lights but with no watch? Will “current boards” keep a floating boat in the channel?”—Edward French, M. D., Boston, Mass. Answer, by Mr. Spears:—You could leave Pitts- burgh in September and make the Gulf by tripping the Ohio, but I’d have my doubts about the Ohio River without a little power—an outboard motor, say. There are long pools above the Ohio River dams, and you’d have to lock through about forty of the dams, which enable steamers to run in low- water periods. St. Louis would be better, for thenAsk Adventure 185 you have the river current all the way down, four to seven miles an hour. A day’s float would be about forty miles. You would be able to drop down about four days a week, on the average, working down comfortably. Say New Orleans in about seven or eight weeks. You could jam through in less time. I took all Winter— seven months—to make this trip down, but I just sauntered along, went over to St. Francis River for 250 miles, and down Chaffelli. I shouldn’t advise night floating without a watch. It is done by old river-men on a falling stage of the river, which keeps the boat in mid-stream, a rising stage throwing the boat to the bank. You might meet a fleet of tow barges and be rolled under them, which happens. I floated nights when the day- winds prevailed, but kept watch. It’s an eery experience! Never heard of any one using current boards, or sea-anchors, except that in winds, shanty-boaters in a hurry run into the drift and make fast, and are towed down. This is dangerous if the driftwood is swirling and the boat is caught in a squeeze. I’m enclosing a slip that’ll help you a little. Come again, if I’m needed. If you don’t want an answer enough to enclose full return postage to carry it, you don’t -want it. Stock-Raising in Australia piNE opportunities there still: Question:—“Having seen your address in “Ask Adventure” columns of Adventure magazine, as one who answers queries regarding Australia and its resources, I am taking the privilege of writing you. I am interested in the stock industry in Australia. Have had ten years’ experience in ranch life in North Dakota and Montana, U. S. A. I would like to get in touch with some of the leading stockmen of your country. Can furnish reference of any kind regarding character and ability.”—John W. Pat- terson, Fort Mills, Philippine Islands. Answer, by Mr. Goldie:—Stock-raising land in Australia is usually divided into first, second, and third class grades. As in all other new countries, the richer soil was the first to be occupied and worked to advantage. In many districts it proved that only the first class would pay under the then existing cir- cumstances; but as conditions impuved, second- class country became as desirable as the first-class had’originally been. Then the third-class came into occupation, and in turn proved profitable because it was secured at low values and had been brought within easy communication with markets. In the most closely settled States, such as Victoria and Tasmania, the greater extent of the land is held under freehold, and the newcomer who desires to secure property will find it best to buy an improved place, or, better still, to purchase some good grazing- land which is still subject'to considerable improve- ment. If he has moderate means, the buyer can make a fair profit on his capital, while he continues to increase the value of his holding. Well-fenced and watered sheep or cattle country, provided with a homestead, may be bought in these States at from 4 to 8 pounds per acre according to its carrying capacity; while properties in first-class order and capable of grazing two sheep to the acre cost considerably more. Usually these are compara- tively small, and. a good deal of cultivation is carried on by the holders. It is on such places as these, however, that a skilful stock-breeder may make money and accumulate property. Like all good things, the best of the grazing- estates in Tasmania and Victoria must be paid for, but once established and with the right class 6f stock in the paddocks, the enterprising breeder from the old country soon makes good on his purchase. What can be accomplished on small sheep- holdings has been demonstrated by the merino sheep-breeders of midland Tasmania. Here many of the old famihes have carried on flocks of the finest merino for many years. They raise thousands of stud sheep and send them to the mainland for sale. The Tasmanian sheep were introduced into hun- dreds of the largest flocks in the other States, and sheep-breeding in Australia owes much to the group of accomplished breeders in the little island. Flocks of one thousand to two thousand sheep, kept up to a high standard, have been sufficient to maintain their owners in the position of small pastoral kings. In other parts of the island, splendid herds of cattle have been reared and maintained on small holdings, and have kept prosperous families on the land. In Victoria there is still some leased country, and there are thousands of acres of grazing-land that offer opportunities to the buyer who has pastoral instincts, and prefers stock-keeping to the more arduous agricultural operations. North from Victoria, on the Murray River, into the southern pastoral districts of New South Wales, a great extent of sheep country stretches away west, and east into the mountains, which stand back from the coast. Throughout this wonderful stretch of pasturage there are great productive grazing- properties which carry from one sheep to an acre to three sheep to two acres. The newcomer, in his search for land, will find large estates varying in value from 4 to 7 pounds per acre. Provided that he is in a position to finance the purchase of land and stock, he can not fail with good management to make any of these properties pay very handsomely. There are also chances of secur- ing land on lease from the Government, and others of acquiring a partly improved portion of a large run which is being cut up by the owners or subdi- vided after resumption by the State. The stock-breeder from older countries finds con- ditions in Australia almost completely the reverse of those to which he has been accustomed. All animals live in the open air in this wonderful climate the year round. With the exception of certain stock, such as special studs, working horses, and perhaps the “house” cows, all the animals feed solely on the sweet pastures of the country. The newcomer wonders at what he considers to be the insufficient steading about the country homes in Australia. It is soon explained to him that no build- ings are needed for housing animals in the Winter, and this mainly accounts for the difference in the size of a live-stock establishment in Australia and one in a cold country. As a matter of course, the working conditions also differ to a considerable extent. Much less labor is required to work the same number of cattle or sheep, and most of the work is in the open paddocks. A direct consequence of this is that one man cani86 Adventure attend'to a large number of animals. There are yearly operations, such as lamb-marking and shear- ing, and cattle-mustering and branding, while in between, the animals are worked from paddock to paddock according to the condition of the pas- tures. “Fats” come on much more irregularly than in a country where hand feeding is universal, but stock fatten very quickly on the rapid flushes of grass and herbage which come in good seasons. The animals make amazing progress from birth to maturity, unless they get a very severe check. The system is thus almost as simple as that on the cattle ranches of Texas and the Argentine. Most stock-owners in Australia stock lightly be- cause of the variations in the seasons. As a rule, when the pastures are very good, the broad acres could carry many more animals than are grazed upon them, but the long-headed stockmen are satisfied to keep them in good condition, and con- serve the grass for recurring periods of dry weather. On his tours of inspection, which are usually necessary before he can settle down, the land-seeker acquires a great deal of knowledge of the country which is essential to his career as a pastoralist. He soon learns to distinguish sheep from cattle country, and picks up many wrinkles on the management of all classes of stock in Australia. Information on the value of water conservation is impressed upon him. Except in Western Australia, where, on extensive pastoral areas, scrub and small timber are necessary to augment the carrying capacity of the country, he sees the advantage gained by killing the exhausting green timber and cleaning up the surface of the land as the dry timber falls. The experienced Australian pastoralist will take him in hand on train and motorcar journeys, and freely impart to him the tips he has gained in years of pioneering in the country. Having at last made his selection and secured his property, which may be 2,000 or 10,000 acres in extent according to his means, his ambitions, and the class of country he prefers, his first year will be almost solely devoted to making decisions as to the best means of working his place and of following such operations as he can compass. If he has purchased an improved property he will learn how to use the paddocks, water supply and other improvements on the best scheme for keeping his stock in condition. As a general rule, he will discover that to make the best of his holding he needs to complete the'improve- ments. Starting on an improved place; however, the new owner has a great advantage in being able to work his stock to immediate profit. He can devote most of his time and his resources to the actual management of his sheep or cattle, and the place begins to produce for him from the jump. On the other hand, if he begins on partially im- proved property, the principal improvement is likely to be the killing of most of the timber and the erec- tion of part of a ring fence. He may secure a tank or a well or two; but on the other hand the place may at first be-waterless. So the new settler must hustle into fencing, tank or well sinking, and the erection of a house and some steading. The princi- pal advantage he has in this country over the man who takes up virgin acres is that the native grass and herbage are well established because the timber has been killed in part, and these respond to a mod- erate rainfall with a lively growth of healthy feed. Therefore the place can be stocked up immedi- ately, and a’lambing and a clip of wool may be expected in the first year of occupation. The operations in making a home, in subdividing the holding, securing a water supply, breaking up a paddock with a plow, and raising wheat or oats, or hay and grain for his horses, establishing a small herd of milkers for the family supply of milk and butter, will be simple enough to an experienced stockman coming from the old country. As the most urgent jobs are completed, a vegetable garden and a small orchard are added to the supplying areas. When the new man in Australia reaches this stage, he is beginning to settle down to the comfort- able life of a landed proprietor. Among the leading stock agents of this country are: Dalgety & Co., Ltd., Sydney; the Cooperative Wool and Produce Co., Ltd., Sydney; the New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency Co., Ltd., with head offices in New Zealand and branches all over Australia; Chisholm & Co., Sydney; Cramsey & Lithbridge, Ltd., Sydney; Hill, Clark & Co., Sydney; and Winchcombe Carson, Ltd., Sydney. There are many others, but if you apply to any of these firms you will no doubt get all the information you require regarding your chances in this industry in Australia. When you get something for nothing, don’t make the other fellow pay the postage on it. A Hotel Built on a Gold-Mine ALSO some tips about bear-huhting and placer mining: Question:—“A couple of friends recently asked me to write you for some information on hunting and placer mining in western Montana. I spent a few weeks in the old Virginia Creek diggings, but know nothing of any place that seemed to suit them. What they want is to find out best time for bear and also a good bear country—they figure on using'a car to within fifty miles of hunting- ground if possible and pack in from car. Can you give any information as to such a place and de- scription of country surrounding? As to the place: They want to know if you know of any place in western Montana where the country hasn’t been worked over too much. Enough so they could work without feeling that older hands had been in and got disgusted and quit. Any informa- tion you can give on either subject will be appreci- ated. If this goes to Adventure don’t publish name or address. P. S.—Both of these men are able to rough it to the limit.” Answer, by Mr. Davis:—It’s a pretty hard propo- sition to combine a bear-hunt with a placer-mining proposition. I am afraid the time is past when the combination that will be productive of results is to be found in the State. The region between Flathead Lake and the Continental Divide is reported to be a first-class bear country. A good way to reach it is to go to Ovando in Powell County, outfit there and hit the trail northeasterly fifty or sixty-five miles. Bear are now coming out of Winter quarters.* •This was written April 14, 1922.Ask Adventure 187 How do you contemplate hunting them? Some trappers use bait and then trap them, others use dogs to find, them and then take a chance with a rifle. If you are going to use neither dogs nor bait the chance of bringing home the bear is consid- erably reduced. The bear may be around, but you are just as apt not to run on to them. If you want the names of reliable guides write C.	A. Jakways, State Fish and Game Warden, Helena, Montana. Horses can be rented at Ovando. I don’t know of any place in western Montana where the't placer ground has not been worked over. I do not believe you will find any such place in the U. S. You write, “Enough so they could work without feeling that older hands had been in and got disgusted and quit.” The mere fact that older hands have worked placer ground does not necessarily indicate that all the gold has been taken. Eight years ago a new hotel was constructed in Helena on Main Street which occupies the site of Last Chance Gulch, where the placer miners worked in the sixties and extracted something like $30,000,- 000. The contractor who excavated for the founda- tion hauled the ground away, washed it in sluice- boxes and made a tidy sum of money. It happened that the old-time placer miners didn’t work the ground thoroughly. There are large areas of so called placer ground in various parts of western Montana which contain gold and which according to report might be profitable if dredged on a large scale. Years ago there was placer mining on Nevada Creek, Powell County, a few miles south of Ovando. Within recent years there have been some dredge and hydraulic placer mining done on Cedar Creek, south of Superior, which is in Sanders' County. There is also some placer mining at the head of the St. Joe River in Idaho across the range from Super- ior. There is conisderable placer mining ground near Libby, Lincoln County. The most recent placer proposition of which the writer has heard is in Park County near the Yellowstone Park. More about the Napo Country TT’S on the eastern slope of the Andes in Ecuador. One thing Mr. Young omitted to mention in his answer, by the way, is that this territory, or a good big chunk of it, is in dispute. Ecuador has it now; but suppose it is awarded to Colombia or Peru; then will the settlers’ titles be any good? Question:—“I read with interest a letter of yours in a recent issue of Adventure, which fell into my hands in Moji, Japan. In fact my interest was so great that I wrote at once to the Mr. Felton you speak of at Napo, Oriente, Ecuador, requesting in- formation. I at present hold a master’s certificate, tonnage and oceans unlimited, and stand a small chance of getting command in the near future. But the sea game has been getting on my nerves for the past few years, and I have looked for a chance to settle down and try to make a stake. If the pros- spects are as good in Ecuador as they appear I should like to go. I have sufficient funds to take me south and to provide a working capital upon arrival. I have a fair knowledge of the Spanish language “as it is spoken,” picked up on the coast of Chile. I seem to be understood. I have some knowledge of farming, a relic of my youth before the lure of the bounding main, etc., snared me. Now my question is: What are the prospects as you see them? If I did go what would I be able to buy South? What should I carry along? To tell you the truth what I want is a complete little book of information if you can write it. Before I close I would say I am writing for inform- ation. I do not know if I shall leave the sea; I may but I am not certain; perhaps if things look good enough—who knows? If this letter should be published I would request that my name and address be withheld.” Answer, by Mr. Young:—I have read your letter with interest. Quife a number of folks were inter- ested in that Ecuador country, and I have received letters from all parts of the U. S. and Canada from men who wish to try it a whirl. Capt. Helms (former President of the Ajax Tire Co.) has already gone, and others have either gone or will go. It looks as if that counrty will soon be opened up unless something happens. The reason the chances have been so good is the fact that there was a small empire over there which was given over to primitive nature and a few red men and a couple of white men. These Indians are as tame as rabbits, although several hundred miles due south there are some bad Indians (Jiveros) who are the other extreme. There is some mighty fine country over there, and after a man once gets across the mountains and down them a couple of days he can sit and float for a matter of six hundred miles in a canoe at the rate of a hundred miles per day. What stuff that is taken out of the country usually goes in this manner, and the Indians pole and paddle back up in about two weeks. I have heard that there is a launch line or two running up as far as Napo from Iquitos now. With one of these light-draft boats that has the propeller housed up inside of the boat and draws the water up by a vacuum created by a stand-pipe from which the air has been pumped, a man could go right on up above Napo with a good-sized boat. I think the name of the company making these boats is the Shallow Draft Boat Corpn. You can get their name from the Board of Trade of the U. S., at Washington, D.	C. They make a canal-boat that only draws eight inches of water, and at the Motor Show they had a small launch running on a half-inch of water. Two of their boats have been shipped to Brazil for use on shallow rivers. The Napo and Cururay are big rivers after you get down them for a day or two, but are not very large above Napo and a similar height on the Cururay. I don’t know what effect it will have on the Indian labor if a number of Americans should go down there and make the demand more than the supply. These Indians come in from the forest and take up with white men and hang around for their board and a tot of rum once a day. Darned queer place over there, and darned queer people. Hu- mility is a part of their custom, and they ask to be whipped once a year with a switch for some wrong they have committed. Civilization may spoil them as it does every one else. The average planter over there feels as if he isn’t188 Adventure doing much unless he has a few score of them around him. For my part I would be satisfied with a less number and a few modern machines and a hydro- electric plant in one of the streams. There isn’t much to tell about the country. You go to Guayaquil and then to Quito over the G. & Q. R. R. and ride a mule one day to Papallacta. From there she is shanks’ mare stuff for about fifty-six miles; but she is a-winder of a trail and will take you four or five days to make it. The Indians travel slow and pack about a hundred pounds all the way for $2.50 and board themselves. Their women follow them for the first day and carry their coca (grub) for them. After you get to the top of the mountain at Bajeza she’s down-hill in a gradual slope for six hundred miles, and where the Napo enters the Amazon it is only about nine hundred feet above sea-level. She’s barren at the top, and going down vegetation gets thicker and thicker until she is typical tropical jungle plus in the lowlands. They don’t have any Winter, only rain, and I’m here to tell the world that I prefer a tropical rainy season to one of these New York Winters with the temperature changing every day. The worst thing about trying to go over there during the rainy sea- son is that the trail is bad and the Indians don’t like to pack over it; but they do pack the mail at all seasons. Near Napo there is an American; and down the river there are a Frenchman and a few white Span- ish settlers. Up to the present they have had this vast country to themselves. ‘The G. & Q. R. R. is figuring onjjbuilding a railroad over there from Riobamba, and if Capt. Helms has his way he will put a trolley line across the mount- ains for them. I had lunch with him before he left, and he sure felt the call of the primitive. He had on a diamond ring with a stone in it the size of a cherry; but he was longing for overalls and a month’s growth of beard on his face. If you do go down there and run across him tell him “how” for me. Fishing: Stocked Ponds THE more you preserve fish in order to angle for ’em, the wiser they get. Brother Thompson has doped out a way to fool Old Dame Instinct, but my guess is that the dodge won’t work more than a few years in any one pond: Question:—“Would like to have your advice on the best method and kind of baits to use for catching old stock of bass in small lake or pond. These ponds were stocked say twelve or fifteen years ago with bass. For a few seasons they were very easy to catch; but for the last four or five years they have been refusing to strike at either live bait or artificial bait of almost every variety. These fish can be seen late of an afternoon close in to the shore but can not be persuaded to strike at any lure.”--, Walton, Ky. Answer, by Mr. Thompson:—I have had the same experience as you with stocked ponds, but finally got bass striking. As fish grow up in a pond I am sure they feel the vibration from a man’s move- ments on shore; also the small fish that feed in shoal water scare quickly at the presence of man and somehow or other transmit this to the other fish by their actions of getting into deep water. I have studied this subject a great deal, and finally began wading. I got to where I wanted to cast, and after getting in the water never made any movement whatever for a long time. I am sure fish do not see you when you are waist deep in water as they do when you are on shore. I used the light- est artificial lures with the grooved heads that I could get. They went deep on fast reeling and brought out many a big fellow. When using a fly rod often I had good results from a bucktail and an 0 spinner, the smallest size. I used many times with success the little fiy-rod wooden plugs, and one of the best methods is to attach a dropper fly as usual and carry along the little plug as trailer. This looks like a minnow chasing a fly, and very few bass can resist it. Many ponds like yours are fine for late evening and night fishing. The best killers are bass bugs with fly rod, and any surface minnow. With it reel slowly, and you will have a fine thrill when they strike. “Ask Adventure” service costs you nothing whatever but reply postage and self-addressed envelop. Exception: Our assaying and oil services. Fancy and Fact in Polynesia THEY’RE far apart when a man is with- out capital, especially when he’s get- ting along in years: Question:—“I am a workingman and filled up and a little tired (I’m 50 years old) of this eternal work, work, and slavery, that seems to be the result of civilization. I want to begin a new life, live every- thing and go where a man may get a living and does not have to worry for tomorrow all the time. South Sea Islands are the subjects of these questions. Do you believe that there is a chance for a man that worked from South America to Alaska for his living, to make it there as a native? Can a man get land there enough to live on? I do not want a plantation; it would mean grinding again. What group of islands would you advise me to go to to take a look for that home? Where can I get maps and literature on the South Sea Islands? How much capital would you think is needed to get a start to live as a native, not as a capitalist?”— Gus Hamil, Ketchikan, Alaska. Answer, by Mr. Charles Brown, Jr.:—You are a workingman, you say. You are fed up with the year-long grind and anxiety of modem civilization. Your only escape lies in spreading sail for the warm, blue waters of Polynesia, you believe. You see an island valley with a little grass house hidden away in the green depths of the jungle. All about it are fruits and vegetables and flowers. And at the foot of the valley is a lagoon, sparkling through long hours of sun. Always it will yield you the choicest sea-foods. Never, never will it fail. Far up in the cool of the valley run wild chickens and hogs and goats and cattle—yours, all yours, for the killing! You have but to call the happy, laughing islanders, _and they lead you to the hunt. At duskAsk Adventure v 189 you return heavily laden, to partake of the kill. Then comes the moonlight, orange-colored and all-pervading. And with it the laughter and the singing of pretty, brown island girls, so that you for- get almost entirely the loneliness and desolation of the bleak land in which you were a prisoner of cir- cumstances. Such is the picture your unhappy mind has painted. But, oh, my fellow creature, out on the beaches of Polynesia life can be as cruel as a prohi- bition officer, especially to the man who, in the twilight of his years, wants to turn native. Now mark. When a happy, reckless South Sea vagabond, holding my own on breadfruit and fish and sleeping out on the decks of becalmed island schooners, I met up with all manner of beach-comb- ers, men that had tired of civilization in much the same manner as you have done. This legion is knocking about with but one regret. Ever they curse the hour they turned their backs on the- home of their fathers for an uncertain struggle under the Southern Cross. And always they whispered to me their one hope, spoke of the day when they would drift back to the lands so far above the line. Oh, my friend, the stories I could write of these “breth- ren of the beach!”' I do not want you to become one of that legion. Nor do I want to think of your plunging lower than a native. You must understand that to a native a beach-comber or white man that has gone native is less than the coral over which the island dogs run. Can you get enough land to live on? Yes, if you have money—several hundred dollars. For in those island valleys cash has now become a medium of exchange, even amongst the “hospitable” natives. “How much capital would you think is needed to get a start to live as a native, not as a capitalist?” Why, when a man “goes native,” he does so because his purse is nothing more than a rag. If he had “capital,” he would never turn to that life. Only when his resources fail, when he can not again get on his two feet, does he accept from the natives what- ever they see fit to throw to him. Indeed, I have stripped the South Sea of its glamour. I’m cruel, am I not? The enclosed form, “Cash Needed by All Guests on Tahiti Island,” tells more about the thing I am driving at. Frederick O’Brien has written two very readable books on French Oceania. “White Shadows in the South Seas” and “Mystic Isles of the South Seas” are published by the Century Company, New York City. I wish from all my heart I could mail you a differ- ent sort of a reply. But, you can readily under- stand, one reason I conduct this section is to prevent men, especially men who have crossed the shadow fine, from becoming one of that homeless legion. Address your question direct to the ex- pert in charge, NOT to the magazine. Deux Rivieres JT’S hunting-country: Question:—“I beg to ask information as to the country and kind of game found in the locality of De Riviere. Also give me your opinion of a .35 Remington rifle, pump or automatic, for moose and red deer. Can this be beaten for above game?”—Guy U. Blackall, Blyth, Ont., Can. Answer, by Mr. Moore:—Deux Rivi&res (Two Rivers), eighteen miles or so east by south of Mat- towa, on the main line of the C. P. R. and north of the C. N. R., is a very good locality for red deer and moose, although from its close proximity to Pem- broke and countryside it is bound to be pretty well hunted. However, as this section lies north of Algonquin Park, and the park serves as a feedei of game for all the country that surrounds it, the Deux Rivieres part will always have a fair share of game. A better moose country is across the Ottawa from Deux Rivieres and north to Lake Kippewa. The country is rough, rocky, hilly, with many Little swamps, lakes, etc. There is considerable timber in there, and lumbering has been going for many years. Other game such as fox, wolves, bear, etc., are there but rapidly decreasing in numbers. As regards the .35 caliber Remington rifle for deer and moose: I have never used a .35, but I notice the editor of “Guns and Ammunition” in Rod and Gun in Canada has this to say about a good moose rifle: “I would suggest the .303 British, the .30- 1906 Winchester or Remington, the .35 Remington Automatic, or the .33 model 1886 Winchester for moose.” In the same magazine Adam Moore, a New Brunswick guide of twenty-five years’ stand- ing, has a word in praise of the .35 caliber for “the big fellows.” Whether the .35 can be beaten or not, is not for me to say. I know men who have shot and killed moose with the .22 hi-power, the .303 Ross sporting, and they will swear by their favorites. As regards a good deer rifle my favorite is a .303 caliber. I’ve seen great work done with Ross and Savage. Hoping I have given you the information you seek, I will be pleased to hear from you at any time, and if I can do anything for you, I will only be glad. Free service (except our assaying and oil services) but zve don ’t pay the postage to get it to you. Other Waterproofing Recipes 'y'WO of ’em; both simple enough, too: Question:—“I take your name from Adventure as being the person to whom to write with reference to camping, etc. I would appreciate it if you would send me a good recipe for waterproofing a tent.”— C. Sulling, New York City. Answer, by Dr. Hathome:—There are various methods for waterproofing tents. One of the best is a preparation consisting of sugar of lead and alum, equal parts, say about a pound of each to a gallon or more of tepid water. When dissolved put tent in soak in the solution, and when thoroughly saturated, hang up and dry. This will make a tent fairly water- proof, mildew-proof and to a certain extent fireproof. Another way is to dissolve paraffin in gasoline, out of doors, then paint the preparation on the can- vas. This will make a tent waterproof, but is not so easily applied, and a tent is more likely’to catch fire from sparks from a camp-fire.190 Adventure Old Songs That 745 Riverside Drive, New York. QNE of the most interesting contributions to this department since I took hold of it is a letter from a dear old lady seventy-six years of age who has sent in as fine a bit of swashbuckling sea doggerel of a time long gone by, as ever came my way. Eleven verses in as clean-cut handwriting as you fellows would want to see. Here’s the letter and the poem—they sure go together: Edgett’s Landing, Albert County, New Brunswick, Canada. Am sending you an old song which may be called a sea ditty—a doggerel affair in these days. It was composed about the time of happening (1791), as my father learned it in the year 1813, when he went to New York, a lad of seventeen. He used to sing it along with others when I was a little girl, until I learned it from hearing him. I am now an old wo- man of seventy-six and probably the only one in the world who knows this song. Wish I could send you the tune as well—a quaint old “come-all-ye” lyric.— Mss. George Essler. Off with your hats, every mother’s son of ye, to the oldest and liveliest member of the Camp-Fire! Does she belong? Well—I guess yes. Here’s the poem: The Bold Nor’westmen Come, all ye bold Nor’westmen who plow the rag- ing main—- Attend unto this tragedy while I relate the same; ’Tis of the Lady Wellington, at anchor where she lay Off Queen Charlotte’s Island northwest of Amerikay. It was in the year one thousand seven hundred and ninety-one Great numbers of wild savages on board of our ship did come, And for to buy their furs of them our captain did begin, But mark what followed after, before long time had been: % ’Twas aft upon our quarter deck two arms-chests there did stand. The keys had been left in them by our gunner’s careless hand. Those savages they seized them and of them made a prize. Oh, Lord! We had no other arms for to defend our lives. ’Twas aft upon our quarter deck our captain he did stand With twenty of those wild savages, their naked knives in hand; Twenty of those wild savages, their naked knives they drew: If he should flinch or budge an inch, ready to run him through. Our captain, then perceiving this, he unto them did say— “If you give back my keys again, great sums to you I’ll pay.”-.	1 Men Have Sung But the savages, not fearing him, they all drew forth their knives, Saying: “The ship, she’s ours now! And you shall lose your lives.” Our captain, then perceiving this—the ship no longer ours— He called unto his sailors bold, likewise his officers: “Go down into my cabin, boys, some firearms you’ll find there. See that they are well loaded; mind that they don’t miss fire.” Then down into his cabin we quickly did repair— But to our sad misfortune no firearms we found there Except a brace of pistols, two guns and one broad- sword— Then instantly we did agree: “Blow her up,” it was the word. Then to acquaint our captain we quickly did repair And instantly he did agree: “Blow her up into the air.” Our powder was got ready, our gun-rooms opened wide, Our souls committed to the Lord, our bodies to the tide. Those savages perceiving this, they in our cabins rushed— Our captain cried out, “Follow them,” and after them we pushed. Ninety of these wild savages we killed upon the floor; As many more we wounded, and the rest jumped overboard. Early on the next morning, just by the break of day, Our captain called all hands on deck, our anchor for to weigh. With our cannon and small arms we on their town did play, Till they brought back those keys again which they had stole away. Now come, all you bold Nor’westmen who plow the raging main— Come listen to my tragedy while I conclude the same; Beware of all wild savages, of them you’ll find no end, And they will strive to take your life when you have been their friend. Comrade Edward E. C. Gibbs of Huntington, Penna., would like to be put on the track of a song that used to be popular among the boatmen of the Great Lakes about forty years ago. “Bound Away in the Twilight” is its name, and one of the verses listens like this: We worked night and day till our fingers were sore, And we cursed Escanaba and damned iron ore. Russell Jacobs, a comrade Adventurer who works in a logging-camp out in Bryant, Wisconsin, writes me that he hears a lot of different songs only half- sung, such as a few words of a chorus, or a part of aAsk Adventure 191 verse here and there. It gets on his nerves to listen to these uncompleted ballads, and he asks me to find the missing portions. Russell, old scout, I’d feel more like going on a hunt for this stuff if you’d only put just one little two-cent stamp in your letter for a reply. It isn’t the two cents, Russell, me boy—it’s the fact that for every one man who thinks to enclose a stamped and addressed envelope in his letter to me, there are four or five who either forget it or are too dumlazy to do it; and just bechune us it makes me plumb weary. Hoffman tells me that I should just waste- basket all letters of this kind. Well—annyhow—here are the titles of the songs that Jacobs wants: “California Joe,” “The Wabash Cannon-Ball,” “Upon the Big Eau Claire,” “When the Work Is Done This Fall,” “Sing Me a Song of the Sweet Sunny South,” and “John Henry, the Steel Driving Man.” “California Joe” and “When the Work Is Done This Fall” will be found in Lomax’s anthology, “Cowboy Songs,” published by Macmillan & Co. My thanks to comrade Neil H. Wilson of North Tonawanda for the “Alphabetical Chantey” and “The Braw Glen Wharple Hieland Men”—two bully good bits of swashbuckling verse we hope to print in Adventure in the near future. Comrade J. M. B. (which is as near as he comes to his name in his letter) from somewhere over on Staten Island has also sent in a fine bit of deep- sea verse entitled “The Dreadnaught,” which will shortly appear. A few' .more like these, comrades Wilson and J. M. B., and we’ll have the makings of an Adventure anthology. Inquiries from several comrades for “Cowboy’s Lament” and “Utah Carroll,” “Barbara Allen” and several others that are new ones to me. “Cowboy’s Lament” was published in Adventure for March lQth. “Utah Carroll” will be found in “Cowboy Songs,” a compilation by Lomax, published by the Macmillan Co., New York. “Barbara Allen” is an old-timer and may be found in almost all collections of Scottish verse—both words and music.
